## Session 2: Negotiation on Mandate Framework (Sep 27, 2025)
Video: /video/k1w/k1w9g0n7gs

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I call to order the informal meeting of the Working Group for consultations on the matter of mandate creation. On behalf of Ambassador Shuolga, I warmly welcome you to these consultations. I'd like to start by recognizing the presence of Mr. Ayaka Suzuki, Director in the Executive Office of The Secretary General Mr. Christophe Monnier, Director of the Program Planning and Budget Division in the Department of Management, Strategy, Policy and compliance and Ms. Min Thu Pam, founder and CEO of Project Starling. Colleagues, as we said last week at the Briefing on Mandate creation, we are a key acutely aware of the importance of the task ahead of us. We can all agree that the UN is at a critical juncture and there is much to do to make the UN more effective, more efficient, more accountable and capable of responding to contemporary needs. Launched as part of the UN 80 initiative, our informal Ad hoc Working Group has a particular responsibility to build a set of principles and actions which can better support the creation, implementation and review of mandates. Our Working Group also has a role in building trust and mutual understanding which can support the broader goal of organizational strengthening as envisaged by the UN 80 initiative. As we have explained, this Working Group is currently engaged in a Discovery Phase where we are seeking to build our shared understanding of mandates, supported by empirical evidence, analyses and lessons learned. As Co Chairs, we want the Discovery Phase to be a safe space where Working Group members feel they can propose ideas, big and small, novel and familiar, for the way ahead. There will be ample time in the days and weeks ahead to assess the merit and viability of proposals, so we encourage Working Group members to keep an open mind about what might be possible, keeping in view the opportunity presented by this process for us to achieve the highest possible ambition. Today, Working Group members will first hear responses to questions posed at the Mandate creation briefing on 13 October. As you are aware, some answers have already been provided writing on 22nd October and should be available on the UNAT Initiative website. Further, we wish to note that in response to Working Group members questions on the Mandates Registry website, as co Chairs we have commissioned the Secretariat to provide a technical briefing on the Registry. This expert level technical briefing will take place virtually on the 28th of October at 10am and the link for that will be provided to the Working Group and the briefing will be recorded and posted on the website. Most importantly, as advised in our letter of 17 October, working group members will today have the opportunity to convey their views on mandate creation and we are very much looking forward to hearing your ideas and proposals. We also Invite you to please submit your interventions as well as any additional inputs to us in writing via our email address, which is unat mandates co chairsmail.com we intend to compile and publish on the Unity Initiative website Working Group Members contributions unless otherwise indicated by you. I'm now going to pass the floor to my Co Chair, Ambassador Schwal.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Good morning everybody. Now, as we turn to the questions posed to the briefers at the Mandate creation briefing on 13th October, we wish to respond to the question posed to us as Co Chairs about how lessons learned from the last mandate review and other processes have been reflected in our consideration. As Co Chairs, we have spoken with several individuals from Member States and the Secretariat who were involved in the last mandate review. We discovered three key learnings. Firstly, the previous exercise on review of existing individual mandates was a complex and time consuming process. Ultimately, the value of the technical exercise was undermined by the prevailing political realities. Secondly, it was apparent that the tools necessary to support a deeper understanding of the overall mandates for framework, such as a mandate registry, were unavailable, underdeveloped or fledgling at best. Thirdly, we learned about the importance of improving understanding between mandate creation and the budgetary process. We have attempted to build these learnings into the approach we are taking in this Working Group. Further, in response to another question, we can confirm that we have also begun engaging with Member States who have recently led relevant intergovernmental processes to see what lessons or ideas we can carry over into this Working Group. One example of this is the General assembly revitalization process. We are also engaging with the Chairs of the Main Committees to understand how the issue of mandate creation, implementation and review is being considered in their Committees and what this could mean for the Working Group's own deliberations. With that, it is now my honour to offer the floor to our briefers to provide additional answers to Questions posed at the 13 October briefing on Mandate Creation. First, I give the floor to Ms. Ayaka Suzuki, Director in the Executive Office of the Secretary General, to provide further answers to questions posed at the Briefing on Mandate Creation.

**EOSG** | Ayaka Suzuki, Director
Thank you so much, Co Chairs, for inviting the Secretariat to provide further information at this meeting. Excellency, distinguished Delegates, first, thank you so much for your strong interest and many questions after a briefing. We have tried our best to answer, provide answers in the slides that we have provided yesterday and hope to provide additional clarifications today. So, let me begin with the registry mandates.un.org There are quite many questions about the timeline, features, content development challenges and future direction. In short, mandates.un.org will keep improving if Member States find it valuable, but it would also benefit from your support and feedback. As announced just now, we will be doing a more technical briefing on this registry next Tuesday and I hope that as many of you would be able to participate so that we can introduce how to utilize it to support your work. And as mentioned by Ambassador Wallace, the briefing will be virtual and a recording will be made available available. So the timeline for completion of this registry, I mean it's an ongoing work. It already has some 4,000 mandate sources cited in the regular and peacekeeping budgets for 2026, and obviously we will be adding more as new budgets are issued and then they will be regularly updated. We will continue expanding features, categorization and functionality. The focus is on fully covering all mandate sources, especially those that are older, providing more details regarding mandates, such as operative paragraphs, entities named actions that are mandated, and then helping users see the big picture, for example, how mandates cluster across organs and topics. We already separate preambular and operative paragraphs and identify the actions, entities and deliverables in each resolution. We were able to do this otherwise very painstaking task by AI, which scans and classifies keywords across thousands of documents, and I will get into that shortly afterwards and how we use the AI. Our biggest challenge isn't so much the data, because that's all available, but in skills in coding, AI, data science and web development. To keep the registry robust and user friendly requires coding skills. And this is what UN 2.0 is about, modern digital capacity. So if you find the registry helpful, we will benefit from continued dialogue with Member States on how to sustain and improve it. The main constant will be skills, as I just mentioned, not cost. Most of the skills we need are found among young people, and that is why your support for UN rejuvenation is so critical. Finally, on scope, the mandates.un.org portal covers identify mandates coming from bodies serviced by the UN Secretariat. In other words, bodies such as the General Assembly, Security Council, ecosoc and so on. Specialized agencies are invited to develop their own registries for their mandating bodies, but using the shared data standards so that information can connect across. So let me now address a group of questions related to resourcing of registries and mandates more generally. So this was the second bigger group of questions and first on the registry development. The current version of mandates was built by a very small staff, small team in our office using skills we are promoting through the UN 2.0 initiative, I.e. with new capabilities in data design and digital delivery. Second, on the financial impact of mandates themselves. Each mandate, even a simple one, carries cost for creation, implementation and review at a minimum. That could mean staff time, translation, editing, or meetings. For such routine tasks, there are standard cost estimates that are transparently laid out in the memorandum, that is before the General Committee at the beginning of each session of the General Assembly. We refer to this document in our answers about $11,000 per day for meetings of up to two days and $25,000 for editing and translation, not the initial drafting of a report of around 8,500 words. Third, on what some of you call the cost of ambiguity. We don't really have a systematic data on the financial impact of vaguely vague language and resolutions, but we know that it matters. When the intent of a mandate is not clear, then we need extra consultations, coordination, which takes both time and resources. And that is why clarity on intent really matters. Finally, there are questions on how time intensive certain groups of mandates are in general act. As the number and complexity of mandates in a pillar grows, so do resource demands on Member states, more meetings, reports and negotiations, and on UN entities that must serve them. We can show at the registry briefing next week how to find large groups of mandates, if you like. The simple takeaway here is that resources are not only financial, there is also human and temporal the time, attention and clarity needed to make each mandate deliverable. The next large group of questions was about artificial intelligence and digital tools and how they're being used to support mandate work. First, on what AI does. It can augment research capacity. As I just explained, we use that to build up registry of mandates, for example, to help identify keywords, recurring phrases, overlaps and similarities across a large body of mandate text. It is an analytical support tool, not one to write or negotiate resolutions. That should of course remain a human process. Second, on data for mandates.un.org the system processes only publicly available documents, resolutions, decisions and budgets, all sourced from the UN Digital library. Third, on data security and quality. This site only uses open sources. No internal, confidential or personal data have been used. AI helps process large amounts of data quickly with built in checks and human reviews for quality. Some variation is normal and our team is improving the system over time. Finally, on future digital tools, we are continuing to build on your feedback over time. New digital tools, including AI capabilities, may allow for quicker comparison of text, of visual or of visual mapping of how mandates relate to each other. The goal is to make information easier to find and to understand. The simple takeaway is that AI can support analysis, not make final decisions. It can help us save time and improve visibility while we keep human judgment at the center of the process. Finally, let me speak to questions on several broader issues that were made from review clauses and flexibility in tasking to guidance for Member States and the timeline for next steps. First, on review clauses, most mandates still lack explicit review or end mechanisms. Over 80% of mandates cited in the current program budget do not specify how or when they should be reviewed. Changing that practice would make mandates easier to assess and adapt over time. We understand that the Co Chairs will hold another briefing on mandate review later this year. Second, on the Secretary General's flexibility, the report recommends allowing the Secretary General to assign tasks based on comparative advantage, meaning the entity best placed to deliver, rather than prescribing one entity in the resolution text. And in fact, that has been the norm over the years and it's only in recent years that it has become more prescriptive. So what that means is that referring only to the Secretary General, that the United nations or UN system would be the preferred or the easiest way to do assignment based on comparative advantage. Third, to better support Member States, the Secretary General proposed three main actions. Improved human support for delegates during the drafting, Enhanced Inter Secretariat cooperation to support visibility across intergovernmental bodies and digital tools. Fourth, on how to ensure new mandates are backed by resources or accompanied by a review of existing ones. Our suggestion here included using review clauses, consolidating agenda items, or proposing the discontinuation of outdated outputs, which is an authority that the Secretary General intends to make make purposeful use of. Finally, on the timeline for Secretary General General related actions, many of these actions are already in progress, including the continued improvements of mandates.un.org Fuller implementation is targeted for 2026 in line with the available capacity and resources. We will keep you updated both in a General assembly plenary and in this Working Group. The key message here is that we want to support your work on impactful mandate design, efficient delivery and accountable reviews, all grounded in evidence and dialogue.

**[Speaker Unknown]**
And with that I wish to conclude Many thanks to Co chairs,

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Ms. Suzuki and I now give the floor to Mr. Christoph M.E. monier, Director of the Program Planning and Budget Division in the Department of Management Strategy, Policy and Compliance, to provide further answers to questions posed at the briefing on mandate creation.

**DMSPC** | Christophe Monnier, Director
Thank you very much, Madam Chair. You know, adding to what my colleague just said, I'll. I'll take a few more of those questions that were posed to us. In particular, there was a few questions that related to what does the Secretariat how does Secretary determine additional resources are needed when mandates are assigned within existing resource mentioned and how is those situations treated? Just want to mention that in such situations, of course, we consult with all the relevant departments to know whether the implementation can be, you know, can be done within the existing resource, whether it can be absorbed, whether the, you know, synergies can be created with already planned activities and determining if reallocation of resources are, you know, are possible while maintaining the commitments and the deliverables that are supporting them and the delivery already. Just also want to mention that we understand and also from this dialogue that happened last week that, you know, often when there is such mention, it is also a way for the for the Member States to convey to the Secretariat that the expectation is that such a new mandate would not have a lot of additional activities or costly activities associated to it. It would not consume significant resources and therefore, you know, it would be implementing through leveraging the resources that are already available or have been planned for other activities and build synergies. There was a few questions and I'll answer on aspects of what I would call budgetary techniques that could be used to simplify or use anticipation for a more efficient budgetary process. I think that there was a mention, for example, of the concept of threshold. Whether using threshold to say, under a certain threshold, for example, there is no need to go and present pbis or above a certain threshold, there is the understanding that there wouldn't always be a PBI and there wouldn't be no absorption possible. So this is not something that we have in place, but it could be something that we can think about with criteria that makes sense so that we avoid ambiguity, a moment of the negotiation of the creation of the mandate. Also a technique that so far is only used in one area, which could be the inclusion of provision for future program budget implications at the time of preparation of the proposed program budget so that to streamline the mandate resourcing process in, you know, today we are including in the budget only things that have already been approved by Member States without anticipating possible approval. The only area where we do that is on renewal of certain mandates for human rights and we call that the consolidation. It's been in place now for a few years and it's proven to be quite a useful tool. It lowers the amount of PBIS that have to be presented in the fall. And certainly that is a tool that I think could be also an idea that could be considered on the aspect of the mandate creation process. There was, I think my colleague already mentioned, you know, how much the addition of tools, the registry of tools that help identifying what's already available not only within the Secretariat is being done, but across the UN system will be extremely useful because today we are not really in a capacity to provide that one is about the mandates. Also, global information of the funding of those mandates is not necessarily available for the UN system, but we should look into how those tools could be enriched with that information so that at the moment of mandate creation, you have access to this information, but that is not a function that is currently happening within the Secretariat and which is not really resourced neither. So that would be something to be looked into. There was also questions related to the possibility we have to understand budgetary implications or that Member States have to understand budgetary implication earlier in the drafting and negotiation process for new mandate, for example, at the stage of the zero draft, rather than waiting at the end of the process when you have more of a final resolution, and that we formally cost it. So, of course, you know, we could imagine a mechanism where throughout the negotiation there is a support of the Secretariat in terms of the costing of the drafts as they are. But again, that is one area where today that is not yet how it is being done and how it's being resourced, neither to have a dedicated team that could accompany Member States throughout the different revisions. That is an idea that is worth exploring, knowing that it would probably have a cost, but it might be worth it because then it would help creating better mandates, probably, or mandates that are calibrated to what the expectations of Member States are. So that was an idea that we also wanted to mention as possible to explore. Madam Chair, I think I will stop here, but of course available for more answers. Thank you.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you so much, Mr. Moliere. I now invite Ms. Mintuvan, co founder and Chief Executive of Project Starling, to provide further answers to questions posed at the briefing on mandate creation.

**Project Starling** | Min Thu Pham, Founder & CEO
Thank you so much, Madam Chair, and thank you all for being here and for inviting me back to brief you. It's a real privilege. I thought the engagement that we had last week was really energizing, and it was great to see so much thought from all of you in engagement in this really critical subject. I want to thank especially to those of you who asked such fantastic questions to those of us on the panel. And I'm grateful to be back here to help you think this through. As I'd mentioned, I was asked to lead the first UN mandate review 20 years ago when I worked in the office of Secretary General Kofi Annan. And it was an ambitious task and to do. So I had to form a team. And it was a team of about 20, maybe a bit more than that, that included UN staff, including retirees that we pulled back in, student interns to read through UN resolutions, manually type into a database to create the registry, and then people to also look at the, to do the analysis. And as part of that effort, I personally interviewed everyone I possibly could throughout the Secretariat, Every chief of staff, policy or planning director, head of office, anyone across the system in New York at least, who was willing, this was before zoom, who was willing to meet with me to understand how the UN really worked and how we could help make it work better. And this culminated in our own mandate registry that we created in 2005, 2006, and an intergovernmental review of all mandates older than five years. And it totaled over 9,000. That meant that some resolutions had more than one mandate. But I do want to just emphasize that part of the challenge back then was the mandate creation process itself. And maybe that is indicative of the challenge that you have here, because the World Summit outcome which gave the mandate to conduct a mandate review, I quote, and this is the World summit outcome in 2005. The mandate was, we resolved to strengthen and update the program of work of the United nations so that it, it responds to the contemporary requirements of Member States. To this end, the General assembly and other relevant organs will review all mandates older than five years, originating from resolutions of the General assembly and other organs which would be complementary to the existing periodic reviews of activities. So the original mandate for that 2006 mandate review was to review all mandates older than five years. So it was not because the Member States decided to take a different path. This was the mandate that they had given, that you all had given yourselves back then. And so what I learned from those discussions is really what I bring to this. I'm not an expert on management reform, at least not anymore. I haven't worked on it in over 10 years. Or am I versed in how the Secretariat currently manages the budgets, which you already have the Secretary of Staff here to do? But I did speak to many UN evaluation experts and many, many people, including heads of offices and chiefs of staff throughout the system. When we last met, a number of Member states asked me how this mandate implementation review can be successful where the previous one had fallen short. And I think the first one is that you are not bound by a mandate that you all had issued yourselves to review past mandates, but that you actually have the flexibility now and the opportunity to look forward, to try to figure out and really fix the mandate creation, delivering and review cycle. One of you had noted last time that we have the tools today that the team in 2005 and six were unable to offer. Chief among them, you have artificial intelligence that can ease the burdens of technical detail. You also have a much more advanced mandate resource registry that, as you've heard from Ms. Suzuki, that the Secretariat has created. As I mentioned, 20 years ago, we also had a mandate registry that was manually populated, and Member States found in those consultations that the mandates that we identified lacked and evaluative and budgetary information. And it sounds like that is still a challenge. How then can we make sure that today's mandate Registry provides you, the Member States, with the information that you need? What information does it currently provide? Right now, as you've heard, the mandate Registry allows you to search information on almost 4,000 active mandates that parts of the UN system are citing in their program budget requests. You can search by organ, body, implementing entity, title or subject matter. So, as an example, if you look at the issue of debt relief, for instance, because especially we're only four months after the fourth International Conference on Financing for Development, which I was involved in, debt relief was obviously a big issue in FFD this year if Member States were considering a new resolution on debt relief. If you look through the current mandate registry, a quick search shows you that there are 18 existing mandate source documents issued by two UN organs and cited 38 times by 15 UN entities. This is useful. Member States can use this information to inform future mandates and see what requests have already made, what you have already asked for in terms of debt relief, and then to shape what you might do in the future. However, does this information allow for a review of past or existing mandates? This is a question that a few of you asked last time. What information would you need to consider and decide on to either streamline or decide that the mandate is complete? If I were the one undertaking this exercise, I would want to know what actions were taken to implement the mandate that you issued before. I'd want to know what those actions achieved and whether it was what you had originally intended. I'd want to know what the impact was and where the effort fell short, and why, and what could be done to make it better next time. Some of this information may be hard to get, and in fact, we know that in general, impact is hard to measure, but we're still better off in trying to measure it. I would want to know this information, and lastly, I'd want to know how much it cost and Again, I'm not an expert on the UN's current budgeting and management, but in my conversations with Secretary of Staff and I think what you've just heard here is that that's not available and it doesn't appear to currently be available. And I recognize it's a huge task. So it's okay. I mean, you have a, you've done a gargantuan task in a very short period of time. But it's why I urge you to consider improving the UN's ability to evaluate its work and to provide that information back to you in a clear and coherent way so that you can make meaningful decisions on your mandates. The mandate review tools as they exist don't automatically make, I think a backwards looking mandate easier because you don't have the information to know what happened on your past mandates, the impact that it had and what then, whether it achieved the objectives and whether they can be improved. So then how can we make this process successful? What's needed currently, it would help to have improved evaluation of mandate implementation. This is something that I mentioned last time, what I've seen, even though I'm not, as I mentioned, an evaluation expert, but just talking to experts and really trying to understand this. And again, I did this many years ago, but right now it seems that the UN does not adequately finance evaluation, especially as compared to best practices. In our last meeting I suggested that perhaps 1% of the UN's budget was put towards evaluation. And again that number needs to be fact checked because the information, I base that on a cursory review, but I may not have access to the right sources. But other institutions and best practices says that we should budget about 3 to 10% of a program to adequate program evaluation. One way for Member States to address this is to carve out funding for mandates evaluation or to designate in a PBI a program budget implication a certain percentage for rigorous self and independent evaluation. The benchmarks and criteria for evaluation could also be identified in the mandate creation process. Perhaps Member States could request the Secretary to prepare and present criteria for evaluation of a mandated program and the impact as well as importantly the timeline for execution. I think you've also just heard from the Secretary that they would prefer to have that as well. And remember, and as I remember it, the budget process looks at inputs, outputs and outcomes. But I wonder, could that be applied to specific specific mandates and then done in a rigorous way so that you have the information you need? Institutionalizing improved evaluation, making that information available to Member States would be a solid step towards helping you all be better custodians of your mandates and make informed decisions on both future mandates and older ones. A final thought for me is that, you know, I've been using the term mandates, as we all have, essentially as a proxy for what the UN or the Secretariat works on. I think really that's what we mean. What does the UN do? Because mandates, at the end of the day, are political decisions. Mandates indicate you, the member states, your intentions. What you budget towards them, whether it's existing resources or not, also indicates your intentions. Someone has to turn the outcome of your political negotiations, these mandates, into programs and budgets. This is why so many mandates repute repeat language, resolution after resolution. What it basically means that I think it's an expression of the emphasis that you put on that topic and not necessarily a duplication of activities. We spent time reading through mandates 20 years ago, and we didn't reduce or eliminate many mandates because the mandates express important intentions. If we really want to review what the UN does to know if they are outdated or duplicative activities, we may wish to consider reviewing the UN's mandated programs themselves. And there I'd look to, first of all, reports. I think the Secretariat has identified that as a ripe area for your examination. These massive volumes of reports especially burden the smallest member state missions. I understand and appreciate the very good question that one distinguished representative last week raised about whether shorter mandates mean less staff time spent writing them. And as I did last time, I'll end with a quote here. Philosopher and mathematician Blaise Pascal said, quote, I would have written you a short letter, but I did a shorter letter, but I did not have the time. As many writers know, being clear and concise very often requires much more effort than writing something longer because it takes time to cut excess and to distill what you mean to say. If you all want clearer information, you could have better evaluation and much clearer and, I think, shorter reporting.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you so much. Thank you so much. And thank you to all of the briefers for your willingness to return to answer the questions that were posed to you by working group members, but also for the commitment that you have shown in preparing some of that material in writing, which, of course, as Ambassador Wallace mentioned, is available on the UNAT website. I now open the floor for working group members to share your views on mandate creation. We hope to hear from you ideas and proposals to make mandate creation more efficient, effective and impactful. Delegations are asked to keep their interventions to 5 minutes for groups and 3 minutes for individual delegations. If you are speaking for a Group, please identify yourself to the Secretariat. I now have the honour to give the floor to the Permanent Representative of Iraq.

**IRQ**, Representative
Distinguished Delegates. I have the honor to deliver this statement on behalf of the group of 77 and China, except the delegation of the Republic of Azerbaijan. Upon its request at the onset. The Group thanks the Co Chairs of the Informal Ad Hoc Group Working Group on the Review of the Mandate Implementation for sharing the tentative schedule of meetings for the next two months. Given the integrated nature of all three stages of of the mandate life cycle creation, implementation and review, the Group trusts that written inputs on these stages will be evaluated holistically in the entire life cycle to ensure a coherent and balanced approach. The Group also seeks further clarification regarding the request for input, as there is not yet a common understanding of the final outcome of the ad hoc Working Group and whether it will take the form of a resolution of recommendation, a statement or any type of outcome document. We would also appreciate clarity on the process and framework through which the suggested input will be considered. The Group reiterates the importance of accelerating the creation of a comprehensive registry of existing mandates. These registries should include detailed information on the cost of the mandate, the staff assigned to them and their nationality distribution. Such transparency will allow Member States to make better informed assessments and decisions. The Group reaffirmed that any review of mandate creation must remain firmly within the purview of the Member States. The Secretariat's operational discretion must be exercised strictly within the established intergovernmental framework. It is equally important that the process ensure full and equal participation of all Member States, including developing countries, taking into account capacity constraints and avoiding a hasty or selective approach. Clear and unambiguous mandates from intergovernmental bodies provide the most solid foundation for the Secretary's successful implementation. Mandate texts are political instruments agreed upon by Member States and this review process should not result in the erosion of the substantive content or negotiated language that embody the collective will of the Member States on the use of new technology, including artificial intelligence. The Group recognizes their potential to improve efficiency. However, such tools should be used to supplement the human dimension of intergovernmental work. The personal accountability of UN staff in supporting Member States remain essential. The use of AI tool without adequate safeguard, privacy and security guarantees and a comprehensive understanding could pose risk, including bias, monopolization, loss of no ones and and distortion of negotiated outcome. The Group reiterates that mandates must be supported by adequate and predictable resources. More clarity is required to understand the downstream implications of having the establishment of new mandate being conditions on the availability of resources, on granting The Secretary General discretionary flexibility to reassign or merger tasks. Such design features should not undermine the intergovernmental nature of United nations or weaken its operational effectiveness and credibility. The Group considered that we need to find a common understanding between Member States and the Secretariat on the scope and implications of the phrasing within existing resources. To conclude, the Group reiterates its commitment to engage constructively in this process. We emphasize that discussions on mandate creation must remain inclusive, transparent and efficient, ensuring full participation of all Member States. I thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair

**EU**, Representative
Distinguished Co. Chairs, Distinguished colleagues, I have the honor to deliver this statement on behalf of the European Union and its member states. The candidate countries, Turkey and North Macedonia, Montenegro, Ukraine, the Republic of Moldova, Bosnia and Herzegovina and Georgia, as well as Armenia, Andorra, Monaco and San Marino, align themselves with the Statement on Mandate Creation, a topic of today. We must acknowledge that effective mandates demand effective design and have full visibility across the entire mandate system, which includes what mandates already exist on the same or related topics, who and how is implementing them and with what results. Therefore, we welcome measures designed to first amplify the overall impact and sustainability of new mandates and eliminate implementation ambiguities. This should be done through the adoption of concise action oriented and outcome driven language, the implementation and impact of mandates should be measurable and system wide evaluation will be critical. Second, introduce sunset clauses with a robust mechanism for periodic review to minimize perpetual extensions and ensure mandates evolve or conclude in alignment with their objectives, as well as review clauses to ensure that regular assessment and evaluation of the relevance and efficiency of the mandates can take place. Third, accompany the creation of new mandates in the UN system with clear resource estimates, timely and transparent program budget implications from the very start. Fourth, strengthen a culture of efficiency and responsibility on mandate resourcing by exploring all options on resource allocation and then make sure that new mandates leading to new activities can be funded by the required corresponding resources and with appropriate oversight as a prerequisite for new mandate creation. Fifth, further implement comprehensive digital mandate registries, including by an open, transparent and trustworthy use of artificial intelligence to conduct a preliminary review and gain full awareness of active mandates to maximize mandate coordination, clear roles, responsibilities and impact, and eliminate duplication in the creation of new ones. We also encourage the Secretariat to identify and put forward rationalizations in terms of mandate execution among Secretariat entities every time mandates allow it Colleagues the European Union and its Member States strongly support the Secretary General's efforts to enhance mandate visibility and coordination across mandating bodies. To ensure effective implementation, the Secretary General must be able to act with appropriate flexibility. Therefore, we welcome greater secretarial support to Member States during mandate creation without establishing new bodies or structures, and encourage the Secretary General to assign tasks according to comparative advantages and resource availability across the UN system to ensure the implementation of agreed mandates. Colleagues it is US Member States that are responsible for mandate creation and we must guide their design and implementation with transparency, accountability, added value, due diligence, fiscal responsibility and a strong sense of common purpose. It is therefore important that the work under this work stream also links up with the discussions about structural reforms under Work Stream 3. We also believe that the above criteria and lessons learned from earlier processes should also inform in due course the review of existing mandates. In conclusion, we appreciate the opportunity to engage in this important discussion and welcome the Secretary General's proposals and vision under the UNAID Reform Initiative. As members of the Organization, we encourage further ambitious proposals from the Secretariat and remain committed to sustaining the momentum and ensuring continued ownership of this process. Chairs we will also submit a more detailed written version of our input. Thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the representative of the European Union and I now give the floor to the Representative of the Solomon Islands, to be followed by Belize

**SLB**, Representative
Co Chairs, Excellencies and Distinguished Colleagues.

**SLB**, Representative
I have the honor to deliver this statement on behalf of the Pacific Islands Forum members based in New York, we welcome the opportunity that the UN 80 reform initiative offers to build a United nations that is more effective, efficient, accountable and capable of responding to contemporary needs. We as Member States must seize this opportunity. We want to thank the Co Chairs for an informative briefing on mandate creation on 13 October. PIF members are optimistic about the potential benefits to be achieved by improving how mandates are created at the structural level and see three key areas for improvement. First, we want to reduce duplication and inefficiencies in mandate creation. We note that most PIF members find the volume of the UN's work program to be highly demanding, especially given the size and capacities of many PIF missions. Small countries are the ones that suffer most from from a lack of discipline in the system as it takes away our attention from the most pressing issues to us, such as the 2030 Development Agenda and focusing on special needs of SIDS like sea level rise and fighting climate change. Negotiating on the same things in multiple forums is a waste of precious resources. Second, it's in everyone's interest to have a shorter, more focused resolutions with clearer and targeted mandates. To achieve this, we recognize that we need to give the Secretariat more flexibility on how they deliver on mandates we have created, whilst being clear that it's for Member States to set out what we want to achieve. We encourage the Secretariat to operate in a way that's more mindful of the limitations of small missions, for example by producing shorter and more focused reports. Third, everyone but particularly small states will benefit from some established tools for lead sponsors of resolutions that enable us to draft more efficient and effective mandates. We welcome the development and expansion of a comprehensive mandate registry which will help us avoid duplication. But we also need tools to support mandate design, particularly when it comes to things like resourcing, costing and evaluation, where the words used in the mandate really matter. Finally, we want to highlight the importance of the UN development system for the Pacific island countries, along with coordination with existing regional architecture and the need for review that supports building a system that is fit for purpose for tackling our unique and urgent challenges. We look forward to engaging collaboratively with Member States throughout this process. I thank you Co Chairs,

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the representative of the Solomon Islands and I now give the floor to the representative of Belize, to be followed by Australia.

**BLZ**, Representative
I have the honor to deliver the statement on behalf of the 14 members of the Caribbean Community CARICOM and align our remarks with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of the group of 77 and China. We thank the co facilitators and briefers for the information session on mandate creation, which provide valuable insight into the first stage of mandate cycle. We also wish to thank the co facilitators for the helpful webpage and calendar of forthcoming sessions. Key takeaways from our recent exchange on mandate creation include that the entire mandate life cycle creation, implementation and review should be considered as one continuum. The tracking implementation is essential yet remains uneven and unsystematic and that mandate review should be standardized and evidence based, yet it is rarely conducted in practice. Two further observations are instructive as we plan the future work of this group. First, success should be measured should not be measured by the number of mandates discontinued, but by whether the system delivers mandates more effectively and with greater impact and second, we should take a forward looking approach that strengthens how mandates are created, ensuring that decisions are informed by full visibility across the UN system and that future reviews are grounded in credible evaluative data. Taking these points into account, CARICOM commends the work done on development of the Mandate Registry. We consider that an immediate and practical step would be to enhance the use and functionality of the UN Regulation Mandate Source Registry. The Registry should evolve into a genuine visibility and learning tool, one that allows Member States to see existing mandates across the system, understand overlaps and identify the opportunities for synergy. In that context, we support embedded a structured feedback mechanism to strengthen its utility to delegations. The G77's proposal on the categories of data to be maintained are particularly valuable. CARICOM notes that the Secretary General's report and Ms. Pham's briefing emphasize the importance of evidence informed policymaking. We agree and would support the initiatives that strengthen evaluation capacity across the UN system, including modest dedicated budget lines for independent and internal evaluations at the point of mandate creation. While our intelligence can assist with consolidating publicly available information and enhancing the Registry, CARICOM cautions against delegating or automating any form of critical policy analysis or assessment. Such responsibilities must remain under the authority of Member States and, where relevant, the Secretariat. With full respect for intergovernmental oversight and processes regarding Secretariat's role, we recognize that it already provides technical support to Member States as needed. We welcome the Secretary General's proposals to improve Secretariat assistance during mandate creation, especially to provide complementary information on related programmatic activities and resources, but underscore that this function must remain strictly technical and non prescriptive, upholding the intergovernmental character of of decision making. Mandate creation should occur based on a number of principles that we must come to agree on these could include demonstrable need, broad support, non duplication, transparency, inclusiveness and a genuine appreciation of the means for their implementation. CARICOM reiterates that mandates must be accompanied by adequate unpredictable resources. This is not only a matter of principle but of good governance. Ensuring that resources match expectations promotes accountability and transparency through a budgetary process and safeguards the credibility of the organization's work. In closing, CARICOM reaffirms its appreciation to the co facilitators for their leadership and reiterates our commitment to engage constructively in this process to ensure that the UN continues to serve people and planet efficient effectively and equitably.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank you, I thank the Representative of Belize and I now give the floor to the Permanent Representative of Australia, to be followed by Kuwait.

**AUS**, Representative
Thank you Chairs for creating the opportunity for an inclusive and collaborative consultation on mandate creation and the informative briefing organised last week.

**AUS**, Representative
I speak for Canada, New Zealand and my own delegation, Australia. As Member States, we are committed to the United Nations Charter and upholding the fundamental work of the United nations system. We welcome this opportunity to reflect on how Member States create mandates and the impact this has on the effectiveness of critical work of the United Nations. CANS highlights the following points to be considered as we focus on principles and actions to guide mandate creation. We need shorter, more focused resolutions with clearer and more targeted mandates. This will both increase the likelihood that mandates will be actively and effectively implemented and reduce current workloads. While we'll need to be clear in what we're asking, we see merit in providing the Secretariat with some flexibility on exactly how they deliver on these mandates to ensure they can do so in the most efficient and effective way possible. We need more support from the Secretariat to empower Member States to make more informed decisions when drafting resolutions to ensure that mandates avoid duplication and effectively complement relevant work taking place across the United nations system. We welcome tools such as the comprehensive online Mandate Registry being developed by the Secretariat that can support this, including assisting the lead sponsors of resolutions to draft better mandates. There are other tools that could be provided to support mandate design, such as those which institute more effective and efficient drafting. We need mandates with built in evaluation and review clauses and where appropriate, sunset clauses. And we need to reduce the number of resolutions introduced year after year with substantially the same content. We all have an interest in not spending precious time and resources on resolutions that have stopped having any real meaning or relevance. We must exercise discipline and restraint in making proposals that have resource implications for the United nations core budget, particularly in the current financial context if new resources are required to implement a new proposal, appropriate offsets should also be identified. All resolutions should include a dedicated section clearly addressing these budget issues. Key to this effort is the need better to link mandate decisions with projected costs and to enhance efficiencies in implementation. Member States must demonstrate maximum restraint in proposing new mandates and a sustained commitment to ensuring the limited resources are directed where they're most needed. We should also ensure synergies with existing processes related to mandates and take the opportunity to implement and advance work conducted under the General assembly revitalization process Sometimes overlaps between mandates can be helpful in supporting strong implementation, but often we experience wasteful duplication. We need to eliminate unnecessary duplication of similar mandates within and across various forums. Finally, while it's appropriate that this ad hoc Working Group focus on the principles guiding mandate creation, implementation and review, we also need to reach agreement on the actions required to apply these principles in practice. CAANS proposes the following concrete actions for Member States. First, include time bound provisions or mandate review clauses in new resolutions as appropriate appropriate. Second, take steps to reduce unnecessary PBIS or establishment of new costly mandates without identifying appropriate offsets. Third, ensure new mandates are linked to the core pillars of the United nations and advance tangible outcomes. Fourth, set and adhere to page limits for new resolutions and ensure these are targeted for maximum effect. Fifth, avoid duplication of mandates within and across forums six, promote multiannual mandates as the default for recurring resolutions and which do not substantively change from year to year and finally, review mandates that have not been active within the past 10 years. These actions could be reflected in a new template or guidance for the drafting resolutions. As we've stated previously, the primary responsibility for achieving a UN system capable of delivering on the outcomes our people expect and deserve lies with us, the Member States. We look forward to engaging further with Member States through the Working Group and working towards an effective reform process that results in a resilient and sustainable UN system able to deliver effectively on all three foundational pillars of its work and into the future. Thank you.

While being considerate of the schedules, the platforms and other processes associated with the UN80 initiative, the GCC reaffirms that this Initiative is a timely and genuine opportunity to achieve a qualitative transformation in the method of work of the United nations through a careful assessment along with supervision of Member States and a high degree of transparency. It is equally important to note that the path forward should not be an exercise in just downsizing, but rather a strategy for achieving the attendant goals of the Initiative, namely strengthening multilateralism, ensuring the full and effective participation of all Member States and doing so in adherence to the principles and three pillars in the Charter Excellencies. Any process undertaken to this end must include an intergovernmental approach. 2. It is essential for the Member States to have access to a comprehensive registry of existing mandates which includes detailed and essential information which would include, but not restricted to, costs of implementing the mandate, the number of staff assigned to service the functioning of the mandate and the distribution of nationalities for said mandate. The importance of this point should not be overshadowed. These details are essential for ensuring that the process achieves its intended goal of increasing efficiencies and streamlining processes while also maintaining a fair representation of nationalities. Above all, mandates at their point of creation and or while under the process of review, must ensure that the language of the relevant instruments are drafted in clear, unambiguous and unequivocal language. This is critical otherwise the system risks misinterpreting the will of the membership. It's not always clear to us what a mandate looks like in practice, what wording constitutes actually a mandate and what does not, what differentiates resolutions that create mandates from those that do not, and why it matters for effective governance and resource use, and also what happens after mandates are created, how they're implemented, followed up, and what the intended outcomes are. **IAHWG**, Co-Chair
Thank you so much. I thank the distinguished representative of Liechtenstein. And I'll give the floor to the distinguished representative of the United States, to be followed by El Salvador chairs, colleagues.

**USA**, Representative
The United nations can be an indispensable part of solving global challenges. But too often its efforts are dispersed across overlapping initiatives, redundant structures and endless reporting. If we are serious about reform the un, we must be willing to confront duplication head on. A system that does too much of the same thing in too many places cannot be truly effective anywhere. The mandate review now underway is our best chance in decades to bring clarity and discipline to how this organization works. With accurate information from the Secretariat about where functions, meetings and reports overlap, member states can make informed choices that simplify the machinery of the UN and strengthen its results. Done well, this review can reduce fragmentation, focus attention on priorities, and ultimately make the UN's work more coherent and impactful. We welcome the Secretary General's proposals as an important start, and we encourage him to go further. The UN 80 process should leave behind a leaner organization that directs resources towards outcomes, not bureaucracy. The next Secretary General's vision for reform will be an essential test of leadership, one that demonstrates a commitment to efficiency, accountability and measurable impact. A more integrated, streamlined approach, guided by results on the ground rather than the number of entities engaged, would deliver stronger support to countries and to their people. The measure of success must be progress achieved, not the proliferation of institutions, hours of meetings or pages of reports dedicated to a given issue. This is true of the development system, of the Human Rights pillar, and of the peace and security architecture. Mandates, too, must be seen through the lens of purpose. They are created to solve problems, not to live forever. When a mandate has achieved its goal, it should conclude that is not failure, it is success. And for every new mandate decided, at least 10 should be eliminated. To realize this vision, the Secretariat and Member States must work together with ambition and resolve. Reform is not an end in itself. It is a means to make the United nations credible, capable and trusted. Once again, thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the distinguished representative of the United States. I give the floor now to the distinguished representative of El Salvador, to be followed by St. Vincent and the Grenadines.

**SLV**, Representative
Thank you. Co Chairs. My delegation aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of the G77 and in national capacity. We would like to highlight the following points. In a context where UNAID initiative has generated uncertainty, this explanation exploratory phase must serve to lay solid foundations. It is essential to define without delay a clear roadmap, concrete expected outcomes and a shared set of principles to guide the work of the group. Whatever the outcome of this process, it must be ambitious and encompass the entire United nations systems. The review of mandates cannot be limited to the GA alone. It must promote a holistic vision of institutional efficiency. Genuine process will not depend on new procedures, but on a change of mindset among Member States. We must exercise responsibility and political discipline when presenting new initiatives, always asking ourselves what impact they will have on the organization and above all, on the people we serve. There are also immediate actions that can already be taken. The role of the PGA and the chairs of the main Committees is crucial in raising awareness of the growing burden of mandates and meetings. This challenge will not be resolved through procedural reform alone, but through coordination, coherence and obviously political will. In this regard, we consider it urgent that the work of the main committees during the 80s session include concrete measures to avoid repeating the pattern of generating countless new mandates. And as has already been noted in the report of dsg, each delegation must be aware of the budgetary impacts of its proposals and act with predictability. In this spirit, El Salvador is open to exploring the creation of a review or pre screening mechanism to anticipate the budgetary implications of draft resolutions, thereby ensuring greater transparency and coherence between substantive and also financial work. The main committees have a decisive role to play in this articulation. While the Fifth Committee prepares to consider the revised estimates for the next budget. The other committees must ensure that the content of the resolutions during the session does not generate more pbis, thereby contributing to a more disciplined and coherent management of the organization's resources. We welcome the launch of the platform mandates un.org which can become a valuable tool for transparency and efficiency provided that it remains up to date, accessible and truly user friendly, helping us to identify duplications and strengthen its coherence across our work. We also recognize the central role of the Secretariat in this context, but we urge them to refrain it from promoting or expanding mandates on its own initiative and to focus in its effort on being faithful and transparent implementation of the decisions adopted by U.S. member states. States finally, El reaffirms its conviction that the UNAID initiative must become a turning point. Dear Co Chairs, this anniversary is not merely a commemorative milestone, but a historic opportunity to demonstrate that the UN does not fear transformation, but that it has the courage to pursue it with ambition, vision and unity. Now these requests mandates are often well intentioned, but they have often also become micro mandates. They are narrow, they give fragmented instructions that cumulatively this erodes coherence, it erodes efficiency and it erodes strategic alignment across the UN system. So overall I do believe we need a culture change in order to strengthen coherence in mandate creation and implementation. The UN system should transform itself or transition from a request based responsive model to a more focused on operational viability in which the organization acts on the basis of member State agree strategic direction rather than fragmented smaller requests. So, but to be practical, we have very good proposed solutions in the Secretary General's report on mandate design. So we fully endorse that. We have to confirm that there is no duplication in how, that no one else is doing what we're proposing to be done. There needs to be a clear time frame. There needs to be proof of financial sustainability and impact assessment and always also an inbuilt evaluation or review mechanism from the very creation of a mandate. So I mean to be discussed in detail, but I think we need some sort of checklist that all those that propose mandates need to go through and show to the rest of us that this is thought through to be within the Charter, to be. To have thought about that it's not duplicated by anyone else. And then thirdly, perhaps we should refrain from mandating new tasks which are not backed by corresponding resources or an explicit reduction in other mandates. So on a trial basis, could we agree that we don't request any new reports from the Secretary General for one year, only modify the scope of existing reports? How many times was the. The report downloaded from the UN website? We could do all things like this before we, before we request new mandates or new things for the Secretariat to do. That approach will help us reduce duplication, ensure effective implementation of mandates, free up resources for priority areas, and ideally reduce the burden on Member States. Expanding and improving the Registry of existing mandates will also be important to enable a process of review and streamlining, and we welcome the update from the Secretariat on this issue. Drawing on the Registry identifies gaps to determine additional mandated activity that might add value and highlights potential budgetary implications and possible efficiencies. Collectively, we should consider enhanced use of modern technology to drive forward efficiency, including to identify duplication and reduce the length of mandate text. This will require oversight, but early investment here will pay off for us all. We are open to further ideas, including the possibility of word limits, while noting the need to safeguard normative standards. Finally, we should acknowledge that many mandates are driven by political considerations. While the Secretariat can help promote governance coherence across the UN system, it is incumbent on us all as Member States to design mandates that prioritize utility, efficiency and impact.

**JPN**, Representative
Madam and Mr. Co Chairs, thank you very much for giving me the floor. As this is the first time for this delegation to take the floor, I would like to express express our sincere appreciation to you both Co Chairs for taking up this important role. I would like to assure you of this delegation's full support and cooperation. Co Chairs. In the un, we have seen an increased number of mandates over the years, often created without sufficient consideration for overlap with existing mandates or their financial implications. This unchecked expansion has contributed significant duplications and budget growth which are ultimately unsustainable. There are two ways to address this. First, when establishing new mandates, we must ensure there is no duplication with existing ones. Second, we should consider budgetary implications, including setting budgetary ceiling to introduce financial discipline. In this regard, Japan would like to propose three key actions. First, it is essential that Member States be able to systematically verify whether any newly proposed mandate overlaps with existing mandates. We welcome the SCG's intention to set up registry designed in the manner that allows such verification to be conducted with a simple one click process. Second, when the resolution includes a new mandate which may incur budgetary implications, the proposing Member States should consult with the same secretariat and provide clear written information on the budgetary implications to all Members. Third, as proposed in the SDG's report, all resolutions creating new mandates should include expiry clauses requiring the mandate to be reviewed and renewed after a certain period in order to prevent unchecked and unlimited mandate expansion. The same principle should also apply to existing mandates which we will discuss further when we take up the mandate review phase. We believe that these steps will strengthen the UN's ability to deliver its mandate more effectively and sustainably. Thank you.

**IND**, Representative

Second, we recognize the importance of using AI tools with necessary safeguards to identify duplicative mandates and duplicative implementing architecture across mandating bodies. In this regard, information on the existing AI tools available with the circuit will be helpful. Additional features such as evaluating mandates may by their impact and effectiveness could be added later to assist Member States in designing more effective, concise and non duplicative mandates. Third, without prejudice to any mandate, it is essential that all mandates include sunset clauses for effective implementation, monitoring, transition and eventual exit. It will also facilitate plugging any systemic automaticity in the budget preparation process. Fourth, a Christmas tree approach to mandate creation dilutes both impact and accountability framework. Often it increases the length of mandate text. Additionally, it demands greater coordination across implementing entities, thus necessitating more resources. We consider a plain vanilla approach should guide mandate creation. Such approach could shorten length of the text, ensure effective implementation and produce greater impact. Fifth, since all three stages of mandate life cycle are interconnected, Member States should be allowed to submit written inputs on three stages after all briefings are completed. Such flexibility can yield holistic and practical insight to guide our work throughout the entire mandate life cycle and deliver ambitious outcomes. In conclusion, Mr. Co Chair, India reaffirms its commitment to remain constructively engaged in this process. I thank you.

**MDA**, Representative
Distinguished Co Chairs My delegation aligns itself to the European Union statement and wishes to add the following remarks in international capacity. Distinguished Colleagues Mandate creation lies in the head heart of the organization's effectiveness and credibility. At the same time, the growing number of complexity of mandates, as highlighted in the Secretary General's report, have created challenges of fragmentation, overlap and limited coordination which risk undermining this very effectiveness. From our perspective, this review process is an opportunity to reaffirm several guiding principles for mandate creation. First, new mandates should be firmly anchored in the Charter and aligned with the Organization's strategic objectives, responding to current realities rather than institutions inertia. Second, the mandate should build upon existing efforts, avoiding duplication and maximizing synergies across entities and pillars of United nations system. Third, fraud, mandate creation, negotiation and implementation, transparency and accountability must be ensured through clear benchmarks, timelines and reporting mechanisms. Fourth, the review of existing mandates should be institutionalized as a constructive exercise, not for reducing ambition but for ensuring effectiveness, coherence and measurable results. In reviewing the process of mandate creation, we wish to highlight three critical three key challenges that, in our view, continue to affect the organization's overall performance. First, mandates are generated across multiple UN bodies the General assembly, ecosoc, Human Rights Council, and various specialized agencies, often without sufficient coordination. This fragmentation leads to duplication, thematic overlap and inconsistent reporting, ultimately with awakening impact and straining the Secretariat's limited capacity. Second, for many smaller delegations, including my own, the process is constrained by limited visibility and transparency. Without a united and accessible registry of active mandates, it becomes difficult to track commitments and engage meaningfully in new initiatives. We therefore support the accelerated development of an integrated digital mandate registry such as mandates.unorg and stronger secretariat support to ensure equal participation of all Member States throughout the mandate cycle. Third, there remains a persistent gap between mandate creation and resource allocation. Too often mandates are approved within existing resources, leading to underfunding and inconsistent implementation. This disconnect undermines for both the credibility of decisions and the impact of UN work on the ground. Each new mandate should therefore be accompanied by transparent costing and clear program budget implications, ensuring the political ambition is matched by realistic and sustainable resources. Looking ahead, we support continued dialogue on practical measures to Strengthen the review of mandate creation process, including greater coherence among mandating bodies, enhanced coordination between the Secretariat and Member States and the use of digital tools to improve transparency and accessibility. Ultimately, effective mandate creation is not merely of a procedural exercise. It is a matter of credibility, efficiency and trust. By improving how we formulate, design and align mandates, we can ensure that every decision adopted in the organization truly responds to the evolving priorities and need of the people who we serve.

We believe that a clearer understanding of potential overlaps between existing and potentially new mandates will have a refraining effect and could lead to to a future reduction of mandates. Second, in the end, we, the Member States decide how mandates look like it's on us to be more stringent, for example Avoid unnecessary cross references. We therefore propose that Member States agree to a voluntary code of conduct with the aim of limit the length of mandates. For instance, a ceiling of 1000 words per mandate could be considered. **CRI**, Representative

Translating principles into concrete outcomes by examining how mandates are conceived, coordinated and implemented, we're also refining the way multilateralism works in practice. In this sense, Costa Rica has three observations. We considered that effective mandates are those that move from general aspirations to specific measurable outcomes. Informal reflections from thematic areas, including disarmament, showed that mandates created with clear objectives, well structured negotiation processes and, when possible, strong capacity building components have greater impact. A deliberate design encompassing a realistic scope and actionable targets ensures that political will is translated into meaningful results. Second, leadership and purpose are enablers of effective mandates. A successful mandate depends on leadership that can guide diverse perspectives towards a shared purpose. Skilled chairs and facilitators help shape clarity and balance in negotiations, ensuring that the resulting mandates reflect both ambition and feasibility. Third, realism in resource logistics and coordination. The phrase within existing resources or subject to the availability of resources no longer reflects the complexity of today's institutional environment. Early engagement with the Secretariat and timely access to operational and budgetary information are essential in all negotiations. System wide visibility and active coordination can prevent duplication, optimize resources and strengthen delivery. To conclude, mandates start with US Member States. They express our priorities and our commitment to fulfill the purposes and principles of the Charter. How to get there? We could consider taking thematic groups of of resolutions in each Main Committee and rotating them over several years.
