**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank you, I thank the Permanent Representative of Iceland and I now give the floor to the representative of China, to be followed by the Philippines. Thank you, Co Chairs China aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of the G77 and China at present, the three work streams of the UN80 initiative are becoming interconnected. From the overall perspective of UN80 initiative and in light of the topic today on mandate creation, China believes that it is essential to properly handle four key relationships. First, the relationship between streamlining and delivery. The goal of the Unity Initiative is not to make the UN smaller, but to make it leaner, more agile, more resilient and better able to provide high quality, efficient and direct support and services to Member States while eliminating redundancy and overlap.

**CHN**, Representative
The UN should also increase the investment in key areas based on the needs of Member States, particularly developing countries, and promote balanced progress across its three pillars. Similarly, the purpose of the mandate reform is not to reduce mandates. We should not bluntly cut mandates, tax reports or meetings simply for the sake of streamlining. What we should focus on is the results that such reform would deliver. We must conduct in depth analysis and make targeted efforts so as to maximize resources, effectiveness in effectiveness and expand the US Overall impact. Second, the relationship between Member States and the Secretariat Mandate creation is both the prerogative and responsibility of the Member States. Mandatory reform requires Member States to shoulder their primary responsibility and exercise due diligence from the outset. At the same time, the Secretariat should strengthen its support. To do that, Secretary Staff should be well versed in their respective areas and related fields and provide comprehensive assistance to Member States. It is also important for various departments and entities within the UN system to break down barriers and enhance coordination and strengthen their communication with Member States, including during the early stages of the work. While artificial intelligence can serve as a supplementary role, the Secretariat must ensure robust security safeguards, verification and oversight. The development of online mandatory registry should be pursued with due consideration of cost effectiveness. Third, the relationship between mandate creation and mandate implementation. Mandates can only have impact when effectively carried out and their implementation is directly affected by their creation. Like fasting the first button on a shirt, Member States must ensure that things are properly done from the very start of mandate creation. When creating mandates, it is imperative to focus on core issues so that the implementation efforts can be well targeted and resource not diluted during mandate creation. Providing previous implementation and available financial resources can help Member States better get the general picture and make informed decisions and thus create favorable conditions for for effective implementation of mandates. Fourth, and lastly, the relationship between reform and continuity. While the Unity Initiative should address long standing institutional challenges by fighting formalism and bureaucracy and practicing frugality, it is also crucial to maintain the stability and continuity of the UN's work and preserve the core value of UN, including multilingualism, so as to instill renewed confidence and momentum in multilateralism through concrete actions and results. Co Chairs China stands ready to work with all Member States and the Secretariat to continue our constructive role in improving the work of the UN and in advancing global governance towards a more just and equitable direction. Thank you Kyle Chair

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the Representative China and I now give the floor to the Representative of the Philippines, to be followed by Maldives.

**PHL**, Representative
Thank you Co Chairs the Philippines aligns itself with the statement of the G77 in China. We appreciate the briefing held on the 13th of October. We acknowledge the Secretary's role in increasing the visibility of the mandates landscape, including by assisting Member States in identifying potential overlaps and duplications and by developing the online Mandates Registry. Despite the existence of tools, improving the effectiveness and efficiency of mandates will ultimately depend on Member States who have the ownership of and prerogative over them. In this regard, the Philippines is pleased to share its recent experience on streamlining of mandates. At the 16 Human Rights Council session, a groundbreaking resolution was adopted to merge the mandates of the Special Rapporteur on Contemporary Forms of Slavery, including its causes and consequences, and the Special Rapporteur on Trafficking in Persons, especially Women and Girls, into one new Special Rapporteur on Contemporary Forms of Slavery and Trafficking in Persons. The Philippines, part of the Core Group on the Mandate on Trafficking in Persons, worked together with the Core Group on the Mandate on Contemporary Forms of slavery to unify the two mandates towards a more agile and integrated response to challenges that are distinct yet strongly linked to each other. The HRC resolution is considered as a historic first and can be viewed as an example of how with strong strong political will and commitment and shared commitment to improving efficiency and impact, Member States can come together to streamline long established mandates. Nevertheless, the Philippines underscores that mandate creation should be guided by the need to address specific challenges improving efficiency and delivering real impact, and should not be driven by cost cutting concerns alone. Any potential merger of mandates should only be undertaken after a thorough analysis of its implications for the peoples and the communities that we seek to serve. Finally, for the work of the informal ad hoc Working Group, the Philippines likewise recommends working closely with other UN organs and processes in Geneva, Vienna, Nairobi and other duty stations for possible exchange of best practices and lessons learned on strengthening officials efficiencies and impact of mandates. Thank you Co Chairs.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the representative of the Philippines and I now give the floor to the representative of Maldives to be followed by Mexico. Maldives, the floor is yours.

**MDV**, Representative
Thank you Coaches. The Maldives alliance with the statement delivered by the group of 77 and China the objective of this exercise should not be to simply reduce the number of mandates. Rather, it must be to make the UN more efficient and adept at handling mandates. We must focus on eliminating duplications, harmonizing mandates and improving the way they are implemented. To advance this goal, the Moldist proposes three practical steps to make mandate creation and follow up more effective. First, equip Member States with clearer information before new mandates are approved. Decisions on new mandates are often made without a full understanding of existing ones that may already address similar issues. The Maldives therefore proposes that the General assembly establish a light review process to assess overlaps and complementarities before adoption. This could draw on existing procedures such as the PBI review and make full use of the mandate's website. Better information will help Member States to place each other and each mandates where it can deliver the greatest value. Second, strengthen how we assess implementation. The Secretary General's reports to the General assembly usually describe actions taken but seldom evaluate their effectiveness. The moralist therefore proposes updating reporting methodologies to emphasize outcomes and outputs, giving Member States a clearer view of what has been achieved. The UN's field offices can support this by guaranteeing data and supplying information and reducing reporting burdens on governments, particularly that of the small island developing states. Third, introduce mechanism for regular review and adjustment. Mandates should evolve as circumstances change. An alternative process within the existing Secretariat structure could provide a periodic feedback from inception to completion, ensuring that each mandate remains relevant and aligned with its objective. Reforms should create a UN that is efficient in process and impactful in results. The focus must be on clarity, accountability and learning from experience. The Maldives remains committed to ensuring that the UN80 process builds on the system that works smarter, collaborates better and delivers real value to the people it serves.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank you, I thank the Permanent Representative of Maldives and I now give the floor to the Representative of Mexico, to be followed by Qatar.

**MEX**, Representative
Thank you Co Chairs and first of all, let me thank for your dedication and these efforts and also the information provided by the Briefers. A full text of our intervention will be shared with you and let me concentrate now in our proposals. Madam Couture in his Report of Mandate Implementation Review, the Secretary General makes available proposals on how to better align, deliver and assess mandates. For instance, to address the aforementioned challenges, the Secretary General proposes strengthening the Secretariat's support to Member States during the formulation of mandates to identify potential duplications among draft resolutions. Mexico welcomes this proposal. We also value the Secretariat's efforts to ensure that Member States have full insight into what already exists across the United nations system when designing new mandates. In this regard, we particularly welcome the initiative to introduce new tools that allow Member States to visualize and interrelate mandates during intergovernmental processes. We believe that a proactive and technologically enabled Secretariat role is indispensable in providing timely information, specialized expertise and strategic guidance to Member States to assess the relevance, coherence and necessity of new mandates. Now looking ahead, Mexico also believes that the success of this process will depend on the honesty and discipline of Member States. As we strongly advocated during the discussions on the revitalization of the General assembly. We must avoid introducing new resolutions that do not bring substantial changes beyond technical or factual updates. We also need to make commitment to reduce the length of mandate text to decide on biennial or triennial resolutions, and even include, whenever possible, the so called sunset clauses. Similarly, it will be rational to discontinue the practice of adopting the same resolutions both in the Committees and in the Plenary. This should include other governing bodies. Streamlining this practice will make our work less duplicative. Mexico is convinced that this process is an opportunity to assess and to optimize the mandates we have created, ensuring that remain relevant, effective and aligned with today's priorities.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank you, I thank the Representative of Mexico and I now give the floor to the Representative of Qatar, to be followed by Croatia.

**QAT**, Representative
At the outset, I'd like to thank the Co Chairs for convening this meeting, we align ourselves with the statements delivered on behalf of the group of 77 in China and on behalf of the Gulf Cooperation Council. Allow me to share a few key points in our national capacity. First, the establishment of comprehensive registries of mandates is essential to ensure full visibility across the mandate landscape and to facilitate the creation of impactful and coherent mandates. Given the fragmentation of mandating bodies, we call for accelerating the creation of these registries for all existing mandates across the UN system. Second, we support enhanced inter Secretariat cooperation to promote coherence across bodies and to avoid unproductive duplication such as parallel reporting on the same subject matter. Third, we should seek to harness the potential of artificial intelligence tools to assist Member States in the mandate drafting process. At the same time, human oversight remains indispensable to ensure that appropriate safeguards are maintained. Lastly, we recognize the difficult financial situation facing the United nations even as it is tasked with undertaking more activities within existing resources. Resources Many resolutions are increasingly long and prescriptive and lack actual resources to implement them. In this regard, we underscore the importance of establishing new mandates supported by corresponding resources. We thank you for your continued efforts and wish to assure you of Qatar's full support in your endeavors. Thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank the representative of Qatar and I now give the floor to the representative of Croatia, to be followed by Algeria.

**HRV**, Representative
Co facilitators. Thank you for the opportunity to share our views on mandate creation and for organizing the informative briefing held on October 13th. My delegation aligns itself with a statement delivered by the European Union on behalf of its Member States, and I would like to add a few remarks in my national capacity. We appreciate your comprehensive approach to examining mandates across their full cycle. As today's focus is on mandate creation, I will concentrate on three key elements we consider essential for crafting mandates that are both implementable and conducive to effective delivery. First, mandates must be concise, clear, flexible, focused and adequately resourced. These qualities ensure mandates can serve as solid foundation for program planning and budgeting and ultimately for achieving meaningful impact. Ambiguity in mandates can lead to misinterpretation and hinder efficient implementation. In this context, early and robust support from the Secretariat, including the Budget Office, is crucial. Second, mandate visibility must be enhanced across the entire UN system. Centralized and accessible information is vital to prevent duplication and overlap, and we welcome the establishment of the UN Mandate Source Registry by the Secretariat and encourage its continued development and refinement. Third, mandate creation should incorporate mechanisms for evaluating impact and delivery. Mandates are not end in themselves. We must be able to Assess, based on evidence, their contribution to improving lives. And this is, after all, the the core mission of the United nations to serve the people of the world. Thank you. I thank the representative of Croatia. And I now give the floor to the representative of Algeria, to be followed by Turkey.

**DZA**, Representative
Thank you very much. Algeria aligns itself with a statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of G77 and China. And I would like to highlight the following points on my national capacity. The discussion on the UNH process and the reflections on how mandates are created and managed go to the heart of how the United nations translate political will into action. Reform, in our view, should help the organization become clearer in purpose, more transparent in process, and more accountable in delivery. We value the development of of tools that map the UN's mandates and make them more accessible. They can certainly help us see the broader picture. However, these tools must support deliberation, not replace it. No platform or algorithm can capture the nuance, intent and compromise that come from negotiation among Member states. Technology should simplify information, not oversimplify politics or replace unstaff. Mandates are not technical outputs. They are expressions of collective intent. Their wording carries meaning, imbalance that must be preserved. Efficiency in form should never come at the cost of substance, of inclusiveness. We also welcome the push for greater financial transparency. Clarity on the cost and scope of mandate strengthens trusts. Yet it remains essential that mandates drive resources, not the contrary. Political priorities must continue to guide budgetary ones. What will determine the success of unity is whether it results in better coordination, credible data and genuine ownership by all national member states. If reform delivers this, it will make the UN more agile without losing its soul.

**TUR**, Representative
Distinguished Co Chairs, colleagues, I'd like to thank the Co Chairs for initiating these important consultations. It is crucial for the UN to continue fulfilling its mandate while evolving into an effective and efficient organization to meet the challenges of today. Turkey has long been a strong advocate for the reform efforts aimed at achieving this goal. The UN 80 initiative holds significant potential to bring about meaningful improvements to the UN structure and operational modalities across the three key work streams. The interconnectivity between these work streams remains evident. Mandates reflect the UN's intergovernmental nature and the exclusive jurisdiction of Member States, as well as their differing needs and priorities within the UN's three pillars. As such, this work stream requires particular attention. The Secretary General's report on this issue provides a valuable reference for Member States in their decision making processes. The report underscores the importance of keeping mandates concise, clear, clear and focused. Moreover, the financial implications of mandate development should be carefully considered. Periodic reviews of mandates are essential. Efforts must be made to ensure complementarity, avoid duplication, and promote synergy between the General assembly and ECOSOC mandates. While we agree that attention should be given to the increased number of meetings and associated costs, we must also recognize that facilitating multilateralism and international diplomacy by bringing Member States together remains one of the UN's core functions. It is also important to seek potential savings within the UN80 process grounded in a balanced cost benefit analysis. We must approach any changes that could undermine the authority of other key decisions making bodies, such as such as the General assembly and its main committees, with caution. These bodies, after all, represent all Member States equally, while ECOSOC offers a general inclusive platform for all countries to engage in its work. In conclusion, as we address work stream two, we must strike a careful balance between the UN's urgent need for reform, the goal of increasing its effectiveness and efficiency, and the authority of Member States to formulate mandates consistent with the UN's intergovernmental nature. I thank you.

In this regard, the immediate implementation of this resolution should be heeded in order to facilitate an operational digital mandate registry, reduce the length of mandates, and ensure that exit clauses are firmly in place. Secondly, it is crucial that the appropriate attention is given to the development of the appropriate new technology and artificial intelligence tools to root out complementarities and duplications across UN entities at the inception stage of mandate creation. Thirdly, it is of the utmost importance to ensure that at the point of mandate design the required financial and human resources are duly allocated. These new mandates must be complemented with regular budget estimates while simultaneously rooting out prescriptive assignments. Without these early linkages, mandates immediately become obsolete and ineffective. We have an obligation to correct the oversight during our review process while taking into consideration the ever looming financial constraints of the organization. In this regard, we support the proposal to refrain from mandating new tasks that are not backed by corresponding resources. Fourthly, the Secretary General proposes improved Secretariat support for Member States that during mandate creation we would need clarity on how this proposal that focuses on human and financial resource allocation would be realistically factored into future budget projections, considering the reductions and revised estimates for the 2026 budgetary process. Finally, consideration would also have to be given to how Member State ownership of resolutions can be maintained. Drafting resolutions as a combined effort may have the positive benefit of assured commitment to implementation. Thank you, Coaches. Moderation supports the Secretary General's recommendations aimed at enhancing visibility, coherence and accountability across the full cycle of mandates, while ensuring that these reforms remain guided by the intergovernmental oversight and the inclusive participation of all Member states. Mandates are the backbone of multilateral cooperation and often reflect a very delicate balance to the position of Member states. Yet the proliferation of resolutions, the growing prescriptiveness of their text, coupled with the tendency to add tasks within existing resources, may have created structural inefficiencies. At the same time, we should remember that mandates reflect the interests of Member States and as such cannot follow a one size fits all approach. Rather, it should be assessed on its own merits. Looking ahead, Ecuador considers important the following discussions take into account the interconnection among the three UN 80 work streams and that we approach them with a balance between ambition and a good sense of realism. In this regard, it proposes moving towards BNL or triennial cycles in order to use resources more efficiently and reduce the burden these repetitions impose on Member states and the system. **CUB**, Representative

We are reaffirmed that it is prerogative of Member States to make decisions on the creation of new mandates as well as the modification or termination of the existing ones. We also emphasize that the review of mandate creation should remain under the authority of Member States States and that the operational discretion of the Secretariat should be consistent with the established intergovernmental frameworks. We appreciate that the use of artificial intelligence will be subject to appropriate human oversight and will only be used as a tool to increase investigative capacity in the mandate design process. Furthermore, while we believe that mandates should be clear and as concise as possible, this should not undermine the presence of nuances that are of interest to all Member States. ## Session 3: Mandate Implementation Planning (Oct 9, 2025)
Video: /video/k1q/k1q3jsibpv

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Good morning, everybody. I call to order the informal meeting of the Working Group to hear a technical briefing on mandate creation. On behalf of Ambassador Wallace, I warmly welcome all of you to this technical briefing. We recognize the presence of Ms. Ayaka Suzuki, Director in the Executive Office of the secretary general, and Mr. Christophe Monnier, director of the Program Planning and Budget Division in the Department of Management, Strategy, Policy and Compliance, as well as Her Excellency Ms. Maritza Tan, Permanent Representative of Costa Rica, and Ms. Min Thu Pham, Founder and CEO of Project Starling. Colleagues, we have an important task ahead of us. The UN is at a critical juncture. The UN 80 initiative provides a unique opportunity to build a UN that is more effective, efficient, accountable and capable of responding to contemporary needs. This informal ad hoc working group provides the mechanism for us as Member States, to be constructively engaged in the effort to strengthen the ability of this organisation to deliver concrete outcomes for the people of the planet as envisaged in the Charter. We encourage Member States to seize this opportunity as the first intergovernmental process launched under the UN80 initiative. The this informal ad hoc working group has a particular responsibility to build trust and mutual understanding and to demonstrate that concrete progress can be made on reforms that can strengthen this organisation and improve the effectiveness, efficiency and impact of its work, as outlined in our letter. On the 7th of October we will begin our work with a Discovery phase. This will include consultations among Working Group members and, subject to the interest of Working Group members, research and analysis to ensure our consultations are informed by empirical evidence and data. Today's briefing marks the start of this Discovery phase and will run until mid December. It is the first of three shorts executive level briefings, each of which will focus on one of the three stages of the mandate life cycle creation, implementation and review. The briefings will provide an opportunity for senior leaders to consider the recommendations under each section of the Secretary General's report on the Mandate Implementation Review, which was published on 31 July in more detail. The briefing also provides you with an opportunity to consider other ideas and perspectives and to ask questions. Each technical briefing will then be followed by consultations on the same topic about a week later. At the consultation, Working Group members will have the opportunity to hear responses to the questions posed at the briefing sessions, convey their views on the recommendations provided in the Secretary General's report or by the briefers, and importantly, put forward their own ideas and proposals for consideration. Today's briefing will focus on the topic of mandate creation. We have invited four briefers to speak on this topic, each has been selected for their depth of knowledge and insights on mandate creation, with each offering different perspectives for our consideration. Like all of us here today, they are invested in the effective functioning of the un. Following their presentations, there will be an opportunity for Working Group members to ask questions of our briefers. As noted in our letters of 7 and the 10October, we remind colleagues that today's session is focused on providing Working Group members with information. We welcome members wishing to deliver statements outlining their perspectives and proposals on mandate creation, to do so at the consultations scheduled for 23rd of October. And I now give the floor to my Co Chair, Ambassador Wallace.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Thank you so much, Co Chair. And we will move immediately into the work for today. I will therefore give the floor to Mr. Ayaka Suzuki, Director in the Executive Office of the Secretary General, to brief the Working Group on current practice exercised in the creation of mandates and to provide an elaboration on the recommendations contained in the Secretary General's report on mandate creation. I also invite her to update the Working Group on progress in taking forward the recommendations in the report that lie within the Secretary General's existing authority as Chief Administrative Officer and what further support the Secretariat may be able to provide to Member States when creating mandates, including access to data and analysis. Mr. Zukey, I give you the floor.

**UN Secretariat** | Ayaka Suzuki, Director, EOSG
Thank you very much, madam. Co chair. Mr. Co chair, good afternoon, or good morning rather. Excellency and Distinguished Delegates, thank you so much to the Co Chairs for inviting us to deliver a briefing today. It's a great pleasure to be here. So today we will walk through key insights and suggestions from the Mandate Implementation Review Report which was issued on 31st of July. And the focus today, as mentioned by the Co Chairs, is the creation phase, where mandates are made and how better visibility, design and stronger collaboration and enhanced support could improve delivery of mandates with a bigger impact. With this presentation, my team and I hope to contribute to a shared understanding of this process. So first, let us just recall from the report that every mandate has a life cycle. If you could advance to the next slide, that would be great. So creation, delivery and review, ideally connected via feedback. When mandate creation is clear and coordinated, delivery improves and review produces learning that in turn improves the next iteration of the mandate. Based on the insight in the report, our goal is structure, strengthening our support to you in that first step so that the whole system can perform better. So before I dive in, let me answer a simple question. How would one define a mandate in simple terms? And for the purpose of the report, we're treating a mandate as a request or directive for action written in the operative paragraphs of of a resolution or decision adopted by mandating organs such as the General Assembly. It is the instruction that tells the United nations or other implementing entities that Member States have asked it to do. This slide shows how a resolution might translate into mandates and results so for example, in resolution General assembly resolution on the pattern of conferences, which was adopted in December 2023, which is the mandate source, contains a request to the Secretary General to produce a report on conferences. This report needs to be planned and budgeted in the program budget. Post and non post. Resources need to be approved so that the requested report can be prepared. Ideally, the report is delivered on time and has data to support your review of our performance and informed decisions on how to improve is a simple chain Decisions, Mandates, Actions and results for Mandate creation the report identifies limited visibility as a key challenge which is the situation today across the UN system. Mandates originate from many bodies, not just the main organs of the United nations, but also mandating organs of specialized agencies or related organizations. Since 1946, more than 40,000 resolutions have been adopted in the General Assembly, Security Council, ecosoc and Human Rights Council alone. However, most bodies lack straightforward and up to date registries, let alone a registry that looks across all of these bodies. That increases the risk of unintended duplication and overlap. The review therefore recommends building comprehensive registries and strengthening coordination among secretariats that are supporting these governing bodies. In short, visibility matters because it is hard to manage better what is hard to see. So to start closing that visibility gap, we have launched mandates.un.org which was launched, as you may recall, in conjunction with the release of the Mandate Implementation Review Report. This site compiles the source documents the UN entities cite when explaining why their programs exist and why they need resources. You can search by entity, mandating organ or subject, and quickly see links between mandate sources and proposed programs. It is interactive and we encourage everyone who have not tried this to explore it. New features are also regularly added. I personally find this to be a very exciting new tool. Here is a concrete example to see what you might find when you use this tool. Youth. You can see how the same subject of Youth appears in 48 resolutions or decisions across eight different bodies the General Assembly, ECOSOC, Security Council, Human Rights Council, and so on. Some 30 UN entities cited these sources 128 times in support of programs proposed for 2026. Sometimes it's helpful, sometimes it may be duplicative and may include extra reporting. Using this tool, mandates un.org we can immediately trace potential overlaps and their sources. As I said, everything's interactive and you could click and it links you to necessary documents later. Ambassador Schwalger, we talked about disability. We have an example of that as well, and we can take a look at that. So visibility is only the first part. The design of mandate shapes how well they can be delivered. And here three challenges stand out. Longer text resolutions are getting lengthier and more complex. More prescriptive wording, which leads to less flexibility for implementation and tasking within existing resources without reducing previously mandated tasks, is becoming more frequent. And it's now appearing more often than even budget is, even as budgets decline. So the report proposes four potential remedies. First, better secretariat support during drafting. Second, AI tools that can help flag potential duplications or complementarities. Third, mandates framed so that the Secretary General can allocate the task to those with the best comparative advantages. And fourth, avoiding new tasks without corresponding resources or reduction in tasks. The message here is clarity, flexibility and practicality. These are what make mandates deliverable. Now, creating better mandates is a shared responsibility. In simple terms, it takes people, guidance and tools that we have summarized on this slide. People capacity, the Technical Secretariat, subject matter experts and training or tools to support efficient drafting, guidance and frameworks to help negotiators craft better processes and language on mandated activities, entities and resource implications. Data and AI tools, registries and drafting modules that flag overlap before it happens. Together, we believe these elements can form the basis of renewed collaboration between all of us. So let me end with three key takeaways. First. First, visibility matters. More accessible information prevents duplication and improves coordination. Second, design drives delivery more concise, clear, properly resourced mandates achieve real impact. Third, enhanced support, stronger people guidance technology and support to assist your work. Our next briefing in the next iteration of this series will go deeper into delivery and resources resourcing. But for today, our core message is simple. And again, this is for the creation phase of the mandate life cycle. Visibility leads to better design leads to stronger delivery equals greater impact. Thank you and I look forward to your questions and reflections. And thank you for giving me the opportunity.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Co Chairs thank you so much, Ms. Suzuki, and to your team for preparing such an interesting and informative presentation. I now give the floor to Mr. Christophe Monnier, Director of the Program Planning and Budget Division in the Department of Management, Strategy, Planning, Policy and Compliance, to brief the Working Group on the relationship between mandate creation and the UN budget process, including how it currently works and how it might be improved.

**UN Secretariat** | Christophe Monnier, Director, PPBD (DMSPC)
Christoph thank you, Ambassador Wallace. And thanks to Ambassador Schrager and Ambassador Wallace, the co Chair of the Working Group, for having us today and giving us this opportunity of the briefing on the I'll pass a few moments to explain a few concepts and then discuss a bit more on the budget part. So on the Legislative framework, the purpose of this slide is to explain how we define a mandate in a budget context and link it to the budget process. So legislative mandate as indicated by my colleague Ayaka is a request for action that is addressed to the Secretary General on Executive Head, acting on his behalf in a resolution or decision adopted by a competent United nations intergovernmental organ. And this is governed by the policy document we call PPBME that has a very long name you see on this slide. The budget themselves are also driven by such requests for action and the Rule 153 of the Rules of Procedure of the General assembly provides two checkpoints to ensure that Member States are fully aware of the resulting resource requirements, one before a resolution is recommended for approval by a Committee and a second checkpoint before approval by the General assembly following a review by the ACABQ and the Fifth Committee. Now how are budget derived from resolution and decisions? The resolutions or decisions I'll be speaking only about resolution for simplification, but I always mean both are what drives the results based budget containing the program plan and the resource proposals. In a resolution we typically have two aspects the preambler paragraphs which express the background, sentiment and concern of Member States and the operative paragraph that provide guidance and mandates for the Secretariat or other stakeholders. The concerns expressed in the preambler paragraph help the Secretariat to formulate a long term objective which reflects the desire and State to address the needs of Member States and the operative paragraphs contain the mandates for the Secretariat to determine the deliverable and output which the Secretariat is expected to produce in order to contribute to the collective achievement of the objective. The strategy is what links the both and is the plan approach to produce the mandated deliverable to meet the needs of Member States and most effectively contribute to the long term objective. On the left side you see the performance measures that are there to demonstrate the progress made, while on the right hand side the inputs on the purple box are the budgetary resources that are required to fund the activities undertaken by the Secretariat to produce the mandated deliverables. So we now focus on those budgetary resources. You've heard us talk about program budget implications. Those are linked to a resolution that indicates that resource that has implications resource implication so a pbi, how we call it is prepared in response to a draft resolution with mandate to the Secretariat to conduct new or expanded activities. It is communicated to Member States prior to the adoption of of a draft resolution and is then later issued as a supplementary budget to request additional resources to implement mandates not included in the current approved budget or proposed budget. After that, it's considered by the ACABQ and then the Fifth Committee before being approved through a formal decision of the General Assembly. Alongside approval of the resolution, we have a different format for PBIS called Oral Statement. Those are used instead of a PBI when we have three situations. Either the resource implications are not yet known and are pending future decision by Member States, for example, if the format and location of a new meeting or conference is still to be defined if the frequency or size of a new report is also to be defined. The second case is when the resource implications are known but will be communicated in a subsequent revised estimate, possibly grouping several program budget implications. This is how, for example, we proceed with PBIS resulting from ECOSOC decisions or from the Human Rights Council. The third situation is when resource implication relates to a future proposed program budget not yet issued by the Secretary General. The resource implication will be proposed in the relevant future proposed program budget, for example, if anything is done today with an impact on 2027 or later, because those are not yet proposed program budget. Now if we look at the budgetary implications are clearly directly influenced by nuances in the language used in the resolution. Without fully getting into the details of this slide, I wanted to mention how the language of the operative paragraph of a resolution drives the understanding by the Secretariat of the intent of Member States to be interpreted as having potential budgetary implications. The resolution must contain a binding request for action and require new or expanded activities. In the interest of time, I will not develop further at this stage, but I would like to highlight the need to ensure the mandate creation process makes the intent of Member States very clear. To warrant the implementation by the Secretariat or the UN system corresponds to the expectations on the aspect of costing resolutions. I'd like to mention that upon reviewing a draft resolution, the Secretariat conducts an internal review to determine the workload related to the required activities and whether these activities can be absorbed within existing resources or what additional resources will be required. The tool presented by my colleague Ayaka would be instrumental in helping both Member States and the Secretariat identifying connections, synergies or potential duplication with existing deliverables and activities. Technically, the use of the expression within existing resource in the draft resolution of the Main Committee does not preclude budgetary implication if the Secretariat assesses that the delivery of those additional mandated activity cannot be absorbed within existing resources. I know that the use of the expression has become more frequent over the years and is quite unpopular amongst my colleagues. I feel, however, that it could be a good indicator of the true intent of Member State within existing resources signals to the Secretariat that Member States expect the additional activities to be of a relatively low impact and to be absorbed within existing resources, accepting de facto that those existing resources originally given for other mandated activity will now be shared for those additional mandated activity, possibly leading to a lessening of some other activities. The absence of this expression within existing resource signaled to the Secretariat that that Member States are ready to consider providing additional resources for those new mandated activities as assessed under the strict principle governing in particular the revised estimates relating to the proposed program budget for 2026 with an expectation of delivery under an efficient framework, making full use of possible synergies and avoiding duplications and this will be my last slide in terms of support to Member States in linking the mandate creation and the budgetary resources. We have in place briefings to the main Committees on the PBI process and we are open to provide them more frequently or to larger audience and to enrich them to better meet the requirements of Member States and are very interested in your feedback on those in terms of support on the drafting. While we are actively supporting the process today, improvements could be made to be more present throughout the maturation of the resolution, but this would require additional focus resources. In addition, it's also noted that during conferences the same drafting consultation with PPBD does not usually take place and this is a weak point that might require further discussions on improving the review and presentation of program plans. We review the mandates and deliverables annually to identify those no longer required to be referenced and and potential streamlining of activities to ensure effective use of resources in the implementation of mandates. The outcome of this Working Group and proposal Under Work Stream 3 would provide opportunities for further strengthening program plans. The revised estimate report on the program budget and support account that I mentioned earlier reflects some of these areas where program managers have identified areas where they can increase the use of existing mechanism to identify opportunities to consolidate and streamline work, including recommendations to change the frequency, format or terminate specific outputs and deliverable. Mr. Chair, thank you very much.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank Mr. Monnier for that interesting presentation. I now invite Her Excellency Maritza Chan, Permanent Representative of Costa Rica, to provide insights from the perspective of of a Member State who has been involved in the creation of mandates in a number of different capacities. Ambassador Chan, the floor is yours.

**CRI** | Maritza Chan, Permanent Representative
Thank you, Ambassador, Excellency, Colleagues, I wish to thank Ambassador Schwager and Wallace for inviting me to share our views. We're certain that with your leadership this will be a successful process. Mandates are truly at the heart of of everything the United nations does. Each mandate reflects an agreement among Member States on how to address an issue and what action needs to be taken. Yet the current context compels us to be more deliberate in how we create mandates. I will share key observations from Costa Rica's experience in either chairing, co facilitating or presiding over different intergovernmental processes. I will highlight persistent experiences which reveal both affected approaches and persistent challenges. A positive example comes from the 2024 conference to review progress in the implementation of the Program of Action to Prevent, Combat and Eradicate Illicit Trade in Small Arms Allied Weapons in all its aspects. RepCom4 in response to the new technological developments such as polymer and modular weapons and 3D printing, Member States agree to establish an Open Endeavor Technical Expert Group. This mandate was needs driven. It emerged from real challenges, focus narrowly on actionable outcomes and build on previous commitments while adapting to new context. The Small Arms and Light Weapons Fellowship Program exemplifies a mandate designed for direct impact. When a mandate is clearly justified, appropriately scope and linked to current realities, it strengthens the UN capacity to respond. A second experience I'd like to share is the 2024 negotiation of the Modalities Resolution for the High Level Meeting on addressing the existential threats posed by sea level rise. The General assembly decided to convene a high level meeting within existing resources, but later on it became evident that those existing resources would not be enough. Additional throughout the negotiation process, delegations recognized that the meeting alone would not suffice for the UN to properly start to address this issue. So three additional mandates were created a Secretary General's report with inputs from Member states, a political declaration, and the High Level Meeting itself. This case highlights two points. First, new thematic areas often require new mandates to build institutional attention from the ground up. Secondly, early coordination with DGACM was essential to anticipate budgetary implications and whether mandates can be realistically implemented and properly resourced. When chairing the first committee in 2024, we observed a third example with both potential and pitfalls. During the 29th session, the committee adopted 77 draft resolutions and decisions. However, only 27 were adopted without a vote. The remaining 50 required recorded votes with 156 separate specific paragraphs votes requested, leading to 201 total votes compared to 148 the previous year. Of course, not all resolutions create mandates. Mandates are created by resolutions or decisions with operative or actional elements. Many resolutions have operative paragraphs that in fact do not invoke action, and frequently operative paragraphs which invoke action are unclear in their intent. Sometimes this is the result of finely balanced negotiations, sometimes it is unintended. Extracting mandates from resolutions and decisions can lead to the Secretariat and wider UN system which implements them to have to make subjective decisions about Member States intentions. As we all know, many resolutions are long, repetitive and lacked sunset clauses or review clauses. One off resolutions are by far the exception rather than the norm. Many also include rollover annual requests for compilations of State views that carry production cost. While the compilations themselves gather little interaction, bringing into question the value of the requests themselves, what at the same time show the difficulty for States to follow up on those requests? This is a problem in several committees. This combination of voting fragmentation, procedural inefficiency and no engagement outputs compel us to ask fundamental questions in a Committee such as the first committee when in 2024 only 27 out of 77 resolutions and decisions achieved consensus. Should we pursue more resolutions annually or would fewer, better focused proposals on biennial or triennial cycles allow time for genuine consensus building? Similarly, we must question whether to continue commissioning Secretary General's reports and annual compilations at the rate we have been doing so when resources are constrained and engagement remains minimal. For instance, first committee mandates that consistently receive fewer than 10 replies could transition to less frequent reporting cycles, allowing the Secretariat to focus resources on higher quality, more impactful outcomes and provide states with more time to fulfill the requirements requirements. Another example is my experience as Vice President of the ECOSOC Humanitarian affairs segment in 20242025 we received divergent views about the annual resolution, particularly its overlap with the General assembly omnibus Humanitarian Resolution. Both segments traditionally produced negotiated outcome documents through repetitive annual discussions. In 2025, Costa Rica took a different approach. Instead of negotiating yet another formal outcome document, we issue a Call to Action. This shorter, more strategic document, developed through streamlined consultation with Member States, sent a strong collective message of support to those affected by humanitarian emergencies and to the humanitarian system. Instead, the result 151 states supported the call to action, a commitment beyond ECOSOC's membership. How can we improve mandate creation? Now turning to this question of improving mandate quality and discipline, I would like to make five points. Firstly, mandates are more likely to be implemented and deliver quality outcomes for people and planet if they are created thoughtfully at the outset. Secondly, there is a growing trend of resolution coming commissioning SG reports. In my experience, the SG reports that actually get read and used are the ones with expert analysis. We saw this with the new topics like AI, they sparked real debate because they offer insight, not just aggregation. A shift from compilation to analysis in SG reports would be transformative. Thirdly, before we create more mandates, we need to map what we already have. When we spotted the duplication between ECOSOC and the General assembly humanitarian resolutions, we addressed it, but that was one case we happened to notice. We need systemic visibility and thus I welcome this fund finding in the SG report on Monday. Implementation Review Fourthly, some simple tests might help us understand whether a mandate we have created is of sufficient enduring interest to the collective membership. Does a mandate with which commissions and SG report get more than 20 responses does a mandate which establishes an agenda item on the UN's books result in engagement from more than a dozen states? Has the mandate produced something concrete in three years? Are the costs reasonable for what the mandate produces? And how would we know unless a monitoring and evaluation mechanism is built in? If we can say yes to to these questions, should it trigger a review of the mandate? Fifthly, I welcome the proposal in the ESRI report which suggests the Secretariat could do a better job of assisting Member States to create higher quality mandates. I encourage the Secretariat to engage with Member States, including those who lead initiatives. Observe as co facilitators or co chair for process on what this support could look like. This is also a shared responsibility. All co facilitators should seek that assistance from the start of each process. Finally, I'd like to add one more thing. Leadership makes a real difference in how mandates are created. It matters who the PGA designates to run a negotiation, and it matters what experience those persons bring. We've seen that skill facilitation can turn a contentious process into a productive one, and that preparation and institutional knowledge help Member States find common ground. Getting the right people in these roles is how we create mandates which delivered meaningful outcomes. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank Ambassador Chan for that interesting and thought provoking presentation. I now invite Ms. Mintu Pam, co Founder and CEO of Project Starling, to provide insights and lessons learned from the last intergovernmental process to consider reform of mandates in 2006 in which she played a central role within the UN secretariat.

**Project Starling** | Minh-Thu Pham, Founder & CEO
Thank you so much. Thank you Excellencies. And thank you Ambassador, the Ambassadors from Jamaica and New Zealand for inviting me here to address you all. Ladies and Gentlemen, distinguished guests, as Ambassador Wallace has just mentioned, I had the, the fortune, or maybe for some the misfortune of being designated within Kofi Annan's office actually 20 years ago now in October of 2005, to lead the Secretariat's mandate review to support the Member States mandate review. And so my comments draw from that experience. And I was heartened to hear that so many of you wanted to hear about what were some of the lessons from that time. So let me get right to it. The 2006-2007 mandate review, one of the core lessons from that is that a detailed backwards looking intergovernmental review of existing mandates was unmanageable. The reason for that is because you don't have the information that you Member States need to do a full review of past and current mandates, including and especially budgetary information that would be required. And third, success for this mandate review, you should carefully think about what are the criteria for success. I was encouraged to see that the co facilitators have laid out a really constructive process, I think, for how you might consider the mandates and especially the process by which mandates are created, delivered and reviewed. And that success should not be reviewed. Success should not be determined based on how many mandates you get rid of. As you heard from Ms. Suzuki earlier today, mandates can be everything from establishing an organization to just designating a report to be written. And those are very, very different types of mandates. So if you say we got rid of one mandate, that could have been the issuing of a report or an entire institution or agency or fund or program within the un. Those are very different things. So just consider how you consider success. Mandates are carefully negotiated outcomes of extensive and often contentious intergovernmental negotiations. You all know that very, very well. Reviewing all existing mandates could mean that you are relitigating decades of difficult compromises. The current governments that are in place now, the ones that you all lead, would be forced to rehash agreements that were made by their predecessors and that would reopen old wounds. You are in a different political context right now. And rehashing old mandates that may or may not still be relevant, I think would reopen those old wounds. And I think you may find that you'll be relitigating 80 years of diplomacy. I have to say the process that the co facilitators have taken to look forward is the way to help build goodwill and trust, which is needed at the beginning of an extensive reform process. In 2006 and 2008, member states took a clerical approach to avoid reopening old wounds. That was the right call at that time, given that the 2006 World Summit's directive, meaning the language that was in the World Summit outcome, was to, quote, review all mandates older than five years, originating from resolutions of the General assembly and other organs. That meant that we did not have the option that you have in front of you now, which is to look forward at the mandate life cycle and what should be done to put in place the systems to make it better. The reason why the past mandate review didn't work so well was because the directive was to review old mandates older than five years. And back then, after two and a half years of tedious work to examine individual mandates for duplication and delivery, the process simply lost steam. In fact, I left the Secretary General's office in the middle of 2007, about halfway through, because I also lost steam this time, I suggest that we look forward. Just as the co facilitators have advised you. You will always find more unity looking forward into the future than looking backwards into the past. You should focus instead on the fundamental reason why we have a mandate problem, the mandate life cycle. And it really is a cycle. It's a feedback loop. How mandates are created is really a byproduct of what happened before then and what exists currently within the system. It's really encouraging to see that the Secretary General has addressed the systemic challenge in his report. And that was something that we in 2006 in Kofi Annan's office also highlighted at the time. We had to focus on old mandates because of the World Summit's mandate. But I really do urge you to follow the lead this time, to look into the future. If you take decisions to strengthen the way that we create and monitor mandates, you'll be better off, the UN will be better off, and you'll enable us also to potentially refresh our existing programs. What do I mean by that? Here's the core problem. Member states create mandates to address transnational problems. So there are challenges in the world that you see the UN playing a role to address. And yet, too often, as the Secretary General's report indicates, you have to do this without full insight into what already exists across the UN system. You don't know in fact what's working, what's potentially overlapping, and where right resources could be shifted or what programs could be updated to then respond to the challenge that you're having to address. In the absence of that visibility, decisions rely on incomplete information or even your own national analyses rather than shared evidence that you all are working from the same set of information. And as you all know not every member state can conduct their own national analyses. As a result, this often results in fragmentation, which you all know is a major problem within the system where some parts of the UN are perceived as more effective than others. This can also increase politicization in the intergovernmental decision making process where you're all relying on your own information or your own perceptions as to what needs to be done. The Secretariat is capable of providing the assessments of implementation and that is essential for you to create new mandates. However, that requires investment and I know that's not something we're talking about necessarily in an environment of budgetary austerity, but it really does require investment. Around the world, well run organizations devote about 3 to 10% of their budgets to program evaluation. You all know this within your own bureaucracies, the UN system, and this is my own calculation, and it was our calculation back in 2006 that the UN spends likely closer to about 1% or less than 1% of its budget on proper program evaluation. You don't know what's happening if you don't have a sense of the assessment of how the programs are working. And I'd encourage that perhaps the Secretariat or others can provide you with this specific number. How much does the UN actually devote to proper program evaluation? If we strengthen the UN's capacity to do internal, meaning self, evaluation, as well as external independent evaluation, meaning externally validated assessments of the UN's work that would provide you with evidence that can then inform your creation of mandates, Member States, you all have to be given the information so that you understand and can then discuss and adopt new mandates. Essentially, it's so that you can be better custodians of your mandates. This is essentially what Secretary General Kofi annan recommended in 2006 and what the current Secretary General has just suggested in his report. In that 2006 assessment, we realized that we had to significantly invest in evaluation capacity across the system. That includes oios, the Joint Inspections Unit, and then individual program based inside the departments and offices and agencies self evaluation so that you could then have better streamlined reporting and more transparent understanding of what is actually required budgetary wise when you ask for a new mandate and when the Budget Office is then giving you a program budget implication. This is the essence of evidence. Informed policymaking. In the Secretary General's recent report under Mandate Creation, here's my recommendation. He recommends that improving Secretariat support for Member States during mandate creation and that you need to complement budget estimates with information on related programmatic activities across the system. I think that's absolutely necessary. And I would urge you all to consider going one step further. Build into every mandate budget a modest dedicated percentage for evaluation, so that from the outset, when a mandate is created as part of the cycle, you are building in implementation, learning and assessment as part of the design of mandates. If we don't know, and we're not budgeting for the system to understand how well and whether your mandates were implemented, there's no way you would know what should be the next mandate that you create for those of you, Because I've suggested a forward looking approach for those of you who are concerned about reviewing existing mandates. What do we do about the backlog of our existing mandates, the thousands that we've discussed, and to know whether they are duplicative or overlap or even remain relevant? I'd suggest that the best solution is not to conduct another sweeping review, at least not yet, maybe a little bit later in your phases, but rather to make these changes, investing in understanding what the visibility across a system of the mandates are, and then whether or not the evaluation is there to know how well those mandates are being done. If you do that, then you'll know whether or not the existing mandates are still relevant or even if they've been delivered well. What you need is the information that tells you not only what activities took place, which is essentially what the content of many of the UN's reports are, but also you need to know, what difference did it make. That information strengthens transparency, accountability, and ultimately public trust, which is in short supply these days, especially in institutions like this one. And this can be reported back to you in a way that enables even the smallest of missions. I know how strapped, because I work with so many of you, how strapped some of the smallest missions are. You cannot read the thousands of reports that the UN issues every day, every day, every year. So it's so important so that you can really understand and then credibly adopt new mandates. So my appeal to you today is not about an issue that's going to make the headlines. It's about the quiet systems that ultimately make multilateral cooperation work. I've devoted my career to improving how the UN delivers because I know personally what a functioning UN can do. My life is. I'm here because of the un. When I was three, after the war, my family fled Vietnam by boat. We were rescued by the UN High Commissioner for Refugees and brought to a refugee camp that UNHCR ran in Indonesia before being resettled. My life is shaped by the UN's ability to work when it works well when it's adequately funded, when we invest in knowing how well it works, the UN can save lives and expands what's possible for millions. Strengthening your ability to better adopt mandates with better evaluative information, with better visibility across the system, so that the UN can learn and adapt is one of the surest ways that it can continue to do so. This was a huge lesson from 2006. The poet Edna St Vincent Millais said, life is not one thing after another. Life is not one thing after another. It's one damn thing over and over again. Her point was that life keeps repeating itself and the same problems keep coming up again and again. I can't believe that I'm here 20 years later talking to you about something that is a scar. I'm still recovering from the 2006 mandate review. But by improving, you really have a great opportunity right now. By improving the mandate life cycle, as has been proposed, and building into that the budget that is needed to do evaluation, by addressing these systemic issues, you can try to prevent the same mandate problems from coming up again and again. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Thank you so much, Ms. Pham, for those insights and for that personal touch as well. The impact of this organization is legendary. It's our task to make sure that it remains so going forward. In the time remaining, I'm going to now open the floor for any questions from delegations on the briefings we have just heard. I'm just going to remind you, delegations, that this briefing will be followed in a week or 10 days by informal consultations at which there will be an opportunity for delegations to deliver prepared statements to ensure today that we can take as many questions as possible in the next half an hour. We're asking that you keep your interventions short, no longer than a minute and a half or so. With that, I give the floor to the distinguished representative of the European Union, to be followed by China.

**EU**, Representative

The report recommends that greater flexibility be granted to the SG to assign tasks when designing or implementing mandates. From our perspective, the solution proposed is not necessarily in line with the problem identified. The question would be is it possible for the Secretariat to provide more in depth analysis on how the proposal in the the report would enhance efficiency? In our view, if the mandates formulation remains unclear, it would lead to delays in implementation, interagency conflict, etc. It is the Member States who should spy specify the implementing entity and the Secretariat which should provide adequate support to Member States to make an informed decision. Second is on the proposal for linking new mandates to corresponding resources or reductions elsewhere. We can agree to this principle when Member States are creating new mandates to be implemented within existing resources. However, applying such a principle to all new mandates is a point of concern. We cannot expect the UN to respond to new emerging challenges if PBIS are our overarching concern. So the question here is can the Secretariat inform whether this principle is implementable on all new mandates and not just those which are specified to be implemented within existing resources and what this would imply in practice for recent new mandates. Third, we agree with the proposal for accelerating the creation of comprehensive registries of existing mandate text issued by all mandating bodies. We recall that the 2006 exercise highlighted by Ms. Fam also created a mandate Registry, but we are unaware of its fate. We would like to ask the Secretariat what arrangements are in place to ensure that such registries remain in place even after the Unity Initiative is completed, and we would also be very happy to hear what what you have to respond to the Chinese question about what resources are being spent on the Registry. The creation of the Registry itself. Thank you so much.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank the distinguished representative of the Kingdom of the Netherlands. And with that I try to hand the floor back over to my co chair for some summary remarks.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you, Ambassador Wallace. And first of all, thank you to all of you. I cannot say how much we appreciate the trust that you have placed in us by working with us in the format and the process as we've prescribed. And we can see that in the presentation of your question and kind of early markers of your intent to come back with fuller ideas and proposals at the session on the 23rd of October. I'd also like to thank the briefers. Each one of you, in your own ways, I think, brought different aspects of this discussion on mandate creation to the fore. This discussion was very rich. It's hard to summarise, but let me at least just touch on a few points before we close on what I think we may have heard today. I was very taken by Mintu's comment that quiet systems make multilateralism work. And I think that is so true and that is the exercise we're embarked on. So it's so great to hear from so many of you that you welcomed the opportunity to hear from some of the lessons we've learned in the past so that we can apply those to our work now and in the future. I heard a lot of commentary around there is a great desire to have better design of the way we make mandates and the reason for that is because we all want to have better delivery and better impact. That came through from both our presenters and from you as well. Having said that, it was always. It was also acknowledged in this discussion that because of the way we negotiate as Member States together, of course some of the ways that we create mandates are a product of our own decision making and sometimes come with unintended consequences, but sometimes come with intended consequences as well. And of course that is completely legitimate. It, however, does raise questions around clarity. We had comments and questions around how are mandates sourced out of the system? How does the Secretariat interpret what we produce as Member States? And we asked and heard a lot of questions around more information. A desire for increased visibility so that that can support delivery. But also transparency came through very strongly as well. It was noted by some in their questions in the briefers that ambiguity can cause challenges for the Secretariat, but can cause challenges for us as Member states too, and our own evaluation or satisfaction on how we believe that mandates are delivered upon or not. There was much discussion around the relationship between the creation of mandates and how resources are allocated to those mandates. Both comments and questions around the information that we need before we start and the better information and understanding of the financial impacts of mandates perhaps before we conclude them. A very strong theme came through today about the importance of monitoring and evaluation, the importance of having better assessments of what we've actually done to inform what we do in the future. And I heard clients comments and questions around not only the impacts of mandates on our finances and our funding, but also on our time and also on our impact. So we have questions there for the briefers as well. Many of you referred to the SG's report. Many of you welcomed some of the recommendations. Many of you had questions about some of the recommendations, whether they were the right recommendations, and some of you who had questions asking for elaboration on some of them as well. There were many questions and comments around the tools, the registry that's been mentioned. What is its functionality, how is it being resourced? We had questions about AI, how can AI help us? But also what are some of the caveats we need to consider when we think about the use of AI in this exercise. Many of you ask questions of about what does giving the SG increased flexibility mean in terms of allocating parts of the system to deliver on mandates. And there was a lot of discussion around the implications of within existing resources. We heard from a couple, including Maritza, about how SG reports are consumed, one of the frequent mandates that we see in resolutions, and there was an encouragement to move from compilation of information to analysis. We also of course heard questions beyond the recommendation in the SG's report and we started to see some flavour and hints of things that you might bring back to us on the 23rd as well. I think there's a great desire to see how the tool that the Secretariat has developed and maybe outside of this format we can ask the Secretary Secretariat to provide a very informal, informal meeting sometime between now and the 23rd where they can show you your experts how the tool works and you can see some of the functionality by testing it on a few case studies that might be something we will commission from the Secretariat. So that was my attempt to summarise this hour and a half or so or almost two hours this morning. I think it's been a really rich discussion and as co chairs we're really thrilled and I'm looking forward to hearing more from you. On the 23rd, but also, of course, before that, as we said at the first session, we remain available to you to meet you individually, to meet you in groups, to meet you in clusters who are interested in particular aspects of this process. So thank you very much again for today. And I have to do the formalities to get close us out. So before adjourning, as previously announced, the next meeting will take place on the 23rd of October. As I said, in the interim, if you wish to meet with us directly in person or by in writing, you can also do so to the email address that we provided in the letters on 7th and 10th of October. But for today, this meeting is adjourned. Thank you.
