## Session 5: Review and Assessment (Nov 14 Part 1, 2025)
Video: /video/k1j/k1jax5ye21

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
Good morning. Good morning, colleagues. May I invite you to take your seats? It. Good morning. I call to order the informal meeting of the Working Group for consultations on the matter of mandate implementation. On behalf of Ambassador Schwarger, I warmly welcome all of you to today's consultations on mandate implementation under the ongoing UNAT review process. We begin by recognizing the presence of Mr. Guy Ryder, under Secretary General for Policy and chair of the UN 80 task force his Excellency Christian Venavesso, Permanent representative of Liechtenstein Mr. Martin Kimani, President and Chief Executive Officer of the African center and former Permanent Representative of Kenya and who is joining us virtually along with Mr. Meera Haq, former United Nations Under Secretary General for the Department of Field Support, former co Chair of the High Level Independent Panel on Peace Operations and former Secretary General Special Representative for Timor Lest and Head of the United Nations Integrated Mission in East Timor. The objective of this session is to collectively reflect on the insights and recommendations emerging from the Mandate implementation briefing on 30 October and to identify concrete next step that can enhance efficiency, transparency and accountability in how mandates are implemented across the United nations system. We welcome views on the recommendations made on mandate implementation in the Secretary General's report, as well as any additional proposals Working Group members may wish to put forward themselves. We aim to foster a shared understanding of the challenges and opportunities inherent in mandate implementation, strengthen coordination among entities and ensure that our collective efforts are guided by the principles of effectiveness, inclusivity and results oriented delivery in support of the organization's overarching goals. In this vein, we wish to reassure Member States that we have taken careful note of the valuable recommendations provided to the Working Group thus far. We reiterate the importance of maintaining a collaborative and inclusive approach with the full engagement of all Member States to ensure a process that is both effective and transparent. Our shared objective remains to achieve the highest possible level of ambition. By the conclusion of this exercise, we aim to inspire fresh, pragmatic and innovative thinking that strengthens the culture of mandate design and implementation across the United nations system. As we continue our engagement in this discovery phase, we hope to deepen our collective understanding of mandates, guided by empirical evidence, analytical insights and lessons drawn from best practices across the United nations system. There appear to be some emerging areas of common interest that we have discerned from the consultations and discussions to date. It is our intention to elaborate on those when we provide our Interim report to you on December 11th and in our subsequent communications to you ahead of our work on the Production phase, which will begin in Mid January. We look forward to hearing more from you today. As we look at mandate implementation. Today, Working Group members will first hear responses to questions posed at the Mandate implementation briefing on 30 October. As you're aware, answers provided by the Secretariat were shared under cover of a letter from the co chairs on the 12th of November and these will also be made available on the UN80 Initiative website. Additionally, as advised in that letter, Working Group members will have the opportunity today to express their their views on mandate implementation and we look forward to hearing your ideas and proposals. We invite you please to submit your interventions as well as any additional inputs by uploading these to the Edelegate portal, as well as sending these to us in writing via our email address@unity mandates.co chairs Gmail. As expressed in our last meeting, we intend to compile and publish the contributions of Working Group members on the UNAT Initiative website unless otherwise indicated by you. Your inputs will contribute to building a comprehensive picture of how we might better tackle mandates in the future. I now pass the floor to my co Chair, Ambassador Schwalgo.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you. Good morning, everybody. Before we turn to the questions posed to the briefers, we just wanted to take this opportunity to share that as Co Chairs, we have recently held engagements with the presidents of the Boards and leadership teams of several UN funds, programs and agencies to deepen our understanding of how the issue of mandate creation, implementation and review is being considered in their organisations and what this could mean for the Working Groups, development, deliberations and approach going forward. We have also been meeting with the Chairs of the Main Committees and the co facilitators of the GA revitalization process to ensure that we understand their operating context and any implications for this Working Group. We also wish to inform members of the Working Group that we, as Co Chairs, will hold a meeting on 19 November in coordination with the Executive Office of the Secretary General, to hear directly from UN system entities on their experiences with mandate implementation and to exchange views on how the review can be made more meaningful and impactful. In particular, we will focus on strengthening UN system engagement and exploring how analytical work can best support the deliberations of this Working Group. We also plan to travel to Geneva and Vienna in early January to undertake further consultations with Member States and UN system entities there. So it is now my honour to offer the floor to our briefers, who will provide additional answers to Questions posed at the 30 October briefing on Mandate implementation. First, I give the floor to Mr. Guy Ryder, under Secretary General, for Policy to further elaborate on the answers provided to Member States in the form of visual illustrations and follow up to the many questions raised during the recent briefing on mandate implementation. Floor is yours. Thank you.

**UN Secretariat** | Guy Ryder, USG Policy
In the first instance, Ambassador Schwalger, Ambassador Wallace, thank you for this opportunity to be one of the briefers in today's session of the Working Group. And more than that, thank you for the extraordinary leadership you're providing in the work of the Working Group. And I think the attendance in the room this morning is evidence of the very strong engagement of Member States in the important work you are carrying out. Now. Let me say that it is important that we do continue to work together on the discussion of the overall life cycle approach to mandates that we are basing our work upon. Focusing today, of course, most particularly on the delivery stage of the life cycle. Do I have graphics on the screen? I do. Can I give you some perhaps context to the discussion today? As has been mentioned by Ambassador Wallace, we shared some written responses to the questions raised at the session of 30th of October. You have received them and I don't think it would be a useful use of your time to repeat what is in those responses. So instead I can perhaps use this time to highlight what seemed to us to be some of the key points that we shared and provide just a little bit of context in some areas that may be of relevance. I want to begin by underlining why the work that is being done here is so critically important, why it is essential that we streamline and strengthen the UN system's mandate delivery. If we take a look at our resource situation, you look at the graphic ahead of us. It's not a new graphic. You can see that we are projecting that resources across the entirety of of the United nations systems might well shrink by as much as 25% in 2026 next year compared to the levels of 2024. And I think that provides a very clear message to us that we simply must focus on optimizing processes, eliminating duplications, if we are to continue to serve the people who need our assistance most. Let me then turn to some more specific aspects in relation to mandate delivery, which is the focus of today's session. The first point that I could perhaps make relates to reports. Let me again draw your attention to two statistics, two numbers that we've already shared, but is perhaps worth lingering on again. If you look at this graphic, you will see that a total of 66% of reports cover recurring topics. Topics have come on, come back again and again, and 46% of our reports are produced either annually or more frequently than annually. So if you look at the red boxes here, you will see that those two boxes up to 46%, sort of. In very plain terms, this means that we are spending considerable time and resources writing reports on the same or similar issues in successive years. And of course, in many cases, this is absolutely necessary and appropriate. We understand that annual updates are often appropriate and necessary, but that does not or should not disguise the possibility that there may be opportunities to optimize the format, the length or the periodicity of certain reports. I cannot claim that the statistics before you are conclusive, but they are certainly suggestive. On the other hand, and in some instances, to preserve both Member States bandwidth and respect, that and the Secretariat's capacity, it might be worth considering whether some reports could shift to a biannual or even longer cycle of publication. That's reports. Let's move on to funding issues. Colleagues, on funding, the graphic before you shows that the UN system's work relies on very heavily on voluntary contributions. Roughly 80% of our total resources are from voluntary contributions, and therefore only 20 are derived from assessed funding of any kind. This also shows that the UN Secretariat accounts for only a relatively small share of overall system resources. Most UN system funding, and therefore most delivery, takes place outside the Secretariat across funds, programs and specialized agencies. And this level of voluntary funding dependence means that the way that voluntary funding is provided its predictability, its flexibility, or its earmarking has a major impact on how effectively we can deliver on the mandates that you hand to us. The other dimension of this consideration concerns the size of the grant packages, the funding packages that we get. I think it is noteworthy that 63% of our grants are for amounts below $1 million. This funding comes in small packages. A large share of our funding is delivered in these many small transactions, and each of these naturally carries with it its own administrative requirements. And inevitably, the consequence is of a high administrative cost per individual grant compared with larger grants, consolidated contributions. This is a situation which raises the level of overhead costs. In addition, in past years there has been declining support for pooled funds. Less than 10% of voluntary funding came through pooled funds in development and humanitarian action. And that is far below the target established in the Funding Compact, which was a target of 30%. The target was 30%. The reality is 10%. And here too is an area where it would be extremely valuable if this Working group could take a closer look at remedial action. May I? Also, without going beyond that, I'm conscious of the need not to do so beyond the boundaries of the work of of this Working Group. Draw your attention to proposals which are included in Work Stream 3, the report on Workstream 3 that was published by the Secretary General shortly before High Level Week, which relate to this topic. For your reference, they are in paragraphs 97 to 101 of the Secretary General's Report on Workstream 3. I think you will notice from this that there is a clear area this is a clear area where Work Streams two and three do converge. In our Work Stream two report, we have identified specific challenges to mandate delivery, and in Work Stream 3, we try to identify specific avenues to address the challenges in terms of our structures and our programs. In the meeting on the 30th of October, several of you asked how we can address issues of fragmentation in our delivery. Issues of fragmentation and duplication have been recurring issues in our Work Stream 3 report. To reference it again, we provided some avenues to address fragmentation, such as scaling up common services, creating a single entry point for macroeconomic analysis, and establishing joint knowledge hubs. But generally speaking, we need to assess where different UN entities have a comparative advantage and how they can work together to leverage those comparative advantages to best effect. And one way to approach that challenge is to look at what particular function an entity fulfills. In the graphic before you, we list some of the big categories of function, normative support, policy advice, data and statistics, capacity building, convening, service delivery and support, and other functions. What we are trying to get to through this lens is where each part of the UN system can deliver on the aspect of a mandate that it is best equipped to handle with clear divisions of labor, which would help to eliminate dangers of duplication. If that sounds rather abstract, let me just try to give an example to illustrate the point. It's an example taken simply because it does illustrate the point, rather than for any other reason, the issue of global vaccination program. So the WHO provides the normative and technical guidance and policy advice, setting the global standards for childhood vaccinations, and working with governments on how to translate global vaccination goals, international public health plans. That's one function. Gavi, not strictly part of the UN system, supports the funding function, and UNICEF in turn focuses on support functions such as procurement to vaccine delivery on the ground, working with your national health authorities to reach families and children to deliver the vaccinations. And here you see what might at first sight look like duplication is in fact a very efficient and complementary division along functional complementary functional lines. In this way, UN entities play Complementary, not duplicative, roles, ensuring that together we can deliver the greatest possible impact. So, co chairs, those were some of the additional clarifications that I wanted to share. I hope they're helpful to you and of course I look forward to listening to your statements and to our continued conversations around the UN systems Mandate delivery. Thank you very much.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you. Very helpful. Thank you. Under Secretary General Ryder, I now give the floor to His Excellency Christian Venavesa, Permanent Representative of Liechtenstein. We're particularly interested in hearing from you on which measures you would prioritise to improve mandate impact and any thoughts you might have on reporting, given that came up very frequently and the mandate implementation briefing and of course, any of the other questions that were posed. The floor is yours.

**LIE** | Christian Wenaweser, PR
Great. Thank you so much. It's very good to be back. And again, big thanks to you for steering this process in such a structured and transparent way. I think people know that the questions asked were mostly asked to the Secretariat and that will not take up that much time for that reason. And you've already heard extremely helpful replies from Guy Ryder and I will focus on on what Carolyn has just mentioned. In particular, measures to improve impact and efficiency across the system. As far as mandates, as you know from my briefing, I strongly believe that we should put more efforts into crafting our mandates very carefully. And I think that would be my biggest wish and my most important suggestion. So there has to be consultation. There have to be consultations, not just among ourselves states, but also with the relevant parts of the Secretariat, which honestly will help us to just understand what we are doing and what we are proposing. And in particular, if we are proposing something that is perhaps already done or in part already done. So we do believe that a consultation on substance, but also on procedure and on whether something is legally sound is extremely important to craft good mandates and to also have an understanding of what the implementation of a mandate will cost. So we believe every resolution should be costed and that we should, before making a decision on how we express ourselves on a proposed new mandate, that we know the financial aspect of a decision. Linked to this is the question of reporting that you have also flagged. Carolyn, as you know, I think the first question for us should be do we need a report? Because we do not believe that every resolution does need a report. The report can be extremely important and can be big value added. But that is not the case always. And right now, I think mentally we are in a sort of space of automaticity. You have a resolution, so you need an SG report. So I think we need to rethink that and look at this, at this differently. Then also think about, well, perhaps it's good to have a report at the beginning to frame a conversation and to give us a real basis to take a topic forward. But maybe as we move ahead in the delivery of a mandate, and Guy has made it very clear in his comments how many reports are on regularly recurring topics and maybe the moment will come where the membership has sufficient information or is sufficiently served with being provided additional information and things that have changed. There are in fact reports that consist mainly of submissions by Member States. These reports tend to be extremely duplicative and repetitive. Sometimes they're very lengthy. These are probably reports, we don't need it all anymore. But again, it should be something that we look at as proponents of mandates, but also as those who are consulted and as part of the negotiations. The periodicity is also something that is best looked at at the very beginning if you propose a new mandate. I think, again, this sort of reflex to say we do this every year because I think it's important is not necessarily, I mean, I don't think that is actually accurate. I think it can have an extremely important mandate that is, that is discussed only three or five years. And that is something we should think about when we, when we draft our mandates. And this is linked also a little bit on impact, but also on the question, what are we doing here? Is this forward looking, is this backward looking? So certainly we would hope that we will have a much better toolbox for ourselves as we move ahead in our work, as we move forward, as we start crafting new mandates, as of course we will and as we have to, and that we implement the measures that are being discussed here, but that will also allow us to do it with already existing mandates because they typically come back. So if we embrace a new way of doing this and if we embrace new best practices, of course that can then be applied also, also to mandates as they, as they come back and as we resubmit or renew them. So that is probably it for me. And thanks for having me. It's a great pleasure to offer my comments.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thank you. That's really helpful as well. We now give the floor to Mr. Martin Kamani, President and Chief Executive Officer of the Africa center and former Permanent Representative of Kenya, to respond to the question posed to him during the briefing on mandate implementation. And for your memory, that question read, to what extent, extent can the UN utilize AI for information or knowledge management and what is needed to become future ready? And Martin of course, you're very welcome to share any brief reactions to any of the other questions posed as well. Floor is yours.

**[Speaker Unknown]**
Thank you very much. VR and Board Chairs, thank you for inviting me once again to deliberate with you on this important topic for this important working group. You asked to what extent the UN can utilize AI for information and knowledge management and what is needed for the organization to become future ready, and I will draw on the remarks that I shared on 30 October which outlined the UN structural knowledge problem and the cost it imposes on implementation. As I briefed you then, the UN is rich in knowledge, yet staff and delegations rarely have that knowledge at the moment of decision. This weakens implementation, slows crisis response, and forces the system to relearn what it already knows. AI cannot replace political judgment, conflict, understanding or diplomacy, but it can remove the friction that consumes staff time and attention. And this is where the opportunity lies. A significant share of UN work is mechanical and rule based. Administrative processing, financial workflows, indicator management, translation support, document retrieval, data cleaning, structured summaries and reference preparation. These processes can be automated quite safely. AI can also assemble inputs for higher level analysis. It can retrieve resolutions, extract factual timelines, surface comparable cases and collate open source reporting. These are repeatable tasks that do not require political sensitivity. In fact, when Kenya was in the Security Council, we had a machine reading team in Nairobi that was doing just that, open source work that helped us a great deal in working on files where Kenya did not have a credible intelligence gathering system. Automation in these areas expands the usable bandwidth of staff who must apply judgment. It reduces the administrative burden that currently constrains the UN's ability to deliver and takes up so much of the financial resources. The Political Call the UN must remain human. Drafting the Secretary General's reports, the political analysis, the mandate interpretation that Christian was talking about, the conflict assessment, early warning, judgment negotiations, mapping of red lines, the way narratives are framed in complex and crisis situations. These depend on contextual understanding and sensitivity to member state positions. They require tacit knowledge acquired through experience and AI cannot perform these functions. So the aim I was talking about last time, and as I repeat now, is reinforcement of political work, not its substitution. Automation strengthens the UN core mandate in three ways. First, deeper analysis. Analysts gain more time for political consultation, reading and scenario testing when no longer required to reconstruct timelines or search for documents. Early warning improves when staff focus on incentives, power dynamics and emerging risks. Second, faster and clearer decisions. Decision cycles accelerate when automated systems produce timelines, comparable cases, legal references, etc. Thirdly, diplomacy strengthens, negotiation improves when envoys have more time for bilateral consultations and political outreach. Automated retrieval, past commitments, voting patterns and red lines frees you to have the energy to shape outcomes. And especially this point is relevant to the smallest missions that struggle with the present workload. So automation protects the space for human political judgment and therefore increases both delegations and Secretariat's capacity without expanding headcount. We also have to respond to the new cyber threat environment. The AI enabled cyber espionage campaign that was documented by Anthropic, which runs Claude AI. If you use it. This mid September, just a few weeks ago, marked a turning point. For the first time it showed a state sponsored actor using an AI model to conduct reconnaissance, exploitation and essentially to undertake the attack at scale with minimal human oversight. The operational cost for adversaries has dropped while the speed and automation of the attacks has increased. The UN is exposed. Its digital environment is fragmented, it's unevenly secured and it's filled with sensitive political and humanitarian data. The risks are theft and contamination of its knowledge systems, disruption of mission communications, and of course, erosion of trust in its analysis. So what's a realistic UN response? I think that requires three actions as I close. One, the UN has to harden its systems. It has to establish a single minimum security standard across the Secretariat funds and programs. And it has to focus on practical steps that reduce fragmentation in the UN's digital environment. This is achievable with the SG's direction and your support financially in the fifth committee. Second, it needs AI assisted threat detection. And third, it needs support to Member States. The UN should offer simple, cost effective cyber resilience packages to LDCs and fragile states. These should include basic protection measures, guidance on secure digital services and help with incident response. The UN cannot defend countries against every cyber threat, but it can raise the minimum level of security for those with the least resources. And I highly recommend this to the LDCs and other developing states. In terms of its workforce implications and the realistic constraints. We know that AI will transform global labor markets. We know that it will transform education systems and industrial strategies. If the UN development system remains centered on project cycles and reporting, it will not meet what Member States need. The UN will need more policy expertise in economics, education reform, labor market design, industrial transformation and digital governance that is aware and expert on AI. And AI can support these experts with modeling and evidence synthesis. The UN cannot compete with industry for elite AI engineers or cyber specialists, but it can bring in targeted expertise through second men's partnerships and short term consultancies. It can also go further. It can build a fellowship, something that we can call an AI core or some other name, which is a structured global fellowship program that would bring engineers, alignment researchers and cyber AI specialists into the UN for a period of time. They would work on secure architecture, digital public goods and knowledge systems. In return, they gain exposure to context they would never otherwise encounter. I meet so many young people who would love to work at the un. Give them that opportunity in the AI realm and I'm sure some top talent will come for some period of time. This model would be realistic if it's funded by member states, philanthropies and universities perhaps, and it supplements political and development staff rather than replacing them. So in closing, what's a future ready AI? In quick point form? It treats knowledge infrastructure as core institutional infrastructure. It automates rule based and repetitive workflows. It protects political functions as entirely human. It modernizes cybersecurity to confront AI enabled threats. It adjusts workforce composition towards technical and analytical expertise. It establishes a standing experimentation unit with the authority to test and iterate. It anchors regional pilots in Africa where most nexus contexts lie. It publishes evidence on gains in search, retrieval speed, analysis time and decision speed. In conclusion, AI will not solve political disagreements or compensate for unclear mandates. It will not replace diplomacy, conflict analysis or negotiation. But it can remove mechanical burdens that limit the UN's capacity to act. It can expand the time available for human judgment which is at the heart of conflict prevention and crisis response. It can harden the organizations against the new threat landscape and strengthen its service to member states. I think you have the chance to build a UN that can do all these things that I have spoken about and I encourage you in the working group to recommend a piloting of these approaches as soon as possible. Measure the effect and scale what the evidence supports. Thank you.

**IAHWG** | Carolyn Schwalger, Co-Chair
Thanks Martin. You've deeply thought about these issues and I really appreciate, and I'm sure Brian does too, the clarity of the recommendations you've put in front of us as well. We'd now like to invite Ms. Amirah Hark, former United Nations Under Secretary General for the Department of Field Support Support, to provide an answer to a couple of questions that were in her field. One of those questions relates to how can system wide coordination mechanisms such as the ceb, un, sdg, IASC be used to improve coherent mandate implementation? And the other question is what drives so many entities to spread themselves so thin across so many mandates? And how can better coordination reduce competition for resources? And of course you're also welcome to share any brief reactions to other questions posed. The floor is yours.

**[Speaker Unknown]**
Thank you very much. In response to the first question, in terms of system wide coordination mechanisms, I mean, there is absolutely, I think, no debate whatsoever on the need for coordination mechanisms. But I think where we need to investigate the time, the transactional costs that are incurred in these mechanisms is the key question. So coordination mechanisms absolutely necessary at the level of CEB and the UNSDG and iasc. But I think when Guy was speaking, I, you know, noted that in the implementation he had written that, you know, we need to focus constantly on improving people's lives. And you know, I can, I can sort of think of if we start each one of these meetings, numerous meetings and other mechanisms that we have in terms of saying, is the time spent, is the time that we're going to spend on this meeting. What is the direct correlation of the time spent in this meeting to actually making an impact and improving someone's lives? I think, you know, that kind of cross sectional analysis is very good in terms of, you know, how much time are we spending, to what effect and what are the actual results. The second thing with respect to these coordination mechanisms and which I observed in the field, particularly when I was in the field in these coordination roles, is that what is discussed and agreed at headquarters doesn't necessarily find its way down to the field representatives. So sometimes we have a situation where I think headquarters and the policy level feels quite, you know, satisfied that yes, we have made these kind of agreements and, you know, certain policies are in place. But I think when you go down to the field level, sometimes at the field, the same kind of discussions which have already been agreed to at headquarters are taking place at the field level. So there is that duplicative nature and that the decisions of headquarters not being transmitted in, I would have to say, in a rather forceful way to representatives on the ground to adhere to what has already been agreed. And so there is a lot of repetitiveness and there's a lot of time spent again at the field level. The third in terms of coordination mechanism is also the host government's role that the UN does not do this coordination, whether it's at the CEB or the UNSDG or iasc in isolation it is working. It is in a host government. And likewise there has to be a parallel mechanism at the host country level that accepts this. So Guy made a very good example of Health and gavi. But very often we would see that a ministry of, say, Maternal and Child Health doesn't necessarily, you know, adhere to something that has come from the planning ministry. And so even at the host government level, there is sometimes a reluctance to come together, to assume a kind of coordination or a leadership maybe of one technical ministry. So the UN in its coordination mechanisms has to have that translated at a host government, host nation level as well. And that also proves one of the challenges of this system wide coordination. And the last point that I would make on this is that member states who are providing the funding are also, you know, I think, bear some responsibility in terms of this fragmentation. And, you know, I was struck by, by the number of grants that are below $1 million, because whether a grant is $1 million or below $1 million or $20 million or more, you know, the same level of administrative and other things, you know, Ambassador Kimani talked about, maybe AI can, you know, can take some of these, but, you know, it still requires the same amount of work because the accountabilities that are set in for a project or a program that is below $1 million is the same accountability of something that is $20 million. So there again is where the transactional costs of these kinds of coordination mechanisms I think, have just gone way beyond, as I said, if we measure, what do all these levels of reports, meetings, discussions and everything else translate actually into improving people's lives? So that is my few words on the system wide coordination. As I said, it is absolutely necessary, but I think it can be streamlined. I think it can be more disciplined. I think there can be more, you know, reaching out to both member states who are providing funding and to the host nations where we are working to also embrace and, you know, try and work within these mechanisms in their respective sides. The second question was, what drives so many entities to spread themselves so thin across many mandates? And how can better coordination reduce competition for resources? And when I read that question, I said, you know, the answer is actually in the, in the question itself because all entities, all UN entities are competing for limited number, limited amount of resources. And that is why sometimes if again, to take the example of the health sector, but, you know, it applies equally if someone is doing the implementation and the, you know, the procurement, for example, of vaccines and all the, you know, equipment and other things that are required for it. But if the, you know, the sort of the supply chain in terms of a policy within the government is not there, it's very difficult for the procurement to happen in isolation. And so what happens is there is a tendency then for someone who wants to make sure that the vaccines are implemented and that go right down to the deep field where all this is so essential, they automatically want to get involved also in the policy, in the funding. So it's very difficult. Even though we may have specializations, it's very difficult to kind of separate that. And the other thing that is so evident in the chart that was put up on the voluntary funding and the assessment funding is that, particularly when I was in the peacekeeping and peace operations, a lot of what came in the assist contributions should have been passed on to UN agencies who have the expertise and the experience and the knowledge of the country, you know, where they have been working for a long time. And a lot could, could be done there. It was, I think I mentioned that it was very, very difficult. It is very difficult still to have that kind of passage between voluntary and assessed fundings again, you know, with, with having the chapeau of is this improving people's lives. But what happens is that we get into more thinking about silos and accountabilities and being able to deliver on something in order to be able to attract resources. So again, here I feel there are responsibilities, and in this context, I would say more with member states who are providing the funding. In the PC peacekeeping world that I was in, there was a total disconnect between those members of the foreign affairs ministries who were working to prepare the implementation and for example, the ministries of planning or development, and others who needed to look at early recovery. So sometimes we had, in different parts of the UN almost at the same time, resolutions of funding decisions that were taking that were, that were not coordinated within that member state itself. And I think that's where, you know, a lot of improvement in, within the coordination mechanisms and this ability to have each entity spread themselves so thin, thinking that, okay, if I have this in this program, it'll attract a little more money from this member state, because that's one of their country's priorities, too. And so you kind of, you're fitting what you're going to do based on where you might align yourself with a member state's priorities, other than looking at, again, that central thing of improving people's lives where we are present. So I think it's difficult to see one element of a mandate in isolation and also the accountability sometimes become very difficult. And I think that's why so many entities are spreading themselves thin just in order to be able to capitalize on the very limited resources that are available for this particular issue. So thank you very much. I'll leave it at that.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Thank you, Carolyn. I now open the floor for Working Group members to share their views on mandate implementation. We continue to look forward to hearing your ideas and proposals to make mandate implementation more efficient, effective and impactful. As usual, delegations are asked to keep their interventions to five minutes for groups and three minutes for individual delegations, and I see already quite a number of delegations who are interested to speak, so we're going to ask you to be disciplined on maintaining the time. I now give the floor to the distinguished representative of Iraq on behalf of the Group of 77 and China

**IRQ**, Representative
Excellencies Co Chairs. Good morning. I have the honor to deliver this statement on behalf of the group of 77 and Chan at the House of the Group wishes to express its appreciation to the Co Chairs for convening this important discussion and for the informative briefing held on 30th October which provided valuable insights into this important topic. The Group further extends its gratitude to the panelists for their insightful presentations and valuable contributions. The Group emphasizes that mandates achieve impact only through effective implementation, often requiring concerted efforts across multiple entities and areas of work. Reflecting the Organization's multidimensional and cross cutting nature, the Group reaffirms that the overarching goal of the UNAT Initiative is to make the United nations more agile, resilient and capable of delivering high quality and efficient services while eliminating redundancy and overlap in mandate implementation. The Group notes proposals for a common administrative platform and merging several of ongoing entities, departments, offices under the UNAT Initiative, emphasizing that any such process should enhance mandates implementation and service delivery while remaining fully guided by intergovernmental oversight and the authority of Member States. The Group believes that the successful implementation of mandates requires translating general aspirations into specific, measurable outcomes with clearly defined objectives. It is therefore essential to engage closely with with the Secretariat, whose role should remain supportive in nature on the modalities of implementation while reaffirming the prerogative of Member States to define objectives and expected outcomes. Co Chairs the Group recognized that artificial intelligence tools may offer advantages in identifying potential duplicative mandates and overlapping implementation across mandating bodies. However, their use must be approached with caution given risks related to privacy, security, and the sensitive nature of intergovernmental work, as well as concerns about accuracy and transparency. Any consideration of such tools should therefore ensure adequate safeguards and that budgetary implications remain imaginable in the current challenging financial context. The Group considers it important to receive comprehensive information on the intergovernmental bodies that have established and overseen the implementation of specific mandates, including updates on the status of implementation and the underlining reasons for any non or partial implementation. Such information would contribute to a clearer understanding of challenges and facilitate more informed discussions among Member States. The Group also underscores the importance of ensuring that the Secretariat maintains a neutral and balanced role in mandate implementation. It is essential that all entities act strictly in accordance with mandates approved by Member States. The Group further calls for mandate implementation to be measurable and supported by a system wide evaluation mechanism. A key component of this effort is the need to link mandate decisions with projected costs and to identify opportunities to enhance efficiency, coherence and accountability throughout the implementation process. Co Chairs to conclude, the Group of 77 and China retreats a strong commitment to engage constructively in this process. We emphasize that discussions to mandate implementation or mandate implementation must remain inclusive, transparent and efficient, ensuring full oversight and participation by all Member States. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank the G77 and China. I give the floor now to the European Union.

**EU**, Representative
Thank you, Mr. Co Chair and dear colleagues. I delivered this statement on behalf of the EU and its Member States. The candidate countries, Turkey, North Macedonia, Montenegro, Ukraine, the Republic of Moldova, Bosnia and Herzegovina and Georgia, as well as Armenia, Monaco and San Marino, align themselves with this statement. We thank the Co Chairs for your remarkable coordination of this Working Group. We also would like to thank the Secretariat for the responses provided and for issuing a structured action plan to further consolidate the work on UN80 and establish a system wide framework for action. On today's topic of mandate implementation, allow me to highlight the following three points to address the proliferation of meetings and reports. The EU calls for a system wide review to reduce duplication and create efficiencies, limiting meetings and reports in number, length and frequency. Take for example the First Committee, which managed to finish its work earlier than scheduled this year. We encourage the streamlining and rationalization of mandated meetings and reporting requests, as well as any measures aimed at reducing repetition across organs and bodies and resources. We fully support the proposal to produce shorter reports and to combine them wherever possible. In this regard, the work on revitalization of the General assembly and its Committee, as well as reform efforts across other bodies, takes on added significance. 2 We recognize that misaligned funding modalities and resource fragmentation constitute a serious challenge for the effective implementation of UN mandates, especially in times of liquidity crisis. Therefore, we support granting flexibility to the Secretariat for the allocation of resources across programs and regions when justified by operational priorities or crisis, and following the implementation of accountability frameworks regulated by clear criteria. The EU also underlines the importance of applying the mutual commitments as agreed in the Funding Compact to strengthen the predictability, quality and transparency of financial contributions and the third point on the division of labor among UN entities. This must be further clarified to avoid unnecessary duplication and functional overlaps. To enhance coordination and information exchange, we support the strategic use of resources through existing system wide coordination platforms and the enhancement of interagency coordination to effectively manage mandate delivery. Also in the field, the integration of open, transparent and trustworthy use of AI into the mandate registry to showcase substantial similarities, potential duplications, risks and overlaps is also something that we support. We also welcome the integration of digital technologies aimed at modernizing the UN system through shared data platforms, digital dashboards and analytics, paired with human oversight and clear accountability frameworks. Lastly, we also support a more adaptive approach to mandate implementation, implementation based on agility, exchange of best practices and measurable impact. Implementation plans should allow for adjustments in methods, timelines or resource allocation in response to evolving contexts and evidence of impact. Dear Colleagues, the UN AD initiative demands more than compartmentalized efforts. It requires an ambitious and truly system wide approach across the Secretariat, organs, committees, agencies and Member States in order to ensure that reform outputs directly translate into more coherent, cost effective, impactful and field responsive operations. Work streams may advance in parallel, but they are not isolated from one another and therefore we must make sure that they mutually inform each other. We also encourage continuous coordination between our discussions on the mandate implementation review and other processes, including GA revitalization efforts, ongoing GA Committee work, and the review of working methods and information exchanges between the Secretariat and Member States. In their capacities as members of the Boards of agencies, funds and programs, the European Union and its Member States stand ready to continue to contribute constructively to the next steps and I thank you.

We just have to have the courage to find the key and use it and not be afraid of the fact that across various geographical groupings, ideological groupings, other divisions which exist among us, there's actually more consensus among us about the changes that we could make if we wanted to make them. Because unless the UN becomes more effective and impactful, it will become less relevant and we're the only ones who can actually change it. **SGP**, Representative
Good morning colleagues. Thank you very much. Co chairs, it's always a challenge to speak after Ambassador Bob Ray. We will miss his wisdom and eloquence and we wish him all the best. Au reboire. As we say to each other, colleagues, Co chairs, I have the honor to deliver this statement on behalf of the Small States Group. A cross regional grouping of small states comprising both developed and developing countries and a list of 53 countries have aligned with the statement. And for the sake of efficient time management, we will make available the list of countries later to the Co Chairs Co Chairs the mandates are essentially an expression of the will and priorities of Member States. Every mandate is a political commitment established with the expectation that it will be fully implemented. Mandates should therefore be drafted in an informed and intentional manner and clearly articulated so that meaningful impact can be achieved through effective delivery. Allow me to make three points on how mandate implementation can be improved. First, strong knowledge management and effective use of technology can play an important enabling role in successful mandate implementation. Improved data systems will allow Member States and the Secretariat to better fulfill their respective roles in requesting for and implementing mandate delivery. For instance, enhanced data systems could include the capacity to view the status of mandate implementation and the entity that is responsible for implementation. Broadly speaking, the establishment of a comprehensive registry would inform users of areas for potential streamlining, which can then be tabled as proposals for consideration by Member States. While the creation of a robust registry will require time and resources, it will be a powerful tool to reduce duplications. Second, the creation and implementation of UN activities and reports would benefit from greater coordination. This is part of a necessary shift in mindset and culture. We should seek to incentivise a stronger sense of shared ownership and collective responsibility over the mandate life cycle. Member States should be open to new configurations of mandated products or modalities of mandate delivery. The aim is to achieve mandated objectives through more efficient and measurable outcomes. As for reports, Member States should empower the Secretariat to produce more concise and useful reports, with a focus on content and analysis that contribute to impactful mandate delivery. Thirdly, the Secretariat and UN entities must address fragmentations in mandate delivery and strengthen internal coordination as an ongoing practice. These two entails a shift in organizational mindset and culture. A number of factors contribute to the problem of duplication and inefficiency, which include different parts of the system working in silos and competition for resources between agencies. Stronger internal coordination and division of labor must be embedded as a matter of course in the process of mandate implementation and delivery management within the UN. We note the Secretariat's proposal in the UN 80 Initiative Action Plan and welcome further efforts by the Secretariat to improve internal coordination and streamlining of mandate delivery. Co Chairs, small States have always been consistent supporters of the UN and the multilateral system. At the same time, we are also among the most affected when the system becomes overburdened. We reiterate our strong commitment to engaging constructively with all Member States and the Secretariat to ensure that the process of mandate implementation is more coordinated, accountable and efficient so as to meet our common objective of effective mandate delivery Co Chairs, Please allow me to make a few additional points in my national capacity.

**SGP**, Representative
First, I want to thank the briefers for their very insightful opening remarks. I found them very useful and and I hope that this will give us additional material for reflection. Second, I agree with our colleague from Liechtenstein that fundamentally, implementation has to be related to the question of how mandates are crafted in the first place. The greater the clarity and specificity, the better it will be for all of us in terms of measuring and monitoring the implementation of mandates. In this regard, I think there is a case to be made to look at the length of resolutions. A longer and more voluminous resolution does not necessarily make the mandate broader or deeper. It may well end up providing very vague and confusing instructions or guidelines, not just to the Secretariat but also to other Member States. It is worth thinking, in my view, of a standardized template for resolutions that States can use as a guide as they begin to craft mandates, especially for resolutions that are new. Ultimately, of course, states would have the sovereign rights and the option to craft their own mandates. But I think it is time that we make the mindset mindset shift to look at shorter and more succinct resolutions, aided hopefully by the idea of a guiding of some guidelines or templates. The second point that I wanted to make in my national capacity has to do with the question of periodicity. This has come up quite regularly. I think we need to make a distinction between activity and accomplishment. So the greater frequency of activity does not mean that we are accelerating accomplishment or mandate implementation. We can adopt a resolution every six months. That does not mean that we are accelerating the implementation of a mandate or making a difference to improving people's lives in the field. So that's the first point that I wanted to make with regard to this larger point about periodicity. Second, we need to make a distinction between an agenda item, a resolution or a draft decision, and reports of the UN Secretary General. An agenda item can be discussed annually, but a resolution need not be adopted annually. And a report of the Secretary General, even if there is a request for such a report, need not be done annually. So let me offer the example of the revitalization we have co chairs here present with us this morning. We look at there's an agenda item on revitalization that is discussed annually, but the resolution is adopted biannually. So it's entirely possible that an item is placed on the agenda, and that's the decision of the General Committee that looks at the list of the agenda item. An item is placed on the agenda possibly annually, but we don't need a resolution to be adopted annually. It could be done biannually or triannually. And we don't need a report of the SG on every agenda item on an annual basis. It could be done biannually, triennially or even beyond. So I really think the data presented by the Secretariat Under Secretary General Guy Ryder about how we spend 10% of our budget on writing reports is not just financially not sustainable. It is simply not possible for any of us to read all the reports that we ourselves demand. So I hope to have further discussions with all Member States on these important issues. Once again, thank you Co Chairs for your leadership.

**BRB**, Representative
Co Chairs. I'd also like to extend our thanks to the Briefers for their valuable and in some cases thought provoking insights. It's also worth noting, I think, Co Chairs colleagues, that yesterday's discussion in the General assembly on the revitalization of the General assembly highlighted the important convergence between that process and this process. We must use this convergence to generate synergies. In discussing mandate implementation. We are dealing with a stage of the mandate life cycle where Member State decisions must translate into coherent action. This is where clarity, coordination and adequate resources matter the most. For small missions, including those small island developing States such as those of caricom, predictability in how mandates are carried out is essential to effective oversight and to our ability to participate meaningfully. Across the breadth of the UN's work. CO Chair's implementation occurs within a wide and complex institutional landscape. The diversity of entities involved reflects the multidimensional character of global challenges, yet it can also result in uneven fragmented responsibilities in delivery and overlapping activities. CARICOM sees merit in strengthening internal coordination and ensuring that roles are clearly communicated and understood. When entities work to their respective strengths, mandate delivery becomes more coherent, more efficient and ultimately more impactful. We further recognize that reporting forms a significant part of implementation. Reports serve as a key tool for transparency and oversight and they must continue to provide Member States with information that is relevant, clear and analytically useful. CARICOM supports efforts that focus on enhancing the quality and value of reports while ensuring that any adjustments remain firmly rooted in intergovernmental guidance. Co Chair's colleagues Funding modalities also shape implementation in profound ways. The continued reliance on highly earmarked voluntary contributions, combined with the limited flexibility of assessed resources creates challenges for planning and coherence. CARICOM reiterates a long standing principle mandates must be supported by resources that are predictable and proportionate to the tasks required. Bridging the gap between expectations and available resources is fundamental to the credibility of the organization and to the confidence of Member States in this regard. It is critical also for CARICOM that the important and meaningful work being undertaken in the implementation of mandates in the field in our countries and regions and the capacity to do so sustainably does not fall victim to this exercise. What this stage of the discussion underscores is the importance of an organizational approach that promotes coherence, reduces unnecessary duplication and strengthens accountability. These are not technical issues alone as they go to the very core of how the United nations delivers on the priorities that Member States have collectively agreed. Co Chairs CARICOM remains committed to engaging constructively as we move through this process. Our interest is in a mandate implementation environment that is transparent, coordinative, coordinated and responsive one that respects intergovernment, the intergovernmental nature of the mandates while enabling the system to operate effectively and one that supports the ability of small delegations to follow and contribute to the work of the organization in a meaningful way. We thank you Co Chairs for your continued leadership and look forward to contributing to the forthcoming discussions on Mandate review, completing our examination of the life cycle in a way that strengthens the ability of the UN to deliver for all and with your permission I would like to offer one brief comment in my national capacity and it connects to the points underlined in particular by former Undersecretary General Amirah Haq. It is Barbados View that we must pay particular attention in the framing of mandates to their on the ground in the field implementation. It is here as she so powerfully underlined, that the lives of the people we represent can truly be touched. I thank you Co Chairs.

**SLB**, Representative
Thank you Co Chairs. I'm honored to speak on behalf of the Pacific Island Forum members based in New York. This is especially critical for small island developing states, including those in the Pacific, which depend heavily on the UN for support, expertise and development financing. As others have said, how well we implement agreed mandates is a key measure of the UN's performance and of us as member states. Unfortunately, current weaknesses in the mandate implementation, such as poor coordination, too many priorities and lack of restraint, are felt mostly strongly by seeds, including the Pacific Island Forum states. The UN development system is fragmented and lacks coherence on the ground, which limits its ability to deliver for seats and makes it harder for us to access essential assistance. Co Chairs the growing number of resolutions, processes, meetings and report is challenging for all, but it can overwhelm smaller delegations who are supported by small policy teams in their capitals. Fifth, missions are displeased about the recurring overlap between the work of the main committees. For example, earlier this week, on Wednesday, the General assembly plenary held a secret ballot to fill one vacancy at the International Court of Justice. At the same time, recorded votes were held both in the second and the fourth Committees. We are mindful that overlaps can never be entirely prevented. However, it is extremely difficult for small delegations to be in three places at once. There are clear steps we can take to ease these burdens. One, we as Member States should be more disciplined in the meetings we mandate. Fewer, shorter and more focused meetings in core priorities should be outboard. Second, we should only request reports when they serve a clear purpose and justify their cost. Reports should be clear, concise and focused on the key information needed for decision making. Pasig Island Forum countries are especially concerned about the fragmentation of activities and resources across the event system. We urge the Secretariat to continue improving coordination and coherence, especially in the field, so that scarce resources go to activities with real impact rather than duplicated programs or administrative overhead. Co Chairs Coordination mechanisms should strengthen UN impact, not add more bureaucracy. Member states also have a role in reducing duplication and fragmentation. We encourage donors, UN entities and development partners to align their support with regional, national and local priorities and to work through existing plans and coordination mechanisms. Finally, Co Chairs we join others in calling for more stable and flexible financing for the UN system. Uncertainty around voluntary funding and rigid funding models make planning difficult, waste resources, and force UN leadership to spend more time fundraising than implementing mandates. The current liquidity crisis is also paralyzing mandate implementation. We repeat our call for all Member States to pay their assist contributions on time in full and without conditions. We look forward to working closely with all members throughout this process and I thank you for Chairs, I thank the Solomon Islands.

I thank Kuwait. I give the floor now to Australia.

**AUS**, Representative
On behalf of Cairns. Thank you very much indeed, Chair. And I thank the briefers who've contributed this morning. And let me just say thank you so much indeed, Bob, for your final remarks. I think the notion that we are indeed the masters of a fair degree of our own destiny and that we don't need to be prisoners of established processes really is the critical reflection we should take away from this morning. I speak of course, on behalf of Canada, New Zealand and Australia. And as the Secretary General states in the Mandate Implementation Review, mandate delivery is the basis on which Member States and the wider public assess the value of the United nations system. It's critical to the implementation of the United nations three pillars, and we need absolutely to get this process right. Many of the recommendations on mandate delivery sit with the Secretariat and we encourage the Secretary General to implement these without delay. But Member States must also play their part. We must recognise that the United nations mandates and operating model have become unsustainable due to our own actions. By mandating thousands of activities in multiple settings, we have left United nations entities spread too thin and promoted duplication and fragmentation of effort across the United nations system. And we have to recognize that that is in very much part our own responsibility. We need to ensure that we're using UN resources in a coordinated and effective manner. Firstly, we need to show restraint in the activities we mandate. This means being mindful of the costs associated with requesting reports, both commissioning new ones and continuing recurring ones, without clear justification. And this point has already been made several times this morning. We encourage the Secretariat to implement recommendations, to shorten reports, combine reports covering similar issues and explore alternative formats, to present information more concisely and to decrease the burden of reporting and improve overall accessibility. We must also exercise restraint in mandating other activities that have resources. Implications for the United nations budget and at a minimum, be clear on the implications, both monetary and staffing, of delivering the mandate. This includes the proliferation of high level meetings and the agreement to limit the number of such meetings due during High Level Week is a useful start, but we all know that we have started that many times before. We suggest we next consider limiting the number of high level meetings, summits and conferences that are held throughout the year. The forthcoming moratorium on new international days is another useful step, although every one of us in this room knows full well that there's a current rush to approve new ones before the moratorium comes into place. Secondly, where mandates with financial implications are agreed, we need to ensure that the United nations has the financial means to implement it. And to this end, it's essential that Member States pay their assessed contributions in full, on time and without conditions. Our final point is on coherence and coordination, which is a shared responsibility between Member States and the Secretariat. Member States need to take a more coherent approach in our engagement across different multilateral settings and be more aware when we are proposing a mandate that already exists creating duplication across the system. The Secretariat also has a critical role to play in addressing duplication and fragmentation. We encourage the Secretary General to implement recommendations on enhanced strategic oversight and transparency over the United nations system to achieve a better division of labour and allocation of resources to deliver on mandates. We also need to strengthen Member State mechanisms for coordinating our work in the General Assembly. Existing mechanisms such as the General Committee and the Committee on Program Coordination, they are not delivering. We need an honest conversation about how we can do better. Mr. Kamani's briefing this morning highlighted the problem of there being no shared implementation picture across the United Nations. And don't we know that well? We need better centralised knowledge shared between the Secretariat and all Member States to enable us to to better track mandate implementation. Achieving more coherent and integrated mandate delivery across the UN system needs to be a key criterion for considering proposals for structural reform and program realignment under Work Stream 3. We want to see a clearer relationship between effective mandate delivery and any proposed structural reform to enable list. Fundamentally, we want a modern, functional, forward looking organisation with the capacity to deliver high quality results. And the above proposals, which cover both Member State and Secretariat responsibilities, in our view form a series of concrete actions that we can all take towards achieving this. Thank you.

**CHN**, Representative
coutures China aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of G77 and China. In light of the Secretary General's report and other briefings, I wish to share the following points. First, internal coordination within the Secretariat should be strengthened. The Action Plan for advancing the UN 80 initiative submitted by the Secretary General will help ensure that the UN 80 initiative is implemented in a more substantive and effective manner which China fully supports the action plan, proposes measures to enhance senior management coordination. China looks forward to detailed recommendations in this regard. The UN system comprises more than 140 entities, each of which has its own areas of expertise. We hope that all entities will focus on their core mandates, leverage their competitive strength, minimize duplication of efforts and make the best possible use of the limited resources. Second, meetings and reports should be concrete and concise. There is a broad consensus on the need for the improvement of meetings and reports. China suggests we focus on the following three aspects. First is to adopt a swift, proactive, programmatic and effective working style focusing on improving people's lives. Meetings should start on time and statements and reports should go straight to the points and avoid lengthy and repetitive discussions. Second is to enhance the quality of meetings and reports. The Secretariat has already proposed ideas such as adapting reports formats, enhanced data representation and focus on key issues. We look forward to to detail the plans to translate these ideas into actions and practical proposals for improving meeting and report quality. At the same time, China stress that the importance and value of the UN's report it has provided tremendous insights to the governments, academias, NGOs across the board. Third is to streamline the number of meetings and reports. The Secretariat has established a work package on Secretary General's reports. We hope this mechanism will view review reports with similar content and release time and make specific recommendations for their streamlining. The Secretariat could also provide specific recommendations to Member States on combining meetings, shortening the length of meeting sessions and reducing reviews frequency with a view to achieving tangible results. Third, on funding. Voluntary contributions are an important supplement to the UN's work, but the assessed contributions are the foundation. As the second largest contributor to the UN regular budget, China has always actively fulfilled its financial obligations. We have already fully paid our assessed contributions this year, a total of US$680 million, and we will continue to firmly support the UN and multilateralism through concrete actions. At the same time, we urge the largest contributor to fulfill its own financial obligations. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair

**CHL**, Representative
Thank you, Co Chairs. Chile aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of the group of 77 and China and with Philippines on behalf of MICS. I also would like to express my appreciation to both of you, the Co Chairs for the organization of the latest briefing and to the panelists for sharing their experiences. An effective and coherent implementation of mandates across the United nations system is fundamental to ensuring that the organizations remain strong agile and credible. For this reason, the implementation stage of the mandate life cycle must be further reinforced and continuously improved. Chile recognized the various challenges that persist during the mandate delivery phase of the life cycle, notably the risks of duplication, misaligned funding modalities, and the excessive volume and duration of meetings and reports, which often outweigh their actual impact. As a former President of ecosoc, I would like to share an example from our work on the 2024 HLPF Ministerial Declaration, which one negotiated must be submitted in parallel to both the General assembly and ECOSOC for adoption in 2024. The submission of an amendment to a vote in the HLPF and the subsequent adoption of the amendment declaration created a lack of clarity on the way forward, as previous practice suggested that the ECOSOC should consider two original text. After initially doing so and following extensive consultations, I ultimately decided to submit to ECOSOC the amended version of the Declaration in order to avoid ending up with two different versions of the same Ministerial Declaration. This example illustrates how procedural arrangement can lead to duplications requiring additional time and resources to resolve matters that should ideally follow a streamlined process. The coordination segment of the ECOSOC itself is a good platform to strengthen the coordination capacity within the Sustainable Development Pillar. The new ECOSOC revision should be coordinated with this work of this Working Group as well. Another challenge lights in the current financial situation of the organization, which continues to erode the capacity of UN entities to fully implement the mandate entrusted to them. These entities face an increasingly difficult environment in this sense, as spending reduction targets further constrain their ability to execute their approved budgets. Decisive and collective action is therefore needed to reverse this trend. Let's turn the funding challenge into an opportunity because, as Brazil mentioned, because even if we had all the money available, we will still need to do this work the way we have been doing its it is losing meaning for the people we are meant to serve. In conclusion, I would like to reiterate our support for the Co Chairs and reaffirm Chile's commitment to engaging constructively in the work of this Working Group. Thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank Chile. I give the floor to Switzerland and then the Netherlands.

**CHE**, Representative
Co Chairs, colleagues, Switzerland thanks you for your continued leadership. During our tenure as an elected member of the UN Security Council, Switzerland led efforts to establish a resolution on the protection of humanitarian personnel. During the course of the consultation, we reached out to the Secretariat sharing wording suggestions and we were advised that in this particular instance it was preferred preferable to opt for a requirement of regular oral briefings of senior UN officials rather than a regular written report. We took this on board and this helped us to devise the mandate accordingly and go into the direction of effective mandate delivery. This example illustrates what previous briefings have shown in more detail, which is that well crafted mandates are key enablers for effective implementation. Co Chairs allow me to make three points. First, we would like to have clearer understanding of what the Secretary General can already do under his or her own authority, especially to improve delivery management. For example, in a quote, making better use of system wide coordination platforms to avoid duplication, end of quote, such as the Interagency Standing Committee or the CEBU are measures that we believe the SG can already act upon today. Second, regarding meetings and reports, Switzerland supports efforts to reduce their number. Reports should be clearer, shorter, more focused and wherever feasible, reports could be combined. We also encourage the use of different formats such as short update reports instead of lengthy annual documents. In our own system, Swiss diplomats across the world have been instructed to privilege shorter reporting and analysis formats for their daily work, as opposed to more detailed reports at specific situations and for lesser frequency. There is no doubt that we can learn and get inspiration from how the 192 other diplomatic representations and services represented here deal with these very same challenges of information overload, acceleration, limited staffing and so on. Switzerland is also open to exploring technical solutions. Could artificial intelligence tool under human supervision be used to cross check reports for redundancies or provide cross references to related reports? Given the successful experience of setting up the mandate Registry, how about improving the technical interface for reporting by creating an online space that allows for user friendly access to all participants products of existing mandates, taking into consideration multilingualism and accessibility for all. Switzerland, including with our academic partners, stands ready to share its experience in this regard and we take good note of suggestions made earlier, including by Ambassador Kimani and others. Third, and finally, real impact in mandate delivery depends on coordination and tapping into synergies. Efficient mandate delivery requires entities to share knowledge and work hand in hand across UN hubs. Take human rights. The Human Rights Council in Geneva and the General assembly here in New York must operate in lockstep. We gain valuable insights from the Council mandates implementation that are of relevance for our work here. So let's break down silos not only thematically but also geographically. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
