**IRN**, Representative
Thank you, Madam Chair. At the outset we thank you for convening this meeting which provides an opportunity to exchange views on enhancing the delivery and coherence of United Nations Mandates the Islamic Republic of Iran aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of Group 77 and China. We recognize that the proposals on the demanded delivery aims at enhancing efficiency and effectiveness through streamlining the meetings and reporting process, make funding mechanism more impactful and less fragmentation and enhanced coherence and coordination. We attach great importance to ensuring that the implementation of mandates is fully aligned with nationally defined development priorities. Such alignment is essential to achieve tangible and sustainable results that respond to the realities and aspirations of Member States and offer greater likelihood of success in this regard. We believe that the successful implementation of mandates must be accompanied by systematic investment in institutional and human capacity in developing countries. Any reduction in resources could constrain training programs, capacity building initiatives and the transfer of technology, thereby weakening our collective ability to address global challenges effectively. The role of regional and sub regional structures is also indispensable for ensuring responsiveness and inclusiveness in mandate delivery. The preservation and adequate resourcing of regional commissions and field offices are vital to enable them to respond effectively to regional priorities and specific local contexts. Downsizing or reducing staff in these offices would risk eroding institutional memory and limiting the ability of developing countries to access timely and equitable support from the UN system. Further, we stressed that the Secretariat should maintain a neutral and balanced role in implementing mandates and supporting Member States in achieving their objectives. We also underscore that the UN entities must operate within the parameters set by Member States, adhering strictly to intergovernmentally agreed language and avoiding any perception of Secretariat driven initiatives. We believe this approach is essential for preserving the credibility and technical integrity of Secretariat's work. Finally, we encourage enhanced transparency and regular update on the status of mandate implementation, including clear explanations for any non or partial implementation. We believe that mandates should be treated fairly and for the benefit of the entire membership. Comprehensive information sharing will enable Member States to identify gaps, improve coordination and ensure that the organization's work remains guided by the collective priorities of all members in an efficient and effective manner. Thank you, Madam Chair.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank the distinguished representative of Iran. And I now give the floor to the distinguished representative of Mexico, to be followed by Argentina.

**MEX**, Representative
Thank you for giving me the floor, Madam Chair. Mexico expresses its gratitude to the Co Chairs for their dedicated leadership and reference its support for the proposals. But forward in the Secretary's General Report, Co Chairs allow me to outline four proposals Mexico believes are relevant for our collective exchange on the implementation phase of the mandate life cycle. First, breaking counterproductive practices. Our long standing practice of layering new directions upon existing ones without allowing Time to fully implement or assess them has generated an accumulation of tasks, overlapping duplications and bottlenecks. A prudent cooling off period would enable mandates to be properly implemented before new decisions are introduced on the symptomatic area. This will help restore coherence and prevent the continuous pileup of paragraphs, requests and reporting lines that ultimately hinder the purpose they seek to advance. Second, a forward looking approach Implementation should not be conceived in isolation from evaluation. A preventive system wide mindset, one that anticipates how a mandate will be assessed, must guide the design and implementation of activities. Ensuring alignment between objectives, indicators and institutional capacities is the only way to secure relevance, pertinence and meaningful impact. 3. Strengthening coherence Improving implementation requires system wide solutions. We propose developing a centralized and transparent architecture for mandate implementation. A dedicated implementation section in the UN Mandate Source Registry should be established to enhance accounting interpretability, prevent unintentional duplication and enable evidence based decision making. It would allow identifying the responsible entity, expected deliverables, timelines, resource requirements and potential overlap with existing tasks. At the organizational level, the Committee on Program and Coordination CPC is the bridge between mandates and programs. It is the institutional body specifically designed to ensure coherence and coordination during implementation. Strengthening and modernizing the CPC must be central component of the UN reform. This requires elevating the level and quality of its deliberations and outcomes and enhancing its visibility according to its responsibility. Fourth, and lastly, clarity and continuity. Persistent inconsistencies in the interpretation of mandates, combined with dispersed institutional memory and internal coordination across the system, have undermined coherent implementation among agencies. The Secretariat should be equipped and authorized to provide formal system wide interpretative guidance on mandates, especially when discrepancies or ambiguities arise. A strengthened duty to warm Mechanism will be enabled to Secretariat to alert Member States when a mandate contradicts an existing instruction, exceeds available capacities or risk duplication. I thank you, Madam Chair. I thank the distinguished representative of Mexico and I now give the floor to the distinguished representative of Argentina to be followed by ourselves.

**PRY**, PR
Good morning to everyone. My delegation wishes to express its recognition, appreciation and full support for the work carried out by you both, Brian and Carolyn, as a co facilitator, we truly value your constructive efforts in this process. Turning to step two on Monday Delivery, we consider this discussion essential to strengthening the United nations capacity to deliver effectively and currently on hinge mandates, we took note of the three main challenges that continue to hinder mandate delivery and reduce our collective impact according to the Secretary General report. First, we are really worried as a small mission, as many other delegations in this room, about the growing number of meeting and length reports. We are really worried this situation has created an administrative burden that often outweigh the benefits. Many reports are not only long but also repetitive and rarely consult to tackle. This preparation is key and in that sense we suggest establishing a system that identifies which meeting reports at a greatest strategic value, suspend or merge those that are redundant and optimize report format with clear length limits. Once again, we support the secure and responsible use of artificial intelligence to help summarize, translate and link related reports, making information more accessible and cost efficient. Second, regarding the management of mandate delivery, stating that there is still a lack of clarity in how responsibilities are divided which lead to duplication and efficiency. According to point number 41 of the report, existing oversight in the United nations system focuses mainly on program planning, budgets and operational issues of individual United nations entities in isolation, rather than seeking to ensure strategic alignment across the system to drive impact and efficiency. To overcome this, we support the idea of having a centralized oversight mechanism within the Secretariat to monitor coherence across man mandates and we see value in establishing permanent interagency coordination platform to ensure better collaboration across entities. Third, with regard the misaligned funding modalities. We encourage the proposed solution to fully apply all commitments by Member States and the United nations system through the founding compact and strengthened dialogue. To this end couture, we need to be pragmatic and we believe this practical step could make mandate delivery more current, efficient and aligned with our shared vision of a stronger multilateral system, one that delivers real results for people and communities on the ground. And finally, we wish to underline the need for greater and better better coordination with the Secretariat so that its perspective and concerns are heard and seriously considered. Especially since the implementation of mandate relies heavily on their daily work. Thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank the distinguished Permanent Representative of Paraguay. And I now give the floor to the distinguished representative of the Russian Federation, to be followed by the United States. United States.

**RUS**, Representative
Thank you, Madam Chair. I would like to say that we support the practical steps to improve coordination and efficiency in the delivery of mandates. Existing interagency platforms can be used more effectively to reduce duplication while respecting the independent and mandates of each entity and division of labor. I think a lot of delegations speak to this end today. At the same time, Russia cannot agree with proposals that would expand the Secretariat's autonomy in prioritizing or streamlining mandated activities. Decisions of priority must remain with the Member States. Financial operational considerations cannot justify the modification, delay or non implementation of mandates adopted by intergovernmental organs. I would echo what Mr. Ray said about the money and this position. To all questions, the answer is money is as old as our Bible, the book of Ecclesiastes. But still we think that we should not allow, as they say, the tail wag the dog. And the Member States have to finally determine what they want and then decide how to provide for it. We are concerned by suggestions to to shorten, merge or reprioritize mandated reports. We totally agree we should have shorter resolutions, but we don't agree we should have shorter reports. Reporting is essential accountability and transparency tool and must remain comprehensive, accurate and accessible. Moreover, the UN 80 is an initiative which is a good opportunity to instruct the UN Secretary General to improve the quality of the Secretary General reports by addressing the issue of methodology of report preparation. In particular, we are very concerned about the sources of information which become, so to say, legitimized by the Secretary General reports. And of course, these improvements must go hand in hand with enhanced methodology for the Secretariat's self evaluation of mandate delivery, which needs to be reflected. And special attention should be given to the sources of information as well as separate review of mandates that are delivered at the cost of voluntary contributions, especially with the information that we have reconfirmed today by Guy Ryder by the amount of voluntary contributions. Proposals to align or redeploy resources internally without the approval of the General assembly would undermine the budgetary prerogatives of the member states. Russia also stresses that we cannot support the reference in the Secretary General reports to the Resident Coordinator system as a successful tool of strengthening internal oversight of the UN division of labor. I think that there are a lot of challenges to this so called successes. Thank you very much.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair

**USA**, Representative
Thank you Co chairs and colleagues. I'm inspired by the remarks of our distinguished colleague who's retiring, the permanent representative from Canada. I think his remarks were spot on and in the spirit of honoring that and being very inspired by what he said, I'm going to throw out most of what I wrote here because it's duplicative of what many of you, almost all of you have said here this morning. Not everyone. Some people like long reports. Some people like overlapping mandates, Some people like spending money on paper in meetings instead of impact on the ground. But I would say that if we took a poll amongst this room, reforming how we write and implement mandates would have more popularity than ice cream. And ice cream is pretty popular. So I'm going to throw out my remarks and make one general point and two specific points. I submit. We submit that efficient and effective delivery and measurable impact should be at the heart of everything we do in our work here at the United Nations. And it's worth noting that in 2024 alone we spent, the United nations spent $360 million on the direct costs, meetings and reports. Let me repeat that in case it wasn't clear. $360 million not on maintaining and promoting international peace and security or providing life saving aid, but on meetings and reports. And These reports are 40% longer than they were 20 years ago. Now if anyone can show me that we are 40% more useful, insightful and actionable because of these reports, are we achieving better, faster or smarter decisions because of these 40% increase in the reports? We don't need four hour meetings and lots of talking in the halls. Let's get this going and we stand ready to support and help Co Chairs in anything you need to put this into action. Thank you so much for your hard work.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair

**HRV**, Representative
Implementation and we would also like to commend your leadership in steering this important process. Croatia aligns itself with the statement delivered by the European Union on behalf of its Member States and I would like to add a few remarks in my national capacity. We welcome the continued focus on mandate implementation as noted in the previous meeting and reflected in the Secretary General's report on Stream 2. Three key concerns the proliferation of meetings and reports, the inadequate management of mandate delivery and misaligned funding modalities. We share these concerns. However, we wish to emphasize that many of these issues stem from the way mandates are created. As we stressed previously, mandates must be concise, clear and purpose driven to ensure effective implementation. Mandates should also be flexible enough to allow the Secretary General and the UN management to allocate responsibility to in the most efficient manner without micromanagement from Member States. In this period, we underline four key points. First, clarity of scope and objectives. Mandates should establish measurable goals aligned with strategic priorities to reduce overlap and sharpen focus. 2. Integrated delivery frameworks we support the SGE's proposal for common administrative platforms and consolidated functions to unify efforts and reduce fragmentation across UN entities. 3. Enhanced field level Coordination Coordination must improve not only at the country level but also regionally to ensure mandates are context sensitive and impactful. Data driven monitoring and robust metrics and real time reporting will support adaptive implementation. 4. Flexible funding with accountability Member States should uphold the Funding Compact and enable the Secretariat to redeploy resources swiftly, especially to safeguard essential country level delivery. This flexibility must be paired with accountability and transparent justification. In conclusion, effective mandate implementation begins with thoughtful mandate design. By ensuring mandates are strategic, streamlined and supported by flexible systems, we can empower the UN to deliver with greater coherence and impact. Thank you.

## Session 6: Continued Review Discussion (Nov 14 Part 2, 2025)
Video: /video/k1q/k1q3djbdxu

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
Colleagues, can I ask you to take your seats as I call to order this meeting of the informal Ad hoc Working Group on Mandate Implementation Review. We will resume where we Left off at 1 o' clock today with the remaining list of speakers, and I will begin by giving the floor to the representative of Costa Rica.

**CRI**, Representative
Thank you very much. Dear co facilitators, Co chairs, I'm sorry. It's great to see you coordinating our works and congratulations from my delegation for all the work you have done so far. Excellencies, Dear distinguished colleagues, we are operating in a context of growing financial pressures that affect the predictability required for effective mandate delivery. This reality underscores the need to strengthen responsible financing, governance and administration so that the United nations can continue to fulfill the mandates entrusted to it. Mandate implementation is where credibility is tested. Allow me to highlight three points and four possible step forwards. First, the reporting burden continues to expand, largely because we Member States continue to request new reports. Costa Rica supports a careful review of periodicities, duplications and mandates whose reporting requirements may be no longer necessary. The goal is to improve the quality, usefulness and timeliness of reporting. While each committee has its own dynamics, more deliberate planning and sequencing of meeting time between bureaus and the Secretariat can support more effective workload management, acknowledging that only part of the cost structure is truly adjustable. Second, a more coherent United nations requires stronger system wide coordination. Clarifying the division of labor on the basis of comparative advantage can reduce overlap and increase impact. For Costa Rica, this must be guided by transparent and more inclusive intergovernmental oversight across the UN system as a whole, particularly in the funds and programs and within the governance of their executive boards. Coordination efforts should respect established governance arrangements while also identifying areas where those arrangements could be refined to support more coherent planning and delivery. Third, mandate implementation, budgeting and liquidity are inseparable. Persistent cash constraints require responsible forms of flexibility within existing governance frameworks. Better internal reprogramming, a better a more strategic use of existing reserve and more coherent resource planning across related clusters can help sustain operations and reduce fragmentation. Pool or joint mechanisms may also help reduce structural competition for resources. Looking ahead, Costa Rica supports first, a focused mandate implementation dialogue, a more comprehensive one and a continuous one. Second, evidence based reviews of reporting cycles. Third, assessments of the cost implications of different reporting and meeting models, modalities and fourth, an annual exchange with the Secretariat to better align mandates, capacities and resources. Costa Rica remains committed to a United nations that is coherent, efficient and capable of delivering the results that the international community expects. I thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Costa Rica. I give the floor now to Indonesia and then we will have Germany, Algeria, Kyrgyzstan, Senegal and Norway. Indonesia, you have the floor.

**IDN**, Representative
Thank you. Co Chairs Indonesia extends appreciation to you for the inclusive, transparent and constructive engagement in the continuation of this process. We also thank all briefers for the responses and also for the presentation last week. Today's discussion on mandate implementation is among the utmost importance of the overall process. This is the point where decisions taken are translated into real change on the ground. It is where expectations must turn into action and where the true effectiveness of the United nations is ultimately measured. Allow us to share our two key reflections on today's topic. First, on the design of the reports. From the last briefing, we noted a persistent pattern that a significant portion of reports are recurring, yet those do not always yield new or meaningful values. Indonesia believes that future reports must shift from repetition to relevance and offering deeper insights in a more comprehensive and measurable way. In this regard, we are of the view that areas of improvement could be applied to the report designs, such as maintaining a similar structure of reports on targeted focus with updated contents in its issuance of reports, that this will enable us to track progress and identify gaps. Meanwhile, emerging issues could be highlighted in a separate chapter with brief details. Second, on the issue of overlapping mandate citations, while entities might cite similar mandates, a similar citation does not necessarily mean similar implementation in practice. Entities may apply the same mandate to different regions, constituencies or operational contexts. We therefore need a mechanism that traces the end use of these mandates, clarifying what activities are undertaken, where they occur and with what concrete impacts. The function parameters presented in the response to the last briefing, ranging from normative support and policy advice to data capacity building and service delivery, could be a starting point as practical tools to map complementarities and reduce unnecessary duplication. Co Chairs to conclude, rest assured that Indonesia stands ready to engage constructively to ensure mandate implementation remains purposeful, coherent and impactful. Thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Indonesia. Give the floor now to Germany and then Algeria.

**DEU**, Representative
Co Chairs Colleagues, Germany fully aligns with the statement made by the EU this morning. We thank you again, Co Chairs, for your leadership and for organizing the very insightful briefing on mandate implementation. Building on what we learned from our great briefers, I would like to make three points in my national capacity. First, on reports and meetings. As others have already outlined, we also think that we need to address the proliferation of meetings and reports. Since 1990, the amount of reports has increased by about 20%. Why not, as a first step, cut the comparable 20% and get back to the level of 9090 when it comes to the amount of reports. Secondly, on internal oversight, one important task is to address the poor division of labor within the UN system that can lead to duplication and also to the misallocation of funds. We should strengthen the internal strategic planning and where appropriate, accompanied by independent internal and external oversight. The goal should be to ensure that UN entities only work on implementation of those mandates for which they have tangible comparative advantages and of course, in a well coordinated manner. Thirdly, and lastly, Co Chairs, let me make a broader point on the UNH spirit. Germany very much appreciates the deliberations and the exchange in this Working Group under the able leadership of you, dear Co Chairs. If we want the reform to be successful, we as Member States need to fully embrace the Unit spirit. Also, outside this room and this Working Group, the current Committee season provides the perfect opportunity to do so. For instance, in the First Committee, France and Germany made some proposals how to make the work in that Committee more efficient. Specific ideas include reduced time for statements and meetings and only submitting draft resolutions if there is a substantial change. That's just one example. I'm sure other delegations and other committees might come up with even better and bolder ideas. But that kind of practical application could in turn feed back into this room and this Working Group. By that, we, the Member States, could fully embrace the UNAT spirit in our everyday Committee work and of course, continue our work here in the Working Group, aiming at high ambitious results under your leadership. I thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Germany for its statement. I give the floor now to Algeria, followed by Kyrgyzstan.

**DZA**, Representative
Thank you for giving me the floor. Algeria aligns itself with the statement delivered by Iraq on behalf of G77 and China and welcomes the opportunity to engage constructively in the ongoing discussions on unity. We share the collective aspiration for a United nations that is more coherent, more coordinated and more responsive. In this regard, we have taken note of proposals that could allow the Secretariat to set or adjust priorities on the basis of internal consideration. We wish to reiterate that the strategic direction of the Organization is determined exclusively by Member States. Mandates, whether political, peacekeeping, humanitarian or development related, cannot be recalibrated due to financial constraints or operational pressures. Their implementation is not discretionary, nor can it be delayed or reshaped outside of intergovernmental decisions. We also recognize the value of enhancing efficiency and fostering innovation in reporting and resolution making. However, these efforts must not weaken Member States ability to exercise their work. What is required is improved analytical depth, clearer methodologies for the preparation of reports, more rigorous self evaluation by the Secretariat and increased transparency. We need to make the best of the accumulated institutional knowledge that the organization has gathered through its 80 years of existence. Furthermore, reforms that affect Africa must be undertaken in close consultation with African states, the African Union, and regional organizations that hold direct responsibilities in peace, security and development on the continent. Inclusion and partnership are indispensable for a credible and sustainable reform process. In this regard, we caution against any measures that could negatively impact peace, security and development in Africa, and recall that the United nations bears a historical responsibility toward the continent, one that this reform process must uphold and strengthen, not depart from. Finally, we underscore the importance of Excuse me, we underscore the importance of maintaining coherence across all UNH work streams. Decisions taken in one area, whether structural, operational or budgetary, inevitably affect mandate implementation in others. To avoid fragmented or unintended outcomes, the process must be considered and managed holistically, with a full appreciation of its interdependences. Thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Algeria for her statement. I give the floor now to Kyrgyzstan and then Senegal.

**KGZ**, Representative
Thank you. Co Chairs Excellencies. Let me begin by thanking the Co Chairs for convening this important meeting and for their continued efforts to advance the reform of the United Nations. Today, the United nations stand at a decisive moment. The UNAT reform must be more than an administrative exercise. It must help the organization act faster, work smarter and deliver better Results. With the UN system resources expected to decrease by almost 1/4 by 2026, we cannot afford excessive bureaucracy. We must focus on what truly effective, coordinated and accountable implementation of mandates. Kyrgyzstan believes that the success of reform depends on our ability to to strengthen cooperation among UN aid entities. We need to reduce duplication, improve coordination, and ensure that every mandate has clear objectives and measurable impact. The UN should move from fragmented and parallel efforts toward a more integrated and complementary approach, especially across the peace, development and humanitarian pillars. Financing remains a central challenge. Kyrgyzstan supports proposals to better align new mandates with realistic financial resources. Efficiency means not doing more with less, but doing better with what we have in a focused and measurable way. We must also make reporting a tool for management, not bureaucracy. Too many reports are rarely used. We need shorter, more digital and analytical reports that help decision making and strengthen accountability. Kyrgyzstan also wishes to highlight the situation of small and developing countries. Many of us with limited staff and small permanent missions face challenges in following numerous meetings and processes. Full engagement of all member states, regardless of size or capacity, is essential for a fair and representative United Nations. At the heart of these reforms must remain the human dimension. The real measure of UN success lies in its ability to prevent conflict, build peace and include people. In these efforts, we emphasize preventive diplomacy, regional cooperation and inclusive peace building that fully engage women and youth as agents of stability and resilience. Co Chairs we stand at a historic crossroads. The choice is ours to remain constrained by outdated procedures or to rise as a force for decisive and principled actions. Kyrgyzstan calls and all Member States to show political will, vision and courage so that the unity reform becomes a true turnpoint leading to an organization that acts swiftly, clearly and in service of all people. I thank you.

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I thank Kyrgyzstan for the statement. I give the floor now to Senegal to be followed by Norway.

**SEN**, Representative
Thank you. Dear Co Chair Dear Co Chairs, the delegation of Senegal aligns itself with the statements delivered by Iraq and Singapore respectively on behalf of the G77 and China and a small States group and would like to share the following points on its national capacity. My delegation thanks the Under Secretary General Ryder for his constant engagement to ensure ownership of this process by Member States and commend you Co Chairs for your efforts to create this opportunity of information and dialogue. You can continue to count on our full support. Firstly, my delegation encouraged efforts in terms of mandate monitoring tools, particularly the development of register of mandates, putting its and artificial intelligence to better contribution. This kind of registry should help us have that global perspective of existing mandates and of primary responsible entities with a view to identify obsolete or dormant mandates, overlaps or duplicates prior to the generation of new mandates. This mandate inventory should also serve to a better identification of implementing bodies, clarification of the rules and mission of entities involved in the implementation of cross cutting mandates, assessment of necessary budgetary and human resources, as well as advancing possibilities for financing through existing budgetary lines. In current programs about financing, the payment of assets contribution in due time remains critical for well implemented mandates. Nonetheless, this process can also nourish our reflections on how to defragment the resources available within the organization, including through the fungibility of voluntary contribution. Secondly, in line with the steps taken through the United Nations General assembly resolution 793 to 7 on revitalization of the work of the General assembly, formulation of mandates should be strengthened through concise, action oriented and time bound resolutions. This kind of approach shall form part of a revitalized coordination between the Secretariat mandated entities, Member State and other stakeholders with a view to discuss in a timely manner ways to create synergies between similar mandates and deciding on adjustments, mergers or elimination as appropriate. In this respect, the review of periodicity, length and format of reports should be considered in a way to give sufficient and efficient time to stakeholders to implement the mandates to take stock of the difficulties of implementation and to provide prospects for improvement and amendments. Finally, the issue of the implementation of mandates speaks to their cycle of life from creation to termination. One part of our challenge is also to give meaning to what that termination would be, whether through a plain and simple conclusion or through a form of continuation via another vehicle of action. By doing that, we will also help reconcile competing and overlapping lines of actions. I thank you.

**NOR**, Representative
Thank you, thank you, thank you co chairs and thank you to the briefers for today. Norway particularly appreciates the clarity of the mandate delivery chapter on the SD report that informs our discussions today. Given that we're already into the afternoon session, I'll just focus on one particular aspect on reporting. We strongly support the Secretary General's proposals and prerogatives to prioritise and streamline requests and mandates for reports or meetings, recognizing the urgent need to modernise how the UN designs, manages and delivers data and information. As we heard this morning, the current culture of extensive reports to documentation and frequent meetings consume significant resources both for those producing them and for us, the Member States that receive them. With more than half of all active mandates requiring reports, most of them recurring and unevenly read, we should use this as an opportunity to step back and rethink how we as Member States are best served in terms of information sharing and accountability. Could we envision a future UN without formal reports instead of moving to digital systems, perhaps through an integrated UN digital platform? One offering a new kind of reporting with up to date multilingual access to visualized data which presents concise information and analysis rather than static written yearly updates? Could it perhaps also provide a snapshot of progress made on mandate implementation, including associated budgets from across the UN system? A new kind of digital visualization of information and analysis could be a valuable tool to enable evidence based decision making when we come to mandate renewals and hopefully improve coordination across UN entities. We welcome other concrete ideas that were presented by colleagues this morning, like the standard templates for drafting mandates as suggested by Singapore. Or does it explore further ideas like cooling off periods for mandates as suggested by Mexico? We also, in closing, wanted to echo the comments from the UK this morning on the importance of core funding, another key challenge outlined in the SG's report. Norway will expand on these ideas and others in our written inputs. Thank you.

Any reforms should ultimately serve the goal of better mandates implementation and service outcomes. Member States should steer the goal setting and assessment process with Secretariat providing supportive input rather than a directive role. Transparency is vital to identify board necklace and driving evidence based improvements. Finally, we insist that all entities adhere strictly to the mandates adopted by Member States, ensuring impartiality and compliance with agreed parameters. ## Session 7: Final Review and Next Steps (Nov 25, 2025)
Video: /video/k1q/k1q2ukeao9

**IAHWG**, Co-Chair
I call to order the informal meeting of the Working Group to hear a technical briefing on mandate implementation. We warmly welcome all of you to this technical briefing. We recognise the presence of Mr. Guy Ryder, under Secretary General for Policy and chair of the UN 80 taskforce. Mr. Locke Bahadur Thapa, Permanent Representative of Nepal to the United nations and President of the Economic and Social Council Ms. Inga Anderson, Executive Director of the United Nations Environment Program Ms. Ilse Branz Kheras, Assistant Secretary General for human rights and Ms. Claudia Bans, director of the Security Council Affairs Division. Before handing the floor to our briefers, please, please allow me to deliver some opening remarks on behalf of the co Chairs. We warmly thank Member States for your continued engagement in this important process. The briefings we convened on mandate creation and implementation provided new important data analysis, information and real world examples to Working Group members. Your questions to the briefers have so far unearthed further insights and your remarks at our two consultations have been thoughtful and have given us some good ideas that we have carefully noted. We hope that today's briefing on mandate review will be just as fruitful. Our briefers will discuss how UN mandates are reviewed across the UN system and how successful, coordinated and useful or not these are. They will of course also elaborate on the relationship between mandate creation, implementation and review from their perspectives. At our briefings and consultations, as well as in our meetings with Working Group members, we have consistently heard agreement among the membership that reform is urgently needed. We are at risk of losing the confidence of both our publics and political decision makers. And to build this back, we must restore the UN's ability to deliver across its its core mandates. We have heard about the clear through line between mandate creation and mandate implementation. But as you all know, and as many of you have mentioned in your statements, this should not be the end of the story. Without any review mechanisms, we will not be able to accurately assess how we are delivering against the objectives we set ourselves, nor the impact our decisions have on the lives of our constituents. The information and analysis that mandate reviews can uncover should play an important role in driving the work of this organisation. Being able to deliver on our undertakings and course correct where necessary, will naturally strengthen the UN's legitimacy. As we said last time, we have a responsibility to our citizens and to each other to implement the things we agree to here, the things our public expect of us to deliver for them and for others. Equally, review mechanisms should allow us to see clearly where we have been successful. When we have completed our work, we should be able to retire mandates. Indeed, ending Mandates in this way is maybe the highest measure of success. We have certain parts of the system have experience in reviewing, recalibrating and completing mandates that we hope to learn from today. It is for this reason that we encourage working group members to keep an open mind to the analysis they hear today and to be ambitious in the ideas they bring in the coming weeks. Without this, our ability to achieve change at the scale needed to improve mandate implementation may be compromised. I now pass the floor to Ambassador Wallace to outline the way ahead and to introduce our briefers.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Good morning. Today's briefing will be followed by consultations on 3 December, where working group members will again have the opportunity to offer their perspectives and ideas for consideration, as well as to ask further questions. We apologize for the short turnaround, but we trust in your understanding of the challenging timelines and constraints we face in this and other processes and remain confident that you are up to the task. As with our prior briefings, the briefers today have been selected for their experience with mandate implementation. They will present from different perspectives for us to all consider, and there will be an opportunity for questions from the floor once they have all presented. Like all of us here today, they are invested in the good functioning of the UN. With that, I give the floor to Mr. Guy Ryder, under Secretary General for Policy and Chair of the UNAT Task Force.

**UN Secretariat** | Guy Ryder, USG Policy
Thank you very much indeed. Ambassador Wallace, Ambassador Schwalger. Good morning, Excellencies, ladies and gentlemen. Well, thank you for this new opportunity to speak with you, to introduce the third stage of this mandate life cycle, the stage that we commonly refer to as the mandate review stage. And perhaps just to situate this discussion in the overall context of this process, we could look at this when it's up. Diagram of the mandate life cycle. I wonder if we can show it. Next one. There we are. This is just to remind us that we're in a mandate life cycle approach, which goes from the. The creation of mandates, how you adopt resolutions, the second stage of delivery, how the UN system implements mandates by turning them into activities to be undertaken across all pillars of the organization. That's the delivery phase. And then what we're coming on to now, crucially, the third phase, in which member states come back to look at whether the work that the UN system has undertaken is indeed achieving the results intended and whether mandates remain relevant or whether they need to be adjusted. And I think one way of looking at this review phase of the cycle is to see it as a sort of a steering operation where member states have the opportunity to set the forward trajectory of the organization and its activities. And if we're able to do that well, the entire mandate cycle can stay on course, moving us where we want to get to towards greater positive impact. And alternatively, when it's not done so well, I think we can all see that there is a danger that the system can enter into a sort of drift, and we want to avoid that. Now, as we see it, for a mandate review process to work optimally, two conditions reflected in the graphic before you would need to be met. Firstly, Member States would need to have available to them a review process that allows them to steer strategically, not simply automatically to repeat discussions year after year as a sort of a. A default position. But that's only one half of the story. The other half is that the UN system, our responsibility is to provide information that you need to assess the work that we have undertaken. You need to have access to clear plans, clear results, and a presentation of the links between resource utilization and outcomes obtained. So we believe these two components would be integral to a successful review phase. Now, perhaps I can go through each of these components in turn, beginning with the Member State review process. Now, the question we might ask is what are the means at the disposal of Member States to provide the type of steer that the organization requires? Obviously, the resolutions that you adopt are the most important and obvious instrument at your disposal. But there is more than that because you shape the organization's direction also by deciding what goes on the agenda, how often issues come back for discussion, the kinds of reports that you want and how frequently you want them. And then all of this comes on top of the resolutions that you ultimately adopt. All of this indicates that you Member States are in the pilot seat throughout this entire process. You set the course and the UN system must follow your direction and do it to its best abilities. Now, in the mandates report, we identified initially the issue of frequently recurring business with limited changes to mandates. That's in quotes. Frequently recurring business with limited changes to mandates. And some of the statistics that we presented in the report, I think are quite suggestive. In 2024, the General assembly discussed about 370 agenda items and sub items. And of these, about 60%. Two hundred and fifteen are discussed every year. Thirty percent of the issues discussed have been considered since 1990, and 55% of recurring resolutions have been very similar to the ones adopted in previous years, meaning at least 80% of the text is identical, the same word for word. And these numbers, as I've said, are suggestive that in certain aspects, the steering process that I'VE talked to might simply be operating by autopilot now. Currently, as I've indicated, many agenda items come back year after year and there may be reason for that. I don't think it is self evident that this is inappropriate, particularly for entity specific or operational mandates. It may be that this makes perfect sense, but perhaps for larger system wide transformative agenda, the ones where change takes time, reviewing them less frequently but perhaps with more time and attention could be more useful. It could give space for meaningful progress over time and means that when negotiations do happen there is a real addition of value. As has been stressed by many of you in previous meetings in this working group, and I recall Ambassador Wray's parting intervention last time, these processes are not set in stone, they are amenable to change. The periodicity of considering agenda items, requesting reports and adopting resolutions can, if you so decide, all be adapted so that issues stay on the radar but recur at the most appropriate and productive intervals possible. In the report we also underlined that Member States could decide to undertake collective assessments of mandates, addressing related issues rather than addressing mandates one by one. Now these kinds of collective reviews, if I could call them those, they do happen, but they are the exception rather than the rule. One example of a collective review is the revitalization of the General assembly, which you are all familiar with. Another is the quadriennnual Comprehensive Policy Review, the QCPR process, which takes a strategic system wide view of the entirety of the UN development system. And as you are aware, the QCPR runs on a four year cycle, setting expectations for the entire UN development system, not just entity by entity or individual issue by issue. And this longer cycle creates a degree of space for real delivery, for learning, and for adjusting course in ways which are meaningful and consequential. There are other examples, but perhaps a QCPR approach is one which is instructive for other pillars as well. A further issue that we highlighted in the report is that most mandates do not include review mechanisms in them, and as you can see from this straightforward slide, around 86% of mandates lack any review mechanisms and this means that renewal can become the default setting even when circumstances may have changed. Of the 14% of UN mandates which do include review clauses, it's interesting to note that most are resolutions adopted by the Security Council and the Human Rights Council. They tend to relate to peace operations, fact finding missions or commissions of inquiry or sanctions measures. And I think you will hear more about these from my colleagues with us today in their presentations in the Development Pillar. One example where we have a Built in process and timeline is Resolution 71 of 2015, the one that adopted the Sustainable Development Goals. That resolution sets the SDG horizon for 2030, after which member States will review the progress made and determine next steps, as was indeed already the case after the deadline set for the Millennium Development Goals. So when it matters, clearly these things can be done. A more recent example, one which might be fresh in the memory of many of us, is General assembly resolution 79325 on the establishment and functioning of the independent Artificial Intelligence Panel. That resolution includes a review mechanism and timeline, and I quote, the continuation of the terms of reference of the Panel and the dialogue may be considered by the General assembly during the review of the Global Digital compact at its 82nd session. These are examples of resolutions which do have built in review mechanisms and we might learn from them. In these cases, the predictability of the review process and timeline may be helpful in ensuring that structured discussions among Member States are prioritized, and this in itself can lead to better outcomes. Now, having said all of that in respect of Member States, let me recall the role of the the system itself, the UN system, in this process, the second component that I mentioned at the outset, and recall the four potential solutions in this regard that were included in the Secretary General's report. And I would recall that these are the manner in which Member States could seek to systematize review practices across mandating bodies, make review clauses the norm, review and steer impact more collectively, meaning at the level of systems development, humanitarian peace and security, or the human rights pillar and streamline agenda to spend less time repeating discussions and more time steering strategically. Sorry, that part still belongs to the Member States component. I'm now moving on to the UN System component Excellency's colleagues. As it regards the UN System side, our role is to provide inputs to enable your review to operate optimally. As I've said, for reviews to work well, the system must provide a clear picture on how mandates translate into results. And here there are four things we need to pay attention to. We need a roadmap, a strategic plan on what goals any entity is pursuing long term. We need a results chain, a framework linking resources to activities, outputs and outcomes. A budget that shows value organized around results, not just cost categories in a more administrative manner. And we need strong communication reports that show our stakeholders how our work is making a difference. Today, as our report reflects, the challenge is that these plans, these results change. These budgets often look different in different parts of the UN system. Some entities have strategic plans, others do not. Some have Integrated results and resource frameworks, but many don't. And how we measure results differs across UN entities. Reporting and planning tools are inconsistent. The consequence of all of this is that it makes it hard for you, our Member States, to judge system wide performance or compare the impact of the work of different entities and from this to draw appropriate conclusions. So that is why we point to one simple but critical potential solution to these challenges in the Secretary General's report. And that is to strengthen and harmonize management for results mechanisms across the UN system, including how results are measured and how they are communicated. Breaking that down in concrete terms, that means that every major entity should have, firstly, a multi year strategic plan, secondly, an integrated results and resource framework based on a system wide standard, and thirdly, a budget that is organized around results, not just categories like staff and operations, administrative cost categories. And when results chains are completed are complete. Excuse me. And when budgets show how they contribute to outcomes, this enables a lot of things to happen. It enables you to have a system wide view of impact and how it is obtained, to see how different entities contribute to shared outcomes, this thorny issue of overlap and duplication, to see whether resources are aligned with results and to see where adjustments might be needed. Co Chairs, Dear participants, let me draw to a conclusion by clarifying that this type of mandate review is not simply about adding another layer of bureaucracy or process. It can be a tool for you to ensure that the UN system is and remains focused, coherent, effective and accountable. With better designed review cycles and stronger performance information, we can make our mandate delivery more strategic and above all, more impactful. Ultimately then, this is not about process, it's about people. It's about the 8 billion people the UN system connects to shared goals. It's about the 400 million people whose lives the UN helps improve on the ground, most of them in least developed countries. So I hope that we can work together through this working group to deliver better for the people who depend on us. And I thank you once again for this opportunity and look forward to your comments and questions.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
Thank you, Jess. Thank you, Guy. Thank you. I now give the floor to Mr. Lok Bahadur Tafa, Permanent Representative of Nepal to the United nations and President of the Economic and Social Council.

**NPL** | Lok Bahadur Thapa, PR; ECOSOC President
Thank you, distinguished Co Chairs, Excellencies, distinguished delegations. Thank you Co Chairs for inviting me today to join you. It is always a pleasure to talk about the ECOSOCS mandate, which is firmly rooted in the Charter entrusting the Council with responsibilities to promote international cooperations on economic, social, cultural, educational, health and related issues. ECOSOC serves as a platform for dialogue and policymaking, coordinating the UN development systems and engaging diverse stakeholders. ECOSOCS approaches the reviews stakeholders stage of the mandate life cycle through a range of legislative and oversight functions derived from the UN Charter GA resolutions and outcomes of the UN conferences and summits. Since the adoptions of the 2030 Agenda, ECOSOC has focused on coherence across the UN systems to support member states in meeting the global commitments. The High Level Political Forum, convened annually under its auspices, is the central platform to review SDG progress, share lessons and adopt our ministerial declarations. Beyond the hlpf, ECOSOC holds annual forums on financing for development, partnerships, youth and science and technology and innovations, adopting resolutions and recommendations across its program of work. It also responds to urgent global issues through special meetings that inform its broader sessions. Excellencies Viewing its own annual work program, the Council is also interested to oversee the work of five regional Commissions, eight functional Commissions, nine Export Committees, two Standing Committees, and a range of other bodies that report to it. These subsidiary bodies address a wide range of sustainable development issues, from statistics and crime prevention to science and technology, forest population, gender equality, indigenous peoples issues, as well as the needs of the LECs. They regularly report on the implementation of the 2030 Agenda. The Council periodically reviews their work on the basis of the recommendations contained in the reports of the subsidiary bodies as well as provides guidance based on current trends and needs on their future work. For instance, during its 2025 sessions, Ecosoc adopted 35 resolutions, signed 45 decisions, not including those related to elections. The Council discharges its charter mandated coordination functions by reviewing the work undertaken by the UN systems on the sustainable development issues across the economic, social, health and related fields, with a view to promoting coordinated actions and advancing long term solutions. Excellencies in recent years there have been efforts to streamline mandates. The ECOSOC Vice President, Costa rica at the 2025 session issued a Call to Action for the Humanitarian affairs segment in state of negotiating a resolution. Streamlining mandates also includes avoiding the introduction of new mandates unless accompanied by commensurate resources or by the clear adjustment or conclusion of existing overlapping obligations. There are also lessons learned from the recent experiences, particularly regarding the process of adoption of the Ministerial Declaration at both HLPF and Ecosoc. The 2024 Ministerial Declaration illustrated challenges due to procedural arrangements and the potential conflicts caused by the different voting rights at the ECOSOC and the hlpf. Member States may wish to address these situations during the forthcoming GA review of ECOSOC and the HLPF Excellencies The General assembly periodically reviews ECOSOC work and the provisions governing the HLPF in its resolutions 78285 the General assembly agreed to fully review at its 80th sessions the arrangement of the ecosocial and the HLPF. These reviews will be conducted jointly. Building on the lesson learned to ensure alignment with the broader 2030 Agenda follow up processes following GA Resolution 75 290A ECOSOC launched an internal review of its subsidiary bodies in 2022 facilitated by the Council Vice President. The process included assessment and recommendations by the Bureau of ecosoc Functional Commissions and expert bodies and informal consultations among Member States. This process resulted in ECOSOC Decisions 2022334 inviting implementations of the recommendations contained in the Vice President's summary. Furthermore, a number of ECOSUC functional commissions also conduct periodic reviews of their own working methods. The next review of ECOSAC subsidiary bodies initially mandated for 2026 is proposed to be postponed to 2027 to allow the process to be informed by the outcomes of the GA review process. In line with the fact of the future, ECOSHOCK launched an intergovernmental process in 2025 to explore option to revitalize the CSW Co facilitated by Ireland and St. Kitts and Navies. The process concluded with the adoption of ECOSOC resolutions on revitalizations of the CSW reaffirming CSW's role in follow up to the Fourth World Conference on Women and its outcome. The efforts have also been made to foster more effective complementarity between the General assembly and ecosac with the QCPR as an example of the two bodies identifying complementary rules namely of policymaking one for the GA every four years and the annual oversight of implementation and guidance by Member States to the UN Development Systems and Funding Compact ousted under ECOSOC in its Operational Activities for Development segment. Regular dialogue between the President of the GA and ECOSOC help ensure coherence including monthly meetings and annual joint meetings to identify shared and important issues of relevance to Member States Excellencies regarding the voluntary national reviews. The 2030 Agenda encourages member States to undertake regular and inclusive reviews of progress at national and sub national levels. These reviews are intended to be voluntary and country driven by presented at the HLPF under the auspices of ECOSA. Since 2015, 402 PNRs have been presented by 190 countries and the European Union with 148 countries submitting two or more reviews. The Resolution 75-190B suggested improvements the BNRs encouraging countries to highlight challenges, gaps and next steps to accelerate the implementation of the SDG and to conduct a BNR once the four year cycle of the hlpf. Nonetheless, certain challenges in the BNR process continue to be observed and addressing them could further strengthen the mechanisms, effectiveness and impact Excellencies. Integrated nature of the 2030 agenda requires close cooperations across intergovernmental bodies. The upcoming ECOSOC and HLPF review by the GA is an opportunity to reflect on the Council work and enhance the complementarity between ECOSOC and HLPF to ensure that their work is mutually reinforcing and accelerate the 2030 Agenda implementation. Taken together, the UN 80 initiative, the General assembly review of EcoShock and HLPF at its 80th session and the EcoShock review of its subsidiary machinery create opportunities for Member States to strengthen the functions, coherence and the impact of the ECOSAC and HLPF in advancing the 2030 Agenda and its SDGs. I thank you.

**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank the president of Ecosop. I now give the floor to Ms. Inga Anderson, executive Director of the United Nations Environment Program.

**UNEP** | Inger Andersen, Executive Director
Thank you very much and good evening from Nairobi, the only global headquarters of the United nations in the Global South. It's a pleasure and honor to speak here today this evening and we think that this mandate review is very critical and we've engaged very much throughout the process. UNEP, the United Nations Environment Program, was founded in 1972 in the Stockholm Conference and it really is the place whereby mandate we're supposed to promote international cooperation on the environment, keep the environment under review, which essentially is science, and serve as a focal point for environmental action within the UN system. And so being that pillar of the environment, we are enriched when other agencies take on the environmental issue but but address it through their mandate lens, be it human rights, be it children, be it health, et cetera. So what some would call decentralization of the environmental mandate to the country, we call it a great strengthening and we need to think about this like you do in the context of your ministries of environment, where of course, whether it's roads or transport or urban or health environment features just as much so for UNEP itself. Over the years since 1972 we have seen Member States through the UNEP processes give birth to a large number of conventions and two of these, you will know well, are not managed by UNEP or not hosted by UNEP. That will be climate change and desertification. But 17 others are indeed hosted by UNEP. And of course, therefore but of course answerable to the party driven governance process of the COP structure, which you will know very well in 2022 and again in 2024 at the United Nations Environment assembly, discussions were held. How can we ensure that the Environmental assembly, the United Nations Environmental assembly, creates a greater degree of synergy without interfering in the sovereign nature, if I can say so, of the multilateral environmental agreements and their cops? And so at this point now in the unea, the United Nations Environment assembly, each assembly has an MEA day, an MEA Week, mea, Multilateral Environment Agreement. Executive Directors and Presidents come and participate in high level discussions and panels, et cetera. There's a tent, because we are a tent city here. When we have unea, which the whole week will focus on MEA dialogue precisely to enable a greater synergistic conversation, so to say, then how do we work and how do we maximize the impact of the mandates that are given to us by Member States through the United Nations Environment Assembly? Well, Member States are about to, in December, at the upcoming UNEA Environment assembly, approve the next four year medium term strategy and the two year program of work. This will be decided of course at the conference, but at the Assembly. But essentially it will deal with the three elements that UNEPT works on. If I can summarize very much, climate stability, nature, biodiversity and land and pollution. And what we then do is we construct a one impact framework that ensures that the whole of UNEP approach, where the different programs and projects that are funded by a variety of sources which are delivering obviously the mandate are then reported on and leads to an organizationally agreed by Member States vision under the medium term strategy. So the impact framework also rides on a very, very strong measurement framework which shows how the work that we are doing here at UNEP is aligned with the International Agreed Goals, I.e. the goals in the Climate Convention, in the Chemical Conventions, in the Biodiversity Convention, et cetera. And so that allows for that. Even though these multilateral environmental agreements and their COPS take their decisions outside of the UNEA structure, nevertheless, a program that we see Member States approve is underpinning the very commitments that Member States have made in the COP structure. So therefore, we have an integrated theory of change that link the mandates to measurable outcomes and that ensures that we translate those mandates that have derived from UNEA or indeed from the cops into quantifiable impact and not just sort of activities. And the targets for these are set on the basis of implementation capacity and budgets, obviously. And so when it comes to resource mobilization, because we only receive around 3% of our monies from the regular budget and the rest we mobilize. And so we seek to do this based on an allocation of all resources. We have a strategic planning process that is linked with program budgeting. Our total program in a two year period is just under 3 billion. The vast amount of this obviously being programmatic. And we mobilize as if regular budget, but voluntary indicative scale of contribution just usually is around 19 million or so per year. That helps to pay for the spine of the organization. So when we seek to then report to Member States, we seek to show progress against agreed indicators which are publicly available. I should say to ensure that UNEP can be transparent and very much seen that here's what the science says, here's what Member States decided, here's what UNEP is doing. That model demonstrates very much how the results based evidence informs, performs the mandate review and connects the mandates to the results, to the learning, etc. We do regular program performance reports to Member States. We do that on a quarterly basis. And so there's also a learning dimension obviously in that. So through all of this with a mandate, we take very much, if I can say so, a life cycle approach to the mandate that is given to us, that are given to us by Member States, the strategic guidance, the development, support and implementation. Now, since we are supposed to keep the environment under the review and therefore do the science, if I can say so, and you will recall that IPCC was created by a UNEP Governing Council resolution, you will recall that ipbs, a Biodiversity and Land Science Policy Panel, was created by a UNEP Governing Council resolution. And just recently the Intergovernmental Science Policy Panel on Chemicals, Waste and Pollution Created by UNEA resolution. That science plus what UNEP Secretariat does itself allows us to do some degree of horizon watching to therefore the Executive Director about nine months or so before the United Nations Environment assembly issue a report to Member States saying these are the areas that are sort of horizon issues where you may wish to. Well, recognizing that resolutions are a totally sovereign issue pertaining to Member States and prerogative. Nevertheless, as we are supposed to review the science, we say here are areas that are coming up that are urgent or important or under attended to. Member States may then decide or not to take inspiration from the Executive Director's report to submit resolutions in that space. Once resolutions begin to be submitted, we submit a what we call a technical note on each resolution. This is a technical note that deals with five issues. How is that particular resolution aligned with the existing mandate and a strategy that you are about to approve at the same assembly, what is the relationship to existing mandates either within UNEP or in other fora that is is it duplicative? In which case we would suggest it's duplicative and therefore you may wish to rethink Third, a legal assessment on the substance and procedural matters. Fourth, the impact of the proposed draft. How would we measure whether we have done the thing that is being proposed? And fifth, how much is it going to cost? And so this we make available this Technical Note to Member States well ahead of the United Nations Environment assembly to allow Member States to use this as they would see fit for their consideration and deliberations. We also allow us assign a technical, technically, scientifically and technical qualified focal point to be available to Member States in each of the resolution discussion meetings and of course we provide oral briefing briefings to Member States on the technical notes to ensure that some of the finer technical and scientific issues are highlighted. Now, once a resolution has been adopted, we then support Member States through the implementation tracking portal and reporting portal. We have established a resolutions portal that is publicly accessible and that monitors and reports on how we're doing on resolution A, B or C. It tracks and outlines the implementation plans for each resolution. It provides milestones, the results, the availability of resources, the learning, the opportunity and the challenges, the risks and the lessons learned, the partners and so on and so forth. And because we see of course Member States circulate in and out in terms of our permanent representatives, so we do familiarization sessions with permanent missions with this tracker so that they themselves, when new PRs come in, can follow this through. And when we have completed a task, and an obvious one would be that we were asked two years ago to help establish the Science Policy Panel on Chemicals, Waste and Pollution and in July of this year this got gaveled and so we therefore have this panel. So now that resolution can be retired, but when a resolution has been completed, then the monitoring and reporting portal for resolutions will provide a detailed report on the completion of that particular resolution. Now you asked me my final point to reflect on the SG's report on the proposed solutions to improve the mandate review and we support all of the above and then some we think that there's a lot that could be done to follow quicker through, to monitor better, maybe particularly in two areas, to systematize and to reinforce the efforts that are underway to strengthen the mandate review mechanisms and to ensure that these efforts are in place across the various mandating bodies and also to include inspiring clauses as we just heard from USG Guy Ryder in the resolutions having more explicit mention of what is the results, when is it achieved and what is the lifespan, so that these resolutions are not carried into the forever, or at least we could have objective conditionalities under which something is continued or not, so sort of on, off switch. Additionally, for the environmental pillar, of course, Member States may want to consider how they could delineate better what belongs in the assembly that the General assembly has established, the United Nations Environment assembly, which has the technical skills, the Scientific Secretariat and the environmental mandate related to the United nations and what they may choose to put at unga. So for us, we very, very much look forward to continuing working on and support the outcome of this working group and to contribute to enhancing the mandate life cycle at this very crucial time for the environment. Thank you very much. Cheers.

The other part of those proposals include institutionalizing regular evidence based reviews, especially for mandates exceeding a certain duration, reflecting on the criteria for those reviews of course, including prioritization, differentiation of those continuing long term mandates versus the short term ones and others introducing standardized clauses for review with opportunity for decision on continuation or termination of a mandate consider linking any new mandates to the conclusion of existing ones in the review, consolidate overlapping mandates and consider short term or single report mandates for emerging issues strengthening the cross pillar coordination to ensure mandates reflect integrated priorities across peace, development and human rights and ensure transparent and solidly calculated linkage to resources. That is again the Geneva coming to New York decisions and the Fifth Committee in particular the linking up on the review and content of IT and of course efficiencies measures reducing duplication and reporting to the Human Rights Council and General assembly considering for instance biannual reporting, virtual presentations and consolidate overlapping mandates in various functions, in particular Protection investigators.
