**IAHWG** | Brian Wallace, Co-Chair
I thank Ms. Brands Karris and I'll give the floor to Ms. Claudia Bans, Director, Security Council Affairs Division.

**UN Secretariat** | Claudia Bans, Director, Security Council Affairs Division
Distinguished Co Chairs, Excellencies, ladies and gentlemen, thank you for this opportunity to share some insights on how the Security Council conducts mandate review. I must admit I first hesitated when the co Chairs invited me to be part of this panel. As the Executive Director of UNEP has already pointed out, the drafting of mandate is a sovereign issue and the prerogative of its members. The Secretariat is usually not there when Council members negotiate mandates. However, we are there when they discuss and take decisions on mandates. And I'm therefore happy to elaborate a bit about one recent trends in the review and termination of mandates by the Security Council and two the procedures, working methods and tools the Council uses to make these decisions and 3 how the Secretariat, and particularly the Security Council Affairs Divisions or my division, supports the Council in this process. While the Council has applied an array of approaches to mandate creation, review and termination over its 80 years of decision making. In my remarks I will focus on the Council's contemporary practice. We can categorize the Council's review of mandates into three broad approaches. First, for most of its practice after the Cold War, the Council has taken a gradual approach to transitions, drawdowns and determination of mandates, guided by recommendations and findings of Secretariat reviews and assessments, and conducted with varying degrees of coordination and consultations with host states and other relevant stakeholders. The most prominent examples falling under this category are the reconfiguration, drawdown and eventual termination of peace operations in Liberia, Sierra Leone, Coteva, Timor Leste, Nepal and Guinea Bissau among a number of other countries, probably the success stories when there was a. Staged approach to their drawdown. Second, the Council has at occasion used time bound mandates with explicitly set deadlines for reviews, drawdowns and eventual mandate termination, some of which include so called sunset clauses. This is the case of Somalia and the deployment of the United Special Political Mission, ANTMIS and the African Union led AWSOM Peace Enforcement Mission. The mandates of these missions include early defined phases of deployment from the very outset, including expected termination dates in the two cases for Antmis it's 2026 and for OSOM it's 2029. Another example was the creation of the United nations mandate in Colombia in 2016. It started out with a narrow mandate to support the implementation of the ceasefire and cessation of hostage hostilities, which was later scaled up and transitioned into a mission with a broader mandate to support the implementation of the peace agreement. The third trend in Council mandate review has been less gradual in approach where major changes or terminations of mandates were made further to requests from host governments or based on case specific considerations of Council members themselves. In 2023, the Council decided to terminate the mandates of MINUSMA, the Peace Operation in Mali or peacekeeping operation in Mali and unitams in the political mission in Sudan. Further to request from the host governments Mali and Sudan and in the context of challenging security conditions on the ground. In 2024, the council did not renew the Mali sanctions regime and thereby effectively terminated the relevant Committee and its Panel of experts. Similarly, while extending the sanctions regime and the 1718 committee, that's the sanctions committee on DPRK. In the same year the Council terminated the mandate of Panel of Experts concerning the Democratic People's Republic of the Korea. Most recently this year in August, the Council decided to terminate after 40 years of deployment the mandate of UNIFIL as of 31st December 2026. The Council also indicated possible reconfigurations of UNMAHA, that's the UN Mission in Hudaydah in Yemen and Minerso in Western Sahara in the coming year. Further to upcoming reviews to be conducted by the Secretariat. Of specific note are also the sanctions regime, in addition to the ones I have already mentioned, which have been adapted regularly, taking into account improvements in the situation on the ground. The Secretariat has contributed to these reviews by conducting assessment visits with benchmarks identified benchmarks and this assessment coincided with Council decisions to adjust the measures, particularly in lifting the arms embargo vis a vis the governments in Central African Republic and Somalia. Now, turning to the Council's working methods, I would like to highlight three practices that are closely tied to how the Council review mandates. The first is the preparation of the Council's monthly Provisional Program of Work, which is the basic planning tool the Council uses to organize its work. While the program I think it's the previous one. While the program is agreed upon by Council members on the first working day of the month, this is being prepared with the relevant presidencies at least four months ahead of the actual Presidency. And this program of work is basically based on it lists all the mandated meetings and mandate renewals and expirations on to which Council members usually add then their elective meetings and signature events. But the framework of the monthly program is actually based on the mandate and report reporting cycle of the Security Council. Although there was a significant increase in so called unscheduled or emergency meetings in the past three years, the mandated activities stemming from previous decisions and existing practice continue to form the overall framework for the monthly schedule in the Security Council as part of its support to Council presidencies and members in preparation of this meeting. Monthly Program SCAD Our Division maintains and circulates to Member two important planning documents on a monthly basis. One is the monthly Forecast. It's a document that outlines all the upcoming mandate renewals, reports and expirations and the Security Council Reporting and Mandate Cycle that provide the overview on the upcoming reporting and mandate expiration in accordance with previous decisions and existing practice. One of the two documents is 100 pages long, but it's structured that you can go month by month, which is useful and for us an indispensable tool to organize the program of work. Both of these documents are publicly available on the website. Second, the Council also has, although not a direct correlation to the number of active meeting mandates, but the Council's annual review of the items on its agenda, which is also known as the Summary Statement, is another important way of how Members strive to streamline the work of the Security Council. So every first week of January each year, the Secretariat publishes a comprehensive version of the Summary Statement in which the items are divided in two categories. One category is those items on the agenda of the Security Council which have been considered by the Security Council in the preceding three years, and the second one is a category that the items have not been considered by the Security Council. This is then reviewed to subject by all by Member States. And those agenda items which have not been considered are actually due to deletion. They will be deleted from the agenda of the Council unless a Member State sends a letter and requests that the agenda item is maintained on the agenda of the Security Council. This process of review was introduced in 1996. Around 1996. In 1993, there were 207 items on the agenda of the Security Council, and now it's 65. So in the past 10 years it was around 60 agenda items on the Council's agenda. The third important aspect of mandate review is the role of penholders in Council decision making and mandate review. Penholdership is codified in the Presidential Note, and it remains an informal arrangement whereby one or more Council members initiate and chair the informal drafting process. The aim of the penholdership, as described in note 507, was to foster timely initiatives to ensure Council action while preserving the continuity of and with a view to enhancing efficiency in the work of the Council. These penholders, they have a role as conveners of the drafting process and they have significant influence in setting out the methodology and content of mandate reviews in the Council. Council members have also encouraged penholders to prioritize the fortune of consensus census, ensure early exchange of information, informal consultations with the broader United nations membership, particularly concerned states to regularly consult the Secretariat and troop and police contributing countries. Most of the penholders are in the hands of the Permanent members or the P3, but all council members can actually work as penholders or carry out the function of penholders. The process remains an informal one, although this has been codified in note 507. And a final point I wanted to mention is about mandate duration in Security Council mandates. While most mandates, I think the large majority, particularly those pertaining to peace operations and sanctions regimes, are set for a period of one year. Others are renewed and reviewed. At longer for example UNOCCA, UNAWAS, these are regional offices and the 1267 monitoring team. They will be renewed every three years and CTET every four years. You also have a number of mandates that have a shorter intervals undof the peacekeeping operation every six months and a number of Other mandates remain, including several peacekeeping operations open ended, as well as some of the sanctions measures. Open ended peace operations are anmo, gib, anso, anmic, UNRCCA and Anschol sanctions measures, Al Shabaab, Al Qaeda and Yemen and as well as the committees of Guinea Bissau and Iraq, they remain open ended. I leave it at that. Co Chairs, thank you again for inviting us.

Again, that decision has to be made by Member States. So my question to the Secretariat is whether the Secretariat will be able to identify a list of mandates that in its view, in the Secretariat's view, faces a minimal level of activity and therefore suggests that there is considerable implementation deficit and therefore requires the review of members to take a decision either to provide additional resources or to restructure the way the mandates are designed or to retire the mandate.

But probably at this time with UN80 initiative, we need a more consolidated and more overview, a more ego approach to this mandate review. Thank you very much. Thank you Malawi. I give the floor to Costa Rica and then South Africa.

**ZAF**, Representative
Thank you co Chairs and thank you. To the panelists for the information provided this morning. South Africa has a few questions concerning the mandate review process. The first question relates to the report which states that more than 85% of active mandates lack instructions on review or termination? South Africa has in our previous deliberations raised the importance of the inclusion of sunset clauses in this regard. Should the inclusion of expiry clauses and review advisories not become standard in the stage of mandate design? Secondly, if a results based management mechanism is a measure is to measure accountability as well as the impact and collective achievement of our mandates, our core preoccupation of this discussion, would it not be crucial to have an effectively run uniform system wide performance management mechanism firmly in place to also evaluate the impact of activities? Thirdly, while South Africa agrees with the proposed solutions for system wide mandate reviews, should the use of such mechanisms not also include mention of the Joint Inspection Unit and the Committee for Program and Coordination? Finally, in setting up a harmonized system wide performance management mechanism, what would be the annual budgetary and human resource implications of ensuring the availability of consistent and predictable resources for the operationalization of Such a mechanism. **QAT**, Representative
Thank you very much. I'd like to thank the Co Chairs for their leadership of this transparent, inclusive process and for all the briefers for their very informative information that they provided us with. So we engage in this mandate implementation review and exercising that enhancing coherence, reducing deployment, application and streamlining. Our first question would be regarding the practical tools available to improve the efficiency of our work across the Main Committees. Under the provision of paragraph 28A of resolution 79 327, the General assembly plenary first takes all required votes individually on draft resolutions or parts where a vote occurred in the Main Committee and only thereafter adopts and one gavel all other resolutions and decisions that were approved in the Committee without a vote? Could the mandate implementation review explore whether this approach, adopting all non contested draft resolutions in a single action during the Plenary could be expanded or applied more systematically across the Main Committees? In particular, could it help reduce repetitive procedural steps, minimize scheduling burdens during the plenary adoption period and support a more streamlined, predictable and efficient resolutions? Second question is on the relevance and coherence of existing mandates across the Main Committees. How will the mandate implementation review identify mandates that remain relevant, those that require updating or consolidation, and those that may no longer contribute effectively to the work of the organization to guide Member States in assessing application of efforts? Mandates that predate major frameworks such as the 2030 Agenda, mandates whose output puts no longer translate into impactful results and proposals that add to procedural burden without advancing shared priorities.
